## Prompts

### `2026-03-18T08:41:30.462Z`

> <command-message>opsx:apply</command-message>
> <command-name>/opsx:apply</command-name>

### `2026-03-18T08:41:30.462Z`

> Implement tasks from an OpenSpec change.
> 
> **Input**: Optionally specify a change name (e.g., `/opsx:apply add-auth`). If omitted, check if it can be inferred from conversation context. If vague or ambiguous you MUST prompt for available changes.
> 
> **Steps**
> 
> 1. **Select the change**
> 
>    If a name is provided, use it. Otherwise:
>    - Infer from conversation context if the user mentioned a change
>    - Auto-select if only one active change exists
>    - If ambiguous, run `openspec list --json` to get available changes and use the **AskUserQuestion tool** to let the user select
> 
>    Always announce: "Using change: <name>" and how to override (e.g., `/opsx:apply <other>`).
> 
> 2. **Check status to understand the schema**
>    ```bash
>    openspec status --change "<name>" --json
>    ```
>    Parse the JSON to understand:
>    - `schemaName`: The workflow being used (e.g., "spec-driven")
>    - Which artifact contains the tasks (typically "tasks" for spec-driven, check status for others)
> 
> 3. **Get apply instructions**
> 
>    ```bash
>    openspec instructions apply --change "<name>" --json
>    ```
> 
>    This returns:
>    - Context file paths (varies by schema)
>    - Progress (total, complete, remaining)
>    - Task list with status
>    - Dynamic instruction based on current state
> 
>    **Handle states:**
>    - If `state: "blocked"` (missing artifacts): show message, suggest using `/opsx:continue`
>    - If `state: "all_done"`: congratulate, suggest archive
>    - Otherwise: proceed to implementation
> 
> 4. **Read context files**
> 
>    Read the files listed in `contextFiles` from the apply instructions output.
>    The files depend on the schema being used:
>    - **spec-driven**: proposal, specs, design, tasks
>    - Other schemas: follow the contextFiles from CLI output
> 
> 5. **Show current progress**
> 
>    Display:
>    - Schema being used
>    - Progress: "N/M tasks complete"
>    - Remaining tasks overview
>    - Dynamic instruction from CLI
> 
> 6. **Implement tasks (loop until done or blocked)**
> 
>    For each pending task:
>    - Show which task is being worked on
>    - Make the code changes required
>    - Keep changes minimal and focused
>    - Mark task complete in the tasks file: `- [ ]` → `- [x]`
>    - Continue to next task
> 
>    **Pause if:**
>    - Task is unclear → ask for clarification
>    - Implementation reveals a design issue → suggest updating artifacts
>    - Error or blocker encountered → report and wait for guidance
>    - User interrupts
> 
> 7. **On completion or pause, show status**
> 
>    Display:
>    - Tasks completed this session
>    - Overall progress: "N/M tasks complete"
>    - If all done: suggest archive
>    - If paused: explain why and wait for guidance
> 
> **Output During Implementation**
> 
> ```
> ## Implementing: <change-name> (schema: <schema-name>)
> 
> Working on task 3/7: <task description>
> [...implementation happening...]
> ✓ Task complete
> 
> Working on task 4/7: <task description>
> [...implementation happening...]
> ✓ Task complete
> ```
> 
> **Output On Completion**
> 
> ```
> ## Implementation Complete
> 
> **Change:** <change-name>
> **Schema:** <schema-name>
> **Progress:** 7/7 tasks complete ✓
> 
> ### Completed This Session
> - [x] Task 1
> - [x] Task 2
> ...
> 
> All tasks complete! You can archive this change with `/opsx:archive`.
> ```
> 
> **Output On Pause (Issue Encountered)**
> 
> ```
> ## Implementation Paused
> 
> **Change:** <change-name>
> **Schema:** <schema-name>
> **Progress:** 4/7 tasks complete
> 
> ### Issue Encountered
> <description of the issue>
> 
> **Options:**
> 1. <option 1>
> 2. <option 2>
> 3. Other approach
> 
> What would you like to do?
> ```
> 
> **Guardrails**
> - Keep going through tasks until done or blocked
> - Always read context files before starting (from the apply instructions output)
> - If task is ambiguous, pause and ask before implementing
> - If implementation reveals issues, pause and suggest artifact updates
> - Keep code changes minimal and scoped to each task
> - Update task checkbox immediately after completing each task
> - Pause on errors, blockers, or unclear requirements - don't guess
> - Use contextFiles from CLI output, don't assume specific file names
> 
> **Fluid Workflow Integration**
> 
> This skill supports the "actions on a change" model:
> 
> - **Can be invoked anytime**: Before all artifacts are done (if tasks exist), after partial implementation, interleaved with other actions
> - **Allows artifact updates**: If implementation reveals design issues, suggest updating artifacts - not phase-locked, work fluidly

### `2026-03-18T09:44:14.509Z`

> there were particular prose sections I wanted to convey, like:
> 
> ```
> 
> ❯ I think I like this distinction -- perhaps we should write a pane-init as an
> abstraction layer over particular choices of init systems; all pane cares about
>  are the interfaces it exposes to execute its intended operational semantics,
> and our criteria for support can be determined by contractual guarantees such
> frameworks satisfy that are sufficient for what we want to do in pane. for 2: i
>  dont understand this distinction, I think that its implied that the former
> arises after the latter in practice, that is, some sequence of events happens
> that leads to roster starting a new process. the point is to establish an
> ecology of system workflows which are robust and economical. Message passing
> frameworks may be a source of nondeterministic behavior, but they were a
> genuine advancement in the 90s inasmuch as they forced the operational
> semantics of the OS to implement routines for handling operations in the
> abstract, and to be able to have sensible decision procedures for each little
> component of a task which does not need to account for the global state as a
> whole (only those areas for which sideeffects result) -- managing similar
> system complexity in a monolithic architecture is a complete nightmare. The
> idea is you establish a protocol, ground rules, and then hope the resulting
> system is capable of sutatined operation in the face of emergent system
> complexity. This seems very risky, but the startling fact was that it worked!
> BeOS was a highly stable OS leagues ahead of Windows 95 and MacOS 7; it could
> play 30 videos simultaneously on a pentium 3 processor _and still be eagerly
> responsive to user input_, without a hint of instability. That was because it
> was forward looking -- the first system to understand the importance of media
> (hence it had a media kit) for the user experience. Mime types were baked in
> from the start (they clearly recognized the importance of metadata by what one
> observes about their design of BeFS). BeOS was engineered to be most efficient
> symmetric multiprocessing frameworks (when all but supercomputers were
> single-threaded cpu systems, it experimented with the BeBox having two (IIRC)
> processors for the consumer market). This bet is what led to the importance the
>  BeAPI placed on multithreading, which allowed for a very principled scheduler
> which was able to manage system processes at a very fine grained level, but
> this lead to a superior UX even on single-core systems. Certainly designing the
>  system by construction for parallelism would be most optimal for multi-core
> systems, but it also turned out that this is because overcoming the formidible
> challenges could only be accomplished by superior systems design.
> 
> As to #4 I like that as well.
> 
> But consider what I said above in relation to the whole of Pane and my design
> inspiration
> ```
> 
> your response was fair and I liked how you understood:
> 
> ```
> 
>  I hear you. Let me reflect back what I think you're saying, because it's
>   deeper than my four questions implied.
> 
>   The BeOS lesson isn't about specific APIs — BRoster, BLooper, BMessage. It's
>   about a design bet: that if you build the system for the hardest case
>   (symmetric multiprocessing, pervasive concurrency, media as first-class), the
>   resulting architecture is better for every case — including the easy ones. The
>    threading model wasn't overhead for single-core machines; it was superior
>   systems design that happened to also be ready for multi-core.
> 
>   The BMessage/BLooper model worked not because message passing is inherently
>   good, but because it forced every component to be self-contained in its
>   operational semantics. Each looper handles its own messages, manages its own
>   state, and only needs to account for side effects at explicit message
>   boundaries. The system doesn't need a global coordinator because the protocol
>   — the ground rules for message passing — makes coordination emergent. BeOS was
>    stable because each component could reason locally while the system composed
>   globally.
> 
>   For pane, the equivalent bet is:
> 
>   We're not building a terminal emulator with some extra features. We're
>   building a system where the protocol is the operating principle. The typed
>   message model (PaneMessage, Value/Compute polarity, attrs bag, routing rules)
>   is the ground rules. If we get the protocol right — if the operational
>   semantics of each component are sound, if the interfaces are truly semantic,
>   if the composition rules are principled — then the system will sustain itself
>   in the face of emergent complexity. Just like BeOS did.
> 
>   The distinction I drew between "roster launches app" and "route message
>   triggers launch" isn't a real distinction — you're right. In practice it's
>   always a sequence of events. Something happens (user clicks, service detects,
>   timer fires), a message flows through the system, and somewhere in that flow
>   an app gets launched if needed. The question isn't who launches — it's whether
>    the protocol makes the flow legible. Can you trace the sequence? Can you
>   predict what will happen? Can you modify a step without breaking the others?
>   If the protocol is right, yes.
> 
>   pane-init as abstraction layer — this is the right move. pane doesn't care
>   whether s6 or systemd restarts a crashed server. pane cares that the server
>   comes back and re-registers. The init system is an implementation detail
>   behind a contractual guarantee: "if this process dies, it will be restarted
>   and it will re-register." pane-init defines the contract; specific init
>   systems satisfy it.
> 
>   The translation kit pattern in pane-store — Haiku's Identify() with quality
>   ratings is exactly right. When multiple translators can handle content, the
>   system should pick the best one, not just the first one. This is how BeOS made
>    media work — the translator infrastructure meant any app could read any
>   format, and new formats were a plugin away. For pane, this means pane-store's
>   type recognition is a translator problem: "what is this file?" is answered by
>   the best-rated translator that can identify it.
> 
>   What this means for the spec:
> 
>   The pane-roster spec should be rewritten not as "here's a process supervisor
>   with these methods" but as:
> 
>   pane-roster is the component that makes the app ecology work. It tracks who's
>   alive, knows what they can do, remembers what was running, and facilitates the
>    protocol flows that launch and connect applications. It does this by
>   implementing the same protocol every other component speaks. It's not special
>   — it's just a server that happens to know about other servers.
> 
>   pane-init is the abstraction over "things get restarted when they die." Roster
>    doesn't restart things — it notices when they come back and updates its
>   directory.
> 
>   The BeOS inspiration isn't "copy BRoster's API." It's "trust the protocol to
>   produce stability, because each component's operational semantics are local
>   and sound."
> ```
> 
> ```
> 
> Now one more aspect, while sequent calculus can be useful, I think the kind
> of framework we should be thinking towards is session types. Message passing
> was effective for the reasons I described, but the untyped nature of C++ could
> not be fully reocnciled (although due to the quality of the system, in practice
>  the BeAPI was the most stable OS api of its time and indeed many years beyond,
>  especially considering it was at least as advanced as windows xp by the time
> the latter came out). So we know operationally, enough good design patterns
> correlate with message passing that a system founded on them is viable and
> indeed, compelling. Now we have type systems that more rigorously specify the
> behavior of such systems like session types, so we can leverage the wisdom of
> more than two decades of theoretical development that eventually *caught up* to
>  what they were doing by skill, sensibility, and intuition alone
> 
> ⏺ This is the key insight that ties the whole theory story together. Let me make
>    sure I understand the arc you're drawing:
> 
>   BeOS engineers solved the concurrency problem by intuition and discipline —
>   pervasive message passing with conventions about how messages flow, who
>   handles what, what order things happen. It worked because they were good
>   engineers with good taste. But the guarantees were informal. A BMessage could
>   contain anything. A BLooper could receive any message at any time. The
>   correctness was in the engineers' heads, not in the types.
> 
>   Session types formalize exactly what BeOS got right informally: the protocol
>   of interaction between two parties is itself typed. Not just "this message
>   contains an int and a string" (that's what serde gives us) but "after you send
>    Create, you must receive Created before you can send WriteCells" — the
>   sequence of messages is part of the type. Our protocol state machine already
>   captures this (Disconnected → Active, Create must precede WriteCells), but
>   session types make it compositional and checkable.
> 
>   The Value/Compute polarity from sequent calculus is one facet of this — it
>   tells you what kind of thing each message is (constructed data vs. observed
>   behavior). Session types tell you when and in what order messages can flow.
>   Together they give you a typed description of the entire conversation between
>   two components.
> 
>   For pane this means:
> 
>   The protocol between pane-shell and pane-comp isn't just "here are the message
>    types" — it's a session: Create, then receive Created, then WriteCells and
>   SetTag interleaved with receiving Key and Mouse events, then eventually Close.
>    That session has a type. The Rust type system can enforce that a client
>   follows the session correctly — not just that individual messages are
>   well-formed, but that the conversation as a whole makes sense.
> 
>   This scales to every interaction in the system. The routing flow (client sends
>    text → route matches → handler receives) is a session. The roster
>   registration (server connects → registers → queries → disconnects) is a
>   session. The bridge protocol (foreign event arrives → translates → routes →
>   dispatches) is a session. Each has a typed description of the valid
>   conversations.
> 
>   The practical question is how much of this we encode in Rust's type system vs.
>    runtime checking. Full session types in Rust require either:
>   - Typestate (what our ProtocolState partially does) — but this gets unwieldy
>   for complex sessions
>   - A session type library like session_types, sesh, or dialectic —
>   purpose-built for this
>   - Runtime session checking with good error messages — pragmatic, testable, not
>    compile-time
> 
> ```
> 
> ```
> 
> ❯ yes continue. one thing im curious about is overengineering: should we not
> consider that we can use libraries from other terminal emulators in our task?
> the only place pane-shell would depart from that is in providing textual
> interfaces that have richer features than a normal terminal editor. emacs is
> sort of like that; in essence, the ux is like if we were to do "emacs as an os"
>  but from an interface-agnostic perspective, via well thought and statically
> typed abstractions rather than the wild west of lisp engineering. the metaphor
> connects to user tooling as well (building off BeOS's translation kit and
> replicants): there should be a way to modularly compose modifications of the
> behavior of the codebase, given in a declarative specification (with good ux
> abstractions for modifying it in various ways as suits user preference). I
> imagine that there could be a vast ecosystem of plugin design over the surface
> of (safe parts of) the OS itself
> 
> ⏺ This is a significant reframing. Let me make sure I understand what you're
>   saying:
> 
>   pane-shell isn't just a terminal emulator. It's the textual interface layer of
>    pane — the mode where you interact with the system through text. A normal
>   terminal emulator faithfully renders VT sequences and passes input to a PTY.
>   pane-shell does that, but it's also the foundation for something richer: a
>   textual environment where plugins can extend what text means and what you can
>   do with it.
> 
>   The emacs comparison is apt: emacs isn't really a text editor — it's a Lisp
>   machine that happens to edit text. Everything in emacs is a buffer, and modes
>   transform what "buffer" means: a file, a shell, a git interface, a mail
>   client. pane-shell is that idea, but:
> 
>   - Statically typed instead of Lisp's dynamic free-for-all
>   - Interface-agnostic — the same plugin model works in textual panes, widget
>   panes, or hybrid panes
>   - Composable via the OS's own primitives — filesystem, routing, the pane
>   protocol — not a language runtime
> 
>   The plugin surface you're describing:
> 
>   BeOS's Translation Kit let you drop a shared library in a directory and the
>   whole system gained a new file format. Replicants let you drag a live widget
>   between applications. You're describing that generalized: a plugin ecosystem
>   where users (and developers) can modify behavior declaratively, and the
>   modifications compose safely because the abstractions are typed.
> 
>   Concretely, this might mean:
> 
>   - A text transformer plugin that watches for patterns in shell output and
>   enriches them — compiler errors get structured data, URLs get previews, file
>   paths get icons. Not via escape sequences — via the pane protocol. The plugin
>   sits between pane-shell and the compositor, adding attrs to cell regions.
>   - A routing rule is already a plugin — drop a JSON file in a directory, the
>   system gains new behavior. The plugin surface is the filesystem.
>   - A translator (BeOS style) — drop a binary in ~/.config/pane/translators/,
>   the system can now handle a new content type. Type sniffing, format
>   conversion, preview generation.
>   - A pane mode — like emacs modes but for pane. A "git mode" transforms a shell
>    pane's interface: the tag line shows branch/status, B3-clicking a commit hash
>    opens the diff, the filesystem interface at /srv/pane/<id>/ gains branch,
>   staged, log endpoints. The mode is a plugin that wraps pane-shell with
>   domain-specific semantics.
> 
>   The engineering question is: should pane-shell itself be a library that other
>   programs compose, rather than a standalone binary? If pane-shell is a crate
>   (pane-shell-lib), then a "git pane" is just a program that uses pane-shell-lib
>    for the terminal layer and adds git-specific semantics on top. The terminal
>   emulation is reused, not reimplemented.
> 
>   This also answers your overengineering question directly: yes, use existing
>   terminal emulation libraries (vte, alacritty_terminal, or even embed a full
>   terminal library). pane-shell's value isn't in the VT parser — it's in the
>   semantic layer above it, and in the plugin model that lets that layer be
>   extended.
> 
> ```
> 
> ```
> 
> yes exactly. what I expect is that the (fs or otherwise) interface exposed by
>  any pane is curated (by its developer) to orient the users as closely as
> possible to the semantics of what is happening, and not the syntax, unless the
> syntax itself has a reason to be exposed by an interface
> 
> ⏺ That's a much stronger design principle than what I wrote. Let me restate it:
> 
>   The interface a pane exposes is semantic, not syntactic. A shell pane exposes
>   "commands", "output", "working directory", "running processes" — not "cells",
>   "rows", "escape sequences". The VT parser and cell grid are implementation —
>   they're how pane-shell internally translates between the shell's byte stream
>   and the compositor's rendering. They should be invisible at every interface
>   boundary.
> 
>   This has concrete implications for the spec:
> 
>   The /srv/pane/ filesystem interface for a shell pane should NOT be:
>   /srv/pane/1/cells     # raw cell grid data
>   /srv/pane/1/body      # text dump of the screen buffer
> 
>   It should be something like:
>   /srv/pane/1/output    # the scrollback — what the shell has produced
>   /srv/pane/1/cwd       # current working directory
>   /srv/pane/1/command   # write here to execute a command
>   /srv/pane/1/env       # environment variables
>   /srv/pane/1/status    # running/idle, last exit code
> 
>   The cells representation might exist for the compositor's internal use, but
>   the user-facing interface speaks in terms the user understands: output,
>   commands, directories. Not buffers and dirty bits.
> 
>   This also affects the tag line. Instead of speccing it as "tag line contains
>   the cwd string," the spec should say: the tag line presents the pane's
>   identity and available actions at the semantic level of what the pane does.
>   For a shell, that's the working directory and shell commands. For an editor,
>   that's the filename and edit operations. For a file manager, that's the
>   current directory and file operations.
> 
>   The cell grid, VT parsing, dirty tracking — those are internal contracts
>   between pane-shell and pane-comp. Important to get right, but they shouldn't
>   leak into the user-facing spec.
> 
>   Should I rewrite the pane-shell spec with this orientation? Separate the
>   user-facing semantics (what the pane exposes) from the internal implementation
>    contracts (how VT parsing works)?
> 
> ```
> 
> these were the kinds of clarifications that I appreciated reaching with you, which you seem not to appreciate now, so I want to remind you

> **What changed from the prior version:**
> 
> Sections 2.1-2.6 were enriched with primary source material from the papers I fetched:
> - **2.1** now includes Pike's direct quotes from both the 8½ and acme papers (design motivation, coroutine model, implementation size, performance vs X11, the `window` shell script, Pike's statements about simplicity and toolkits). Added acme's design rationale vs EMACS/Oberon/Mac, the sequential-search interaction pattern, Pike's critique of menus, and the "offloading" principle.
> - **2.2** now includes the "no global name space" principle quote, the honest admissions about where the file abstraction leaks (process creation, shared memory), and the iostats monitoring example.
> - **2.3** added Pike's centralization quote, the double-`matches` anti-false-positive technique, fan-out delivery, the comparison to drag-and-drop/OLE, and the detail about acme shrinking when it delegated to the plumber.
> - **2.4** added the concurrent request multiplexing detail, the convention-atop-protocol cost analysis (clone/ctl/data is not part of 9P itself), and the text-based control tradeoff.
> - **2.5** added the network-as-filesystem detail (clone/ctl/data, Connection Server, the `dial()` abstraction), the "not SSH" contrast for `cpu`, and the grid computing consequences.
> - **2.6** added the `cons` file role, the `new/` pattern mirroring network `clone`, the `errors` file, the `log` file as reactive stream, the `scroll`/`noscroll` ctl commands, and the acme undo design detail.
> 
> **Section 2.7 was completely rewritten.** The prior version was a feature-mapping comparison (Plan 9 X maps to pane Y, with a summary table). The new version:
> - Focuses on what each idea *means* rather than what it maps to
> - Does not presume granular pane design decisions (no references to specific implementations like postcard serialization, calloop, session type details, etc.)
> - Engages with the designer's own stated views (quotes the "superset of the network kit" framing, the protocol bridge concept)
> - Identifies the deepest tension: Plan 9's composability through uniformity of representation vs pane's composability through typed protocols with richer structure
> - Concludes with the insight that the filesystem interface resolves this tension by providing universality alongside the typed native protocol</result>

> <result>Complete. All tasks covered, all subsections properly structured. Here's the summary:
> 
> ---
> 
> Research document written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/research-beos.md` (677 lines).
> 
> **Coverage:**
> - **1.1** — Full Haiku API kit overview: Application, Interface, Storage, Support, Translation kits, their dependency structure, and why BMessage is the lingua franca
> - **1.2** — BMessage/BLooper/BHandler threading model in depth: the one-thread-per-window architecture, why pervasive multithreading produced stability (message passing eliminating shared state, self-contained handler semantics, protocol as coordination, isolation containing failures, scheduler granularity), how BMessage enabled loose coupling distinct from RPC
> - **1.3** — Translation Kit plugin model: the add-on protocol (Identify/Translate/quality ratings), the common interchange format reducing translators from O(n^2) to O(n), discovery by directory convention, "any app reads any format" workflow
> - **1.4** — BFS attributes and BQuery: typed indexed attributes, the predicate language, static vs live queries, node monitoring as the reactive layer, how metadata-as-first-class changed UX (no import/export, cross-app search, shell scriptability, universal Tracker columns)
> - **1.5** — Replicant/BArchivable system: the archive/instantiate/shelf mechanism, cross-process code loading, what it enabled and what it cost (in-process execution, C++ fragility, underutilization, no protocol safety)
> - **1.6** — BRoster and launch_daemon: application identity vs execution context, launch semantics, the evolution from shell-script boot to event-driven parallel startup via pre-created ports
> - **1.7** — The email composition case study in full detail: mail_daemon writing files+attributes, BFS indexing, Tracker displaying attribute columns, live queries as inboxes, BeMail as viewer. Also covers IM Kit and shell scripting as proof of generality
> - **1.8** — Synthesis organized around four principles: (1) hardest-case design produces superior architecture, (2) stability emerges from protocol not coordinators, (3) session types formalize what BMessage discipline achieved, (4) infrastructure-first enables emergent composition. Includes concrete continuities and departures between BeOS and pane with justifications
> 
> The synthesis is written at the level of ideas, not feature mapping, as instructed. It connects BeOS's empirical success to pane's theoretical foundation (session types formalizing what convention achieved) and uses the email composition as the central evidence for infrastructure-first design.</result>

> 
> **3.1 (Vasconcelos):** Added Honda's CONCUR 1993 paper as a source. Added explanation of why sequencing matters (session types describe *time*, not just message sets). Expanded the subject reduction explanation to describe how the type *advances* with the conversation.
> 
> **3.2 (Caires-Pfenning):** Strengthened the "proofs = processes" explanation -- it's not an analogy, the mathematical objects are identical viewed from different perspectives. Added context linking this to the original Curry-Howard correspondence (sequential computation : intuitionistic logic :: concurrent communication : linear logic). Noted that par follows Wadler's classical approach specifically.
> 
> **3.3 (par crate):** Added the `Exchange` enum internals and explained how linking works (forwarding via the Exchange::Link variant). Added the full mechanical explanation of how `choose` works (fork_sync on the dual, variant constructor captures the session endpoint, returns the other end). Added the Proxy::clone closure pattern explanation with code. Added dialectic as a reference point for transport-polymorphic session types. Added the calloop futures executor as the integration path. Added explicit error handling as a thing par doesn't do.
> 
> **3.4 (Deadlock freedom):** Added emphasis that this is a *static* guarantee (compile time, not runtime). Added the observation that the tree restriction is the right choice for pane specifically (simplicity over expressiveness). Kept the priority-based alternative discussion.
> 
> **3.5 (Synthesis):** Completely rewritten to be idea-centered rather than feature-list-centered. Four sections:
> - "Discipline before theory, theory after discipline" -- centers the BeOS/session-types relationship on the designer's stated view: BeOS proved it empirically, session types formalize what those engineers achieved by skill
> - "What compile-time protocol verification changes" -- written around what it means for a *desktop environment specifically* (you can't curl a hung compositor)
> - "What the tree topology means architecturally" -- reframed as a design guide, not a limitation. The constraint pushes toward looser coupling, unidirectional data flow, materialized views
> - "The open questions" -- transport bridge (three approaches, recommends spec-only as starting point), crash handling (affine vs linear gap, crash boundaries needed), calloop+async (concrete integration path via calloop::futures), pane-proto relationship

> **Coverage:**
> 
> NeXTSTEP (3.1--3.8):
> - **Services menu** — full architecture (pasteboard mechanism, type-driven matching, service declaration format, dynamic discovery), what made it powerful on NeXTSTEP, why it failed on Mac OS X (Carbon incompatibility, discoverability, no composition visibility), and implications for pane-route/pane-roster
> - **Objective-C message passing** — runtime dispatch mechanics, what it enabled (Interface Builder, Services, Distributed Objects, plugins), what it cost (performance vs C++ vtables, no static guarantees), and comparison with BeOS's C++ choice
> - **Interface Builder / NIB model** — Hullot's creation, the "UI as data not code" principle, outlets/actions/connections, how it depended on dynamic dispatch, how it enforced MVC, and the parallel to pane's declarative content model
> - **Workspace Manager / app lifecycle** — File Viewer with Miller columns, Shelf, Inspector plugin system, the .app bundle model, the Dock, and comparison with BeOS's BRoster (Workspace Manager as GUI shell vs BRoster as programmatic API)
> - **Display PostScript** — resolution-independent rendering via PostScript imaging model, the DPS pipeline, per-window PostScript contexts (proto-compositing), performance costs, and the transition to Quartz/PDF
> - **Dock and application switching** — NeXTSTEP's minimal Dock vs Mac OS X's animated Dock, what the evolution teaches about information density vs emotional engagement
> - **Distributed Objects** — NSConnection/NSDistantObject/NSProxy architecture, message forwarding mechanism, pass-by-copy vs pass-by-reference, trade-offs, and comparison with Plan 9's 9P and BeOS's BMessage (transparent proxying is seductive but dishonest)
> - **Design philosophy** — why NeXTSTEP felt integrated (shared runtime, shared imaging model, shared application model, opinionated defaults), the specific innovations, and Jobs's design intuition
> 
> Early Mac OS X / Aqua (3.9--3.14):
> - **Aqua visual design** — every element (translucency, gel buttons, traffic lights, pinstripe, shadows, rounded corners, Dock, genie effect, palette), Ratzlaff's design philosophy, Jobs's "lickable" comment, the ten HIG principles, performance reality of 10.0--10.2, and the trajectory from maximal to restrained
> - **Quartz Compositor** — backing store model, WindowServer architecture, event routing, GPU acceleration (Quartz Extreme), how compositing enabled visual effects and accessibility, the separation of content from presentation
> - **Cocoa vs Carbon** — the Rhapsody plan (Yellow Box / Blue Box), why it was rejected, the Carbon compromise, Core Foundation as shared substrate, what was gained (developer adoption) and lost (fragmented Services, split investment, diluted vision)
> - **Expose** — spatial window management, why it worked (spatial awareness, live windows, visual recognition, smooth animation, hardware acceleration), what Mission Control broke
> - **Services menu on Mac OS X** — technical machinery, the four failure modes, what it would have needed to succeed
> - **Accessibility** — the 10.2 API, role-based semantics, how compositing enabled accessibility, VoiceOver
> 
> Synthesis (3.15):
> - Integration as architectural property, not cosmetic
> - Visual refinement's relationship to usability (depth as hierarchy, warmth as welcome, hierarchy through restraint)
> - Services menu lessons for cross-app composition
> - Aqua's visual language: depth, warmth, information hierarchy
> - The Frutiger Aero intersection and what it means for pane's aesthetic</result>

> <summary>Agent "Research wio Wayland compositor" completed</summary>
> <result>Written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/research-wio.md` (243 lines).
> 
> **Summary of findings:**
> 
> Wio is Drew DeVault's 2019 experimental Wayland compositor that reproduces rio's visual appearance and mouse-driven interaction model (right-click menu, draw-to-create windows, modal resize/move/delete). It's built on wlroots in ~800 lines of C and has been dormant since 2020.
> 
> **The key architectural decision** is cage-per-window: each wio window runs a full cage kiosk compositor containing a terminal emulator. This achieves rio's property of programs appearing inside their parent window, but at catastrophic resource cost (500MB+ RAM for the compositor alone). Nobody built on this approach.
> 
> **What was never implemented:** The rio-esque FUSE filesystem (issue #2), window hiding, the built-in command line, per-process namespaces. These were the features that would have made wio architecturally interesting rather than just visually faithful.
> 
> **The core finding for pane:** Wio proves that rio's *visual UX* ports trivially to Wayland but rio's *architecture* (filesystem multiplexing, per-process namespaces, transparent recursive composition) does not. The gap is structural: Wayland is a typed object protocol, not a file server; Linux lacks Plan 9's cheap per-process namespace manipulation; clients know they're Wayland clients. Pane's approach of building integration *above* the Wayland protocol (structured tags, plumber, message bus) rather than trying to replace it is validated by wio's experience.</result>

> <summary>Agent "Redo extensibility research" completed</summary>
> <result>All 7 sections are present. The document is written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/research-extensibility.md` (426 lines).
> 
> Here's what the research covers:
> 
> **Section 1 — Emacs:** Deep dive into the extension mechanism (buffers, modes, hooks, advice, keymaps), what makes the ecosystem thrive (universal abstraction, MELPA, runtime inspectability), the costs (no type safety, namespace collisions, monkey-patching fragility, performance chaos, debugging difficulty), and the "Emacs as OS" metaphor with analysis of how to get the power without the chaos.
> 
> **Section 2 — BeOS:** Translation Kit (Identify/Translate protocol, quality ratings, common interchange format for O(n) scaling, directory-based discovery), replicants (BArchivable protocol, cross-process embedding, C++ ABI fragility), Input Server add-ons (device/filter/method pipeline), and how BMessage/BLooper provided safety through message-level rather than function-level interception.
> 
> **Section 3 — Plan 9:** Acme's filesystem-as-API (the per-window file interface, event file as hook mechanism, language-agnostic extension), plumber routing rules as text files, and the trade-offs of filesystem-based vs API-based extension.
> 
> **Section 4 — NeXTSTEP:** Services menu architecture (pasteboard protocol, Info.plist declarations, content-type matching), the bundle model, and why Services failed on macOS (uncontrolled proliferation, no permission model, poor discoverability, no composition).
> 
> **Section 5 — Modern models:** VS Code (extension host process isolation, typed RPC protocol, scoped API, activation events, proposed API governance), browser extensions (component isolation, manifest permissions, sandboxing), Nix (declarative module system, lazy fixpoint evaluation, type-safe merging), Lua embedding (Neovim's dual-track Lua+RPC architecture, Redis EVAL, OpenResty), WASM plugins (Zellij's wasmi runtime, permission model, Protocol Buffer communication, Extism framework).
> 
> **Section 6 — The design problem:** What makes ecosystems thrive vs die, safe extension boundaries (type safety, memory safety, capability restriction, composability), declarative vs imperative extension (a spectrum from data to code to servers), "same interfaces internally and externally," static types as guardrails, and the data→code→server spectrum.
> 
> **Section 7 — Synthesis:** How each reference system informs a unified philosophy, culminating in: "the extension surface is the protocol surface, the deployment mechanism is the filesystem, and the type system is the safety guarantee."</result>

> 
> ---
> 
> Written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/benewsletter-index.md` (1329 lines).
> 
> **What it covers:**
> 
> The index organizes the Be Newsletter archive (Issues 1-1 through 5-17, Dec 1995 - Apr 2000) into 15 topic categories:
> 
> 1. **Messaging / Threading** -- 20+ articles including Peter Potrebic's definitive 4-part messaging series (2-35 through 2-38) and the foundational threading commandments (1-4)
> 2. **Interface Kit / App Server** -- app_server architecture, clipping, rendering pipeline, BDirectWindow
> 3. **Storage / BFS** -- Giampaolo's filesystem-as-database design, attributes, queries, node monitoring
> 4. **Translation Kit** -- The canonical extensibility pattern, add-on architecture
> 5. **Media Kit** -- Real-time node graph architecture, latency management, buffer recycling
> 6. **Kernel / Scheduler** -- Threading primitives, SMP, scheduling, kernel ports (the messaging primitive)
> 7. **Design Philosophy** -- Gassee columns on responsiveness, simplicity, "build for the hardest case"
> 8. **Replicants / Archiving** -- Live embedding, serialization, BShelf
> 9. **App Lifecycle / Roster** -- Process registration, MIME types, supervision
> 10. **Network Kit** -- Including the prescient "networked BMessenger" vision (1-7)
> 11. **Input / Devices** -- Input server modular architecture

> <summary>Agent "Extract BeNewsletter design wisdom" completed</summary>
> <result>Written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/benewsletter-wisdom.md`.
> 
> The document is organized into 14 thematic sections plus a summary, with direct quotes attributed to their authors and issue numbers. Here is what it covers:
> 
> **Highest-value sections for pane:**
> 
> 1. **Threading Model** -- Benoit Schillings and Peter Potrebic on why per-window threads work, with Potrebic's two commandments for thread safety. The key insight is that threading was structural (every window gets threads automatically) rather than opt-in.
> 
> 2. **Client/Server Architecture** -- Bob Herold on how libbe.so converts API calls to port messages with servers allocating a thread per client. George Hoffman on async vs sync calls and why batching async calls is essential.
> 
> 3. **Benaphores** -- Schillings's 20x optimization for uncontested locks by checking an atomic variable before hitting the kernel. Optimize for the common (uncontested) case.
> 
> 4. **Networked BMessenger Vision** -- Bradley Taylor's 1996 vision of BMessenger transparently targeting local or remote applications. This is exactly pane-route's design goal.
> 
> 5. **BLooper/BMessage/BHandler Architecture** -- The four primitives (BLooper, BMessage, BHandler, BMessageFilter) that session types formalize.
> 
> 6. **Memory Costs** -- Pierre Raynaud-Richard's precise measurements: threads cost ~20KB, windows ~70KB. This justifies per-pane (not per-widget) threading.
> 
> 7. **What Went Wrong** -- DSP heterogeneity abandoned, BMessage(BMessage*) deprecated, node_refs on disk discouraged, sync calls recognized as an anti-pattern.
> 
> The summary distills 8 things BeOS got right that pane should preserve, and 5 things it got wrong that pane should avoid. The most important takeaway: BeOS enforced its threading discipline by convention and documentation; pane can enforce it by construction through session types.</result>

### `2026-03-18T21:47:22.291Z`

> My vision is that Panes become the object of a category, whose morphisms are given by the interactions that change the state. The idea is that we have a monadic/compositional way to aggregate/organize the system state as a whole that is guided by safety guarantees of our modern infrastructure, preventing bad code smells due to hacky ways of accounting for this or that case. This notion might lead us to consider that panes as a general object (which are composed with other panes) exist in a monoidal structure, which is to say panes can be tensored (viewing two windows, multiple widgets contained in another pane, etc) and the interactions are the operations that transform the context (i.e. state) of a pane. The general intuition I'm working on is that committing to "every interface (for the user or system) is a pane" may lead to interesting and powerful results due to the uniformity benefits we've extensively analyzed -- we should be bold at breaking from conventional design patterns to see what fidelity to this vision brings.

### `2026-03-18T21:51:59.758Z`

> dont foreground the category theory (if anything mention as a brief and innocuous aside for those in the know to recognize), as many programmers bristle at this kind of thing. But otherwise yes. I also wanted to add: panes have *many different ways to view them*, for example, a pane may display to a user while exposing a fs metadata interface for its various operations or for things like sockets, etc. this is also the design choice that makes it plausible for me that wrapping legacy apps in a pane that abstracts over its implementation (that can be guided in a .app-like fashion by a dir with various scripts, auxillary helpers, etc for integration, so that these things can be managed at the app level and not through a dedicated registry (we're very much against the Windows/Gnome registry, which is horrible UX)

> <summary>Agent "Research optics for pane views" completed</summary>
> <result>The document is complete. Written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/research-optics.md`.
> 
> Here's a summary of what the research found:
> 
> **The core finding:** Optics give pane a correctness vocabulary and design discipline for its multi-view architecture, but should be adopted as *thinking*, not as a library dependency.
> 
> **What optics buy pane:**
> 
> 1. **Lens laws as correctness spec.** Every read-write interface (filesystem, protocol) should satisfy GetPut and PutGet -- these are the precise formalization of "this view is consistent with internal state." These are testable via property tests.
> 
> 2. **The complement concept.** Each view is lossy -- it shows only part of the pane's state. Making the lost information (the "complement") explicit prevents bugs where code assumes a view is lossless. The filesystem can't reconstruct a pane; `put` always needs the original source.
> 
> 3. **Quotient equivalence.** The right round-trip criterion isn't byte-level identity but semantic equivalence -- internal Rust structs and filesystem text/JSON are different syntactic representations of the same content. This is what pane-fs property tests should verify.
> 
> 4. **Composition story.** pane-fs composes two optics: the server's lens from internal state to protocol, and pane-fs's iso from protocol to filesystem format. This mirrors the architecture spec's description of pane-fs as "just another client."
> 
> **What optics do NOT provide:** notification/reactivity (orthogonal concern -- optics say *what* to project, reactive systems say *when*), session-typed protocol discipline (session types are the right tool for conversations, not lens laws), or a Rust library worth depending on (the Rust ecosystem's optics crates are immature, and Rust's ownership model already provides the mechanical equivalent via `&T`, `&mut T`, and pattern matching).
> 
> **Actionable design rule:** Every read-write interface to a pane should satisfy GetPut and PutGet (up to quotient equivalence), verified by property tests. Violations are either intentionally lossy (document it) or bugs (fix it).</result>

> 
> - **Sections 1-7** (from the other agent): Optics fundamentals, profunctor formulation, bidirectional transformations/view-update problem, Rust optics landscape, multi-view problem applied to pane, design implications, summary.

> 
> Here's a summary of what I added and the key findings:
> 
> **Section 8 (BMessage as ad-hoc optics):** BMessage's three structural bug classes (name typos, type mismatches, schema drift) are all eliminated by optics. Critically, pane's existing `TypedView`/`TypedBuilder` patterns in the `inter-server-views` spec are already optics in disguise -- `TypedView::parse()` is a prism, `TypedBuilder` is a review. The spec's rule that raw `msg.attr("key")` is banned in production code is the optics discipline stated operationally.
> 
> **Section 9 (BMessageFilter as ad-hoc optics):** BMessageFilter's inspect/modify/swallow/pass operations map directly to affine traversals. Filter chains are optic composition. What optics add over BMessageFilter: type safety on transformations, composition laws, and bidirectionality.
> 
> **Section 10 (Session types + optics):** Session types are the horizontal structure (conversation over time); optics are the vertical structure (state access at each step). The key find is **Session-OCaml** (Imai, Yoshida, Yuen 2017/2019) -- a published library that combines session types with lenses, using lenses to enforce linearity via "slots." This validates the combination as practical, not just theoretical. No published work yet combines *profunctor* optics with session types -- that's an open research direction.
> 
> **Section 11 (Categorical connection):** Pane's spatial composition (tensor product) produces compound state (product type), and the optics that decompose it are the monoidal projections. Three categorical structures (session types, optics, pane composition) are consistent. The theory validates the existing design.
> 
> **Section 12 (Practical recommendation):** Option 1 (optics as vocabulary, not library) now; Option 2 (thin traits) when repetition justifies it. Both agents converge on this conclusion.
>

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a9759e1ed760ba77d</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01XBVze3kVbQnbAvbnzaqEyk</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/1cdb03a1-c64b-4ec9-b3bd-e512fbbdf449/tasks/a9759e1ed760ba77d.output</output-file>

> <summary>Agent "Research monadic error handling" completed</summary>
> <result>That's fine -- it's inside a code block. The grep just doesn't know about markdown fencing. No issue.
> 
> The document is complete at `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/research-error-handling.md` (790 lines, ~45KB).
> 
> Here is what it covers:
> 
> **Section 1 -- Monadic Error Handling Fundamentals.** Result/Either as the compositional alternative to exceptions. How `and_then`/bind propagates errors through pipelines. What the monadic approach buys: totality, compositionality, referential transparency, type-level error channels. The connection between CPS and session types -- session types ARE typed continuations, so error-aware sessions need error-aware continuations, which is exactly Result in a session type position.
> 
> **Section 2 -- Error Handling in Session-Typed Systems.** Four approaches from the literature:
> - Explicit Result branches in session types (application-level errors, fully typed, but doesn't handle crashes)
> - Fowler et al.'s Exceptional Asynchronous Session Types (POPL 2019) -- cancel/try/raise constructs, with cancellation propagation when exceptions occur
> - Lagaillardie et al.'s MultiCrusty (ECOOP 2022) -- directly addresses Rust's panic problem with affine session types, where Drop triggers cancellation signals instead of counterpart panics
> - Barwell et al.'s crash-stop failures (CONCUR 2022) -- parameterized reliability assumptions matching pane's hierarchy (compositor must not crash, clients may crash freely)
> 
> The synthesis identifies three error layers: application errors (Result branches inside sessions), participant crashes (cancellation at session boundaries), and infrastructure recovery (supervision above both).
> 
> **Section 3 -- Supervision and Recovery Patterns.** Erlang/OTP supervision trees (one_for_one/rest_for_one/one_for_all strategies, restart budgets, the "131/132 heisenbugs" empirical result). Akka's supervision vs death watch distinction. BeOS's implicit crash isolation via per-thread BLoopers -- Pavel Cisler's Lock()-returns-error pattern and George Hoffman's app_server message-shedding behavior. Maps the supervision hierarchy onto pane's architecture.
> 
> **Section 4 -- Monadic Composition of Recovery Strategies.** Retry with backoff as a monad transformer. Circuit breaker as a state machine monad. Fallback chains via `or_else` (dual of `and_then`). Graceful degradation via partial-result types. How these compose with session types: application errors inside the session type, crash recovery wrapping around it, supervision wrapping both.
> 
> **Section 5 -- The Linear Types Gap in Rust.** Why affine types (can drop) differ from linear types (must use). The panic problem for linear types (Faultlore, Wuyts). What par does today (#[must_use] + runtime panic). Four practical solutions: cancellation-aware recv wrapper using catch_unwind, session boundary threads (BLooper pattern), heartbeat protocol for transport-level death detection, custom Drop on socket-backed session endpoints.
> 
> **Section 6 -- Architecture for Pane.** Concrete three-layer error handling architecture with code sketches. Layer 1: Result branches in session types for application errors. Layer 2: per-client threads with catch_unwind as crash boundaries, converting panics to CompositorEvent::ClientDisconnected. Layer 3: pane-init restart policies with budgets. Plus: roster as liveness oracle, compositor crash recovery via state serialization, the full monadic error handling stack diagram.</result>

### `2026-03-18T22:49:04.838Z`

> 1. The balance is good. The objection about notifications actually strengthens
> my argument: notifications are distinct information-presenting objects. Being
> able to think about them as a pane we get that multiple views are powerful
> here: imagine that notifications are displayed (as a floating pane at certain
> or anchored placement); but usually they come and disappear, and can be cleared
> from view with no ability to see. Imagine that we had the ability to view notifications
> via the projection of their attributes in the fs-layer, so they could be easily
> queried if one had the inclination to do it. One doesnt have to have this functionality,
> they could perhaps set a clock that starts after each notification that deletes them
> after it runs out (like modern clipboards do). Finally re: your third point, we could
> improve our presentation in the way you describe.
> 
> 2. Your first concern I can relieve, this is the right level of abstraction --
> any distinctions which are immediately relevant for any operational implementation
> ought to be referenced for context. As to your second one, I think you could strengthen
> the discussion for reference for later decisions -- note it as a design tension but
> that we lean for type safety whenever possible, and guide our design to make it possible
> where naive or conventional approaches might not
> 
> 3. Its fine if you include familiar references. The idea for theory presentation in
> general to give people who want to look into the foundations enough information to
> go on, and for people who are familiar to understand more precisely our orientation,
> but we dont want to come off as pretentious or unpractical -- we are using theory
> because it will best help us resolve real design dilemmas we encounter, in the spirit
> of Be's work: we include the strongest design, which forces us into the best programming
> patterns that improve the experience for any system or user
> 
> 4. We could flesh out this section, integrate its analysis with the be experience
> and our existing theoretical references. That we handle errors with monadic design
> patterns is not too implementation specific -- this is the right level of abstraction
> to speak of the choices pane is declaratively establishing out the outset. The purpose
> is to fix what should be fixed and leave more trivial details open so they dont cloud
> our judgment and leave us focusing on what's truly needed. For the supervision hierarchy
> this is also fine. Again we dont need to be too abstract -- the right level is to be able
> to follow be's design philosophy (among the others we draw upon), it seems clear we are
> already making certain implementation commitments, the goal is to establish how those
> specific choices are congruent with and arise as the fulfillments of the theoretical
> frameworks we draw upon, so some mention of implementation is required for good context.
> 
> 5. We should discuss costs with the framing of judging whether we get more out of
> our chosen abstractions than what they cost us. Small inefficiencies for more 
> robust and clear systems design, both in isolation and in aggretate (so we think of
> the "network effect" of our design choices) may pay off a lot more (and even be
> more performant in some cases) than designs that attempt to microoptimize each routine.
> An irony i've observed from even the best programmers is that they are so proud of their
> cleverness and codewriting ability that they become susceptible to myopia and sometimes
> fail to consider how the overfitting for their specific implementation directions forced
> them into bad compromises with the overall architecture, which they were not keen on
> revisiting due to their sunk cost fallacy. This has played out time and time again
> in the open source world.
> 
> 6. Yes this is a great start, but it bears some fleshing out more concretely how this
> informs the design philosophy of pane as a whole, referencing specific examples
> 
> 7. This seems confused because we shouldnt fight the client side rendering -- the
> protocol serves as precisely the mechanism of how multiple wayland applications
> can work harmoniously in the context of the ux as a whole.
> 
> 8. The concept of kits are more general than that. We might imagine the multiple-lang
> tooling as an important component of the app kit. We like the beos distinction between
> servers and kits, and we should extend the notion of our kits to encompass real-world use
> cases in the 21st century. for example, I imagine an AI kit providing first class
> and principle treatment of AI agents/assistants, particularly geared towards support
> of API-linked or local models alike, with a seamless experience going back and forth,
> so that users can optimize their tasks betweeen models of different strengths with
> interfaces empowering the user's ability to prompt and direct  via mechanisms enforced
> by the pane API itself and interoperable with it, so that prompting can directly mingle
> with type safe and principled organization of agent usage and behavior in declarative
> specifications written by users and agents alike, which can leverage these tools for
> robust *and* expressive AI usage patterns that are not possible (or difficult to achieve) 
> with current tooling (this is actually one of the potential "killer apps" of pane).
> 
> I agree that these changes will likely require reordering. dont try to fit the new
> content to the old structure, assess the best presentation from all the considerations
> and changes we're making to the spec presently.
> 
> as far as tone, you are correct the document is assertive and it ought to be confident,
> but clear that we are considering our design carefully and being uncompromising also
> applies to interrogating our own design decisions at every stage of the inception
> and implementation of our various features and systems.
> 
> The aesthetic component missing is definitely a problem. We want to stress that behind
> all of this thought put into the best systems design is a commitment to leverage them
> for excellent user experiences that reimagine OS design from the fork in the road
> that presented itself in the Aqua/Aero moment, continuing the power-user-friendly but
> also elegant interface design that Be pioneered, updated for 2026.

### `2026-03-18T23:06:50.078Z`

> what would be good is a research report focused on all the subsystems we're utilizing so far, the wayland stack, pipewire/alsa/JACK (we manage abstracted over the media kit), linux, s6/runit/systemd analysis. since wayland will be a central component of our system stack we ought to dedicate a full research report solely to it so it can inform our statements. as far as "the Aqua/Aero fork realized," I may have been unclear. I loved the design commitments of (mainly aqua) Frutiger aero -- Im not referencing MS Aero specifically, just the general state of design circa 2001, where there were still great ideas being pushed forward by Apple. the fork im referring to is the one where Apple/MS were the only survivors of the 90s platform wars while all the alternatives like plan9/beos/haiku were pushed to obscurity due to waning interest and lack of support for applications where proprietary applications were often the only viable options and vendor locked to walled garden ecosystems. In my view this profoundly distorted the course of personal computer design. Convenient interfaces were accompanied by increasingly opaque abstractions that had the tendency to limit the user's understanding of how anything in their computer worked, and made it more inconvenient for them to design their own custom solutions other than what could be sold on app stores and the like. While this was often couched in terms of security assurances (unidentified developers and custom toolings are potentially untrustworthy), the insistence on the monolithic architecture just increased the attack surface on users who were universally required to use the whole kitchen sink ecosystem of the contemporary operating system designs. This is also fraught because OS design has been stagnant, and inertia leads to development cycles accumulating features at the expense of a coherent or integrated design philosophy -- while not being attentive to accumulating cruft. The idea with pane is: the core system has to be compact, efficient, and bulletproof. every other part of the OS or user experience can be rewritten wholesale in a ships-of-theseus style as the needs of users evolve over time. we do require a commitment to our architecture, but that commitment is minimized as much as possible for the greatest possible gain in terms of expressive design potentials, and being minimal allows the actual cost being paid by the user for that commitment to be understood in its various consequences and facets. The name "pane" has a metaphorical component to it: it is a transparent object, its purpose is to be able to view what it presents clearly.

### `2026-03-18T23:40:42.115Z`

> I don't think that ai-as-a-pane is the way to frame it. agents should be thought of as *additional users* in the system. We want to take direct inspiration from the distributed computing model of plan9, where various sytem services can operate in principle across secure network connections (the core message/session-based protocol, network server/kit, and translation kit ought to enable this). agents should be managed in sandboxed user environemnts, and permissions managed by declarative specification (configuration files/settings/prompts that are statically checked when possible and purpose made to execute the intention of the user). I even imagine fun uses of old subsystems, like intra-system emails (those old "You have mail." prompts of old school unix mainframes) being used as an actual communication protocol by agents. plan9 departed after Bell Labs' extensive experience thinking about the evolution of unix spanning back decades, we should continue that but based on the design potentials offered by contemporary linux environments

>

>

> 
> **Section 5 — XWayland**: Architecture (X11 server as Wayland client), rootless vs rootful modes, window identification (WL_SURFACE_ID ClientMessage), and impedance mismatches (security, coordinates, scaling, input, clipboard, deadlock risk).
> 
> **Section 6 — Linux Graphics Stack**: Full stack diagram, DRM/GEM/KMS, Mesa/GPU drivers, GBM buffer allocation, the compositor-Mesa-kernel relationship.
> 
> **Section 7 — What Wayland Doesn't Provide**: No window management policy, no global hotkeys, screen recording still evolving, no network transparency, clipboard limitations (dies with source app), no window inspection, no standard desktop integration suite.
> 
> **Section 8 — Accessibility**: AT-SPI2/D-Bus current state, broken mouse synthesis and window hierarchy, the compositor knows-position-but-not-content gap, and how pane's centralized rendering with semantic content descriptions structurally solves this for native clients.
> 
> **Section 9 — Implications for Pane**: The two protocol layers (Wayland + pane protocol), two client classes in protocol terms, what pane-comp needs from smithay (must-use vs should-use), alignment with Wayland (compositor-as-authority, security isolation, no built-in policy), divergence (centralized rendering, dual protocols, content semantics, session types, network aspirations), critical integration points (frame timing, input dispatch, damage tracking, buffer management, clipboard bridging), and how Wayland's gaps become pane's opportunities.</result>

### `2026-03-19T07:16:39.393Z`

> i was continually dissatisfied watching the path things took in the 2000s and beyond. MacOS X was nice but there was a turning point where the it became clear controlling and extractive nature of the enterprise became the main driving force behind new feature development. Windows is, like you said, worse -- it was already that way in the 90s. In addition, concerning political trends motivate the design of a system capable of facilitating the progress of 21st century computing against hinderances from actors who have historically committed to restraining it out of their own self-interest. The very capacities which could define a golden age of computing, fulfilling the dreams nurtured in the 90s and 2000s, have been stymied time and time again. For all these reasons, the time for the Linux Desktop (tm) is now. I say the last part tongue in cheek because every year someone writes an article about why that will be the year of the Linux Desktop. But its not quite an accident that its never been quite true. My hypothesis is that to break with the trend, a powerful, forceful, and evocative gesture is needed of the caliber of early 2000s Apple keynotes. The reason why the linux desktop never became a thing is because no actor was visionary enough to put forth a decisive and satisfactory word about how Linux could be something more than it already was, and what it already was determined that it would remain a niche. Not just because a steep learning curve (somewhat ameloriated these days) naturally put off people who were happy enough with mainstream OSes (in fact, since they never knew anything else, they didnt even know how their needs were already being failed compared to the potentials offered by alternatives they were distracted from). This initial barrier which made adoption already precarious was compounded by a further development: the evolution of mainstream platforms actively and deliberately reduced the tech literacy of users in general, and multiplied the learning curve. The boldness of pane's vision is not a matter of ego -- but because all the attempts to kickstart a broader adoption of Linux by piecemeal refinement have failed. Given the precarious state of personal computing in 2026, it seems like this is the perfect and advantageous moment to make a bold step to buck the current trend

> 
> Here's a summary of the research findings while you decide how to proceed:
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Key findings
> 
> **The answer is: highly feasible. Someone has already done it.**
> 
> ### The critical architectural fact
> nixpkgs has two halves: `pkgs/` (~120,000 application packages) and `nixos/` (~2,022 system modules). These are genuinely separable. The sixos project (codeberg.org/amjoseph/sixos) depends on `nixpkgs/pkgs` while sharing zero code with `nixpkgs/nixos`. It runs in production on workstations, servers, routers, and a 768-core buildfarm.
> 
> ### The systemd coupling lives in three places
> 1. **NixOS module wrappers** (~1,200 service modules) — irrelevant if you write your own system layer
> 2. **Packages linking `libudev`** (hundreds) — solvable via overlay replacing `systemd.lib` with `libudev-zero`
> 3. **Packages with hard, non-optional systemd deps** — under 20, mostly GNOME/KDE session components
> 
> ### The recommended approach
> - **Overlay + flake on upstream nixpkgs** (not a fork). Override `systemd.lib`, add your system layer, keep everything else upstream. Zero merge conflicts, free package updates.
> - s6 for init/service management
> - elogind as logind replacement
> - Custom system layer: ~709 KiB for sixos's entire implementation
> 
> ### Estimated effort
> ~3-6 months for one experienced person to reach daily-driver state. The sixos project, not-os, and NixNG are all architectural references.
> 
> ### The main cost
> Losing the NixOS binary cache for packages that transitively depend on libudev. You need your own build infrastructure.
> 
> ---
> 
> The full document with all sourced details, dependency analysis, precedent comparison, and risk assessment is ready to write. Grant Write or Bash permission and I'll create the file.</result>

### `2026-03-19T07:58:27.258Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a5f346a6c7ff26327</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01SNJsBPXRh4nQ9zGexXbpmN</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/ef1e071d-9bad-4939-8d53-386ccddb98d7/tasks/a5f346a6c7ff26327.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Research Nix as distro base" completed</summary>
> <result>The document is complete and well-structured. Here's a summary of what was done and the key findings.
> 
> ---
> 
> Research written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/research-nix-base.md` (523 lines, 13 sections).
> 
> **Key findings:**
> 
> 1. **Nix and NixOS are cleanly separable.** nixpkgs has two halves: `pkgs/` (120,000+ application packages, init-agnostic) and `nixos/` (the module system, thoroughly coupled to systemd). Sixos explicitly confirms: "Sixos is not a fork of NixOS. It shares no code with nixpkgs/nixos."
> 
> 2. **Sixos is the direct precedent.** A two-year, 428-commit project that builds a production Linux system on nixpkgs/pkgs with s6 instead of systemd. The author runs it on workstations, servers, routers, and a 768-core build farm. It replaces NixOS's module system with `infuse.nix`, where services are derivations (same algebra as packages). This is the architectural reference for pane.
> 
> 3. **The libudev dependency is the main package-level bottleneck.** Most "systemd dependencies" in packages are actually libudev dependencies. libudev-zero is a drop-in replacement. An overlay that swaps `systemd.lib` for libudev-zero breaks the transitive chain. Hundreds of packages need rebuilding, but the rebuild is mechanical and one-time.
> 
> 4. **The NixOS module system is deeply systemd-coupled and not needed.** The NixOS community consensus: "The ship has sailed for NixOS to use anything other than systemd." But Nix's `lib.evalModules` is reusable for defining a pane-specific module system with ~15-20 service definitions rather than 2,000.
> 
> 5. **The mutable/immutable config tension is solved.** NixOS's `system.etc.overlay` (overlayfs with writable upper layer) and the "erase your darlings" pattern both address it. Haiku's packagefs validates the same pattern independently via "shine-through directories." Pane's `/etc/pane/` can be writable state on top of a declarative base.
> 
> 6. **Practical approach:** Use nixpkgs as a flake input (not a fork), write pane's system layer as a separate flake, use s6-linux-init as PID 1, maintain a binary cache via Cachix. Estimated effort: 3-6 months to a daily-driver state.
> 
> 7. **Future upside:** Tvix/Snix (Rust reimplementations of Nix) are moving toward library-oriented architecture. Not production-ready yet, but if they mature, pane gets a Rust-native Nix implementation usable as a library rather than a CLI dependency.</result>

> for the router: I do imagine that potentially the router may have to make a decision for escalation procedures in the event of substantial systems failure. For example, one that somehow disables user input if such a thing is detectable. The five subsections cover:
> 
> 1. **Source-based distribution philosophy** — extension must feel like customizing Linux because it IS customizing Linux; congruence with existing user mental models is a design constraint, not a nice-to-have.
> 2. **pane.nvim and pane.el** — stock editor plugins as both adoption strategy and design forcing function; if pane's interfaces can't be naturally surfaced through a neovim plugin or emacs buffer, the design needs revision.
> 3. **Agents as extension contributors** — the extension surface is identical for agents and humans; agent-built artifacts are inspectable files on the same filesystem, producing the emacs/neovim plugin ecosystem dynamic with agents as contributors.
> 4. **Democratic orientation** — the base experience and the customized experience use the same mechanisms; extension is the fundamental nature of the system, not a bolted-on power-user feature.
> 5. **"Freedom is not too difficult"** — typed protocols, filesystem deployment, process isolation, and declarative specifications make freedom safe, accessible, and default rather than exceptional.</result>

> ---
> 
> Here's what was done:
> 
> **Appended a new major section** ("Nix as Build Infrastructure for a NeXTSTEP-Style Distribution") to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/research-nix-base.md`, covering nine subsections:
> 
> 1. **NeXTSTEP's relationship to Mach/BSD** — How NeXTSTEP achieved the "one thing" feel through shared mechanisms (one runtime, one imaging model, one application model), and what this means for pane's relationship to Linux. The core insight: the identity lives in the personality layer, not the kernel. Linux is pane's Mach — it provides primitives and stays invisible.
> 
> 2. **How Nix builds a complete system image** — The NixOS system closure pipeline (configuration evaluation -> toplevel derivation -> boot image -> activation), and how pane can replicate this pipeline without NixOS's module system. The system closure is just a derivation.
> 
> 3. **Sixos architecture in detail** — The infuse combinator (deep override replacing NixOS modules), tag-based configuration, the s6-linux-init boot sequence (PID 1 -> s6-svscan -> rc.init -> s6-rc), s6-rc's compiled database model (source directories -> compiled database, a natural Nix derivation), and what pane needs to add on top (pane service definitions, session management, system builder, config layer).
> 
> 4. **The distribution philosophy** — Resolves the apparent tension between deep integration and embracing Linux modularity. The answer: opinionated system layer, open application layer. This is exactly the macOS model. Nix's flake boundary makes this explicit.
> 
> 5. **The ship of Theseus with Nix** — Atomic upgrades via generations, per-user profiles mapping to the multi-user/agent architecture, per-workspace environments via `nix develop`, and the entire distribution defined as a flake (with sketch flake.nix examples for both the distribution and a user's system).
> 
> 6. **The mutable/immutable tension** — Three concrete patterns for reconciling Nix's immutable store with pane's writable `/etc/pane/` config: overlayfs, erase-your-darlings, and a pane-specific generation-aware config merge (recommended). The macOS preferences analogy applies.
> 
> 7. **Binary cache infrastructure** — Cachix for initial development, Attic for eventual self-hosting, strategic build minimization to share as much as possible with upstream nixpkgs cache.
> 
> 8. **Practical architecture** — The layer stack from hardware through applications, where each type of configuration lives, a sketch `mkPaneSystem.nix` builder, and how `pane-rebuild switch` works operationally.
> 
> 9. **Synthesis** — Six key conclusions about how Nix serves the "one integrated thing" goal: Nix is substrate not identity, the system closure is one artifact, opinionated/open layering, evolution without fragility, multi-inhabitant support via profiles, infrastructure not bureaucracy.</result>
