### `2026-03-20T07:39:52.058Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a3ebda1f9eba0d886</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_0157Capow14vrGmREQXw35Fc</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/61f51498-8a84-418f-90ca-c81947e7eb32/tasks/a3ebda1f9eba0d886.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Review foundations spec" completed</summary>
> <result>Now, my review.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Design Review: Pane Foundations & Architecture Specifications
> 
> I read both specs carefully, cross-referenced against the Haiku source (`~/src/haiku`) and the project's own BeOS research document. This is a serious design document that gets more right than wrong about what mattered at Be. But it also has blind spots, some overclaims, and a few places where the translation from BeOS's world to pane's world loses important nuances. I'll go section by section through your five questions.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 1. Where it faithfully captures and extends BeOS's design convictions
> 
> **The BLooper model and per-component threading (Section 5).** This is correct at the level of principle. The core claim — that BeOS's stability came from self-contained operational semantics per component, sequential message processing within each thread, concurrency arising between components not within them — is exactly right. I confirmed this in the Haiku source: `BLooper` owns a `thread_id` and processes messages sequentially via its run loop; `BWindow` inherits from `BLooper` (Window.h:93); on the server side, both `ServerApp` and `ServerWindow` extend `MessageLooper` (`src/servers/app/MessageLooper.h`), each with their own thread and port. The architecture spec's description of "servers allocate a thread per client" directly mirrors how app_server worked — one `ServerApp` per application, one `ServerWindow` per window, each with its own message loop thread.
> 
> **The email composition example (Section 7, and architecture spec).** This is the single best paragraph in the document. The description of how mail_daemon + BFS attributes + Tracker + BQuery + live queries composed into an email UX without any component knowing about email is precise and complete. The research document at `openspec/changes/spec-tightening/research-beos.md` lines 452-548 has the full technical picture, and the spec accurately captures the lesson: infrastructure-first design is inherently democratic because the infrastructure doesn't presume what the user does with it.
> 
> **The Translation Kit as extension model.** The architecture spec's translators section correctly identifies the pattern: drop a binary in a well-known directory, the whole system gains a capability. The quality-rating-based selection is mentioned (Section 7 architecture spec, line 100). The research doc has the full details including the common interchange format design, which is the real genius — linear translator count rather than quadratic.
> 
> **Session types as formalization of BMessage discipline (Section 3).** The framing that session types formalize what BMessage/BLooper achieved by convention is historically accurate and intellectually honest. The `what` field was indeed just a convention. Developers could send any message to any handler at any time; the protocol lived in documentation and the engineers' heads. Verified in the Haiku source: `BMessage` has a public `uint32 what` field with no constraint on values, and `BHandler::MessageReceived` dispatches on `what` via switch/case with an "unrecognized messages get passed to parent" pattern. Making this compile-time-checked is a genuine advance.
> 
> **Kits as programming model, not wrapper (architecture spec lines 248-256).** This gets the crucial distinction right. The kits were never thin bindings over a protocol. They were a complete programming model. When I wrote code against the Interface Kit, I was thinking in BViews and BWindows, not in "messages to app_server." The fact that BView drawing commands were batched into messages to the server was invisible. The spec correctly says "If kits are thin wrappers, developing for pane feels like wrestling with a protocol" — yes, exactly. This is the difference between a SDK and an FFI.
> 
> **The DSP lesson (architecture spec, Section 8 of foundations).** The reference to Be's abandoned heterogeneous DSP experiment and the "two programming models" lesson is correct and well-applied. Maintaining two system software stacks was indeed the problem.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 2. Where it misunderstands or mischaracterizes what BeOS got right
> 
> **"George Hoffman documented that the app_server would 'start to throw away update messages'" (Section 6, line 136).** I searched the Be Newsletter archive for this specific claim. George Hoffman did write in Issue 3-6 ("Subversive Features"), but that article is about BView state stacks in R3, not about message shedding. I couldn't find the specific quote attributed. The behavior described is plausible — app_server did handle unresponsive clients — but the attribution to Hoffman seems unverifiable in the newsletter archive. This matters because the spec is using this as evidence for a design principle. The principle is sound (graceful degradation), but the specific sourcing should be verified or softened.
> 
> **The Gassee "scaling" quote (architecture spec, line 118).** "Gassee observed that 'most systems only scale upwards — they bleed to death when a cut is attempted in order to downscale them.'" I searched the newsletter archive for this quote and couldn't verify it. Gassee wrote many newsletter columns (they're in the archive), but I couldn't find this specific phrasing. The claim that BeOS could fit on a 1.44MB floppy is also not quite right in the way implied — BeOS required at least a CD-ROM for installation and ran from a hard drive. There was a minimal bootable demo floppy at one point, but the idea that the client-server architecture is what enabled it is a stretch. The modularity was real, but the floppy claim overstates the evidence.
> 
> **"Schillings could say 'programming should be fun'" (architecture spec, line 64).** I searched for this in the newsletters. I cannot verify this specific attribution to Schillings. The sentiment was genuinely part of Be's culture — the newsletters are full of playful engineering writing — but floating an unverifiable quote as attributed fact in a design document weakens it. Either cite the source or say "the Be team believed" without attribution to a specific person.
> 
> **BMessage characterization is slightly off.** The foundations spec (Section 3, line 55) says "A BMessage could carry any typed data and flow through the system without tight coupling between sender and receiver." This is correct. But the implied characterization that BMessage was primarily about typed data is incomplete. Looking at `headers/os/app/Message.h`, BMessage carried delivery metadata (return address, whether source is waiting, drop point for drag-and-drop), scripting specifiers (the whole `B_NO_SPECIFIER` through `B_SPECIFIERS_END` enum), and nested messages. The richness of BMessage as a complete protocol unit — not just a data container — is important and underappreciated in this document.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 3. Where it makes commitments that BeOS's experience would caution against
> 
> **The "pane as universal object" may be repeating the Replicant mistake in different clothes.** The research doc at lines 302-363 is honest about what went wrong with Replicants: they were powerful in theory but barely used in practice, partly because they were framed as toys, but mostly because they required a commitment from developers that exceeded the perceived benefit. The "everything is a pane" commitment carries the same risk. If the abstraction is too heavy — if making a notification into a pane requires more ceremony than a transient popup — developers will work around it. Be's experience was that the right abstraction must be lightweight enough that developers reach for it naturally. The spec acknowledges this in passing but doesn't grapple with it concretely. What does a notification-as-pane cost in terms of resources, protocol setup, and lifecycle management compared to a notification-as-transient-event?
> 
> **"One opinionated look, no theme engine" (architecture spec, line 392).** This is a bold commitment that goes further than BeOS went. BeOS shipped with a decorator system in app_server — window chrome was pluggable. Haiku has this too (`src/servers/app/decorator/`). The Interface Kit had a consistent look because the primitives were consistent, but there was always room for decoration customization. The spec wants to be more opinionated than BeOS was. This might be right for a 2026 project trying to establish identity, but it is a departure from Be's approach, not a continuation of it. Be didn't ship a theme engine, but the architecture didn't prevent theming. Saying "no theme engine" while also saying "the stock aesthetic IS pane's identity" means you're using rigidity as a design tool. Be's experience suggests that once you have users, they will demand this. The Carbon/Cocoa lesson the spec cites is about API splits, not aesthetics.
> 
> **The router as "dispatch of last resort" (Section 6, line 146).** The spec says the router "is the one component that must not fail, because the entire communication model rests on it." This is exactly the kind of single point of failure that the rest of the document argues against. BeOS didn't have a central router. Messages went directly from sender to receiver via kernel ports. There was no intermediary that could become a bottleneck or a single point of failure. The BRoster was important but not essential to moment-to-moment operation — applications could communicate without it once they had each other's ports. Making pane-router critical to all communication is architecturally different from BeOS's peer-to-peer messaging, and the risks should be addressed more directly. What happens when pane-router restarts? Do all conversations break?
> 
> **Session types may be too rigid for the escape hatches BeOS needed.** The architecture spec correctly notes (line 74) that "BeOS's MIME type philosophy applies: escape hatches for edge cases, not a rigid cage." But the commitment to session types as the universal protocol mechanism may conflict with this. In BeOS, any handler could receive any message — the `what` field was just a uint32. You could send ad-hoc messages between applications that hadn't been anticipated by either application's designer. The `hey` command-line tool could script any application through the standard messaging protocol. This flexibility was essential for the scripting protocol to work. Session types, by design, constrain conversations to predetermined shapes. The document needs to address concretely how ad-hoc, unanticipated communication works in a session-typed world. The scripting protocol is almost entirely absent from both specs, and it was one of the most important things BeOS got right.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 4. Where it misses opportunities to learn from BeOS/Haiku
> 
> **The scripting protocol is the biggest gap.** Every `BHandler` in BeOS implemented `ResolveSpecifier()` and `GetSupportedSuites()` (I verified this in `headers/os/app/Handler.h` lines 58-61). The `property_info` struct in `PropertyInfo.h` declared what properties each handler exposed, what commands they accepted, and what specifiers addressed them. This meant every application was automatable through the same messaging system it used internally. The `hey` command-line tool could get and set properties on any running application. This was a first-class design commitment, not an afterthought.
> 
> The pane spec mentions filesystem exposure via FUSE as the scriptability story. But this misses what BeOS's scripting protocol provided: the ability to query and modify application state at runtime, in a structured way, through the same messaging system used for everything else. `hey Tracker get Poses of Window 0` was a real command that returned real data. The filesystem projection is valuable, but it's a different thing. It exposes state as files; it doesn't expose behavior as a queryable protocol. Pane should think about how session types could subsume and improve on BeOS's scripting protocol — this is a natural fit that the spec doesn't explore.
> 
> **Live queries are underspecified.** The architecture spec mentions that "live query maintenance" is "a client-side composition of index reads + change notification subscriptions." This is architecturally correct — in BeOS, live queries were implemented by the kernel sending B_QUERY_UPDATE messages to a target BMessenger when indexed attributes changed. But the spec doesn't acknowledge the subtlety: live query updates in BeOS could arrive before the initial result set was fully iterated (research doc line 264), and the B_ENTRY_REMOVED message didn't include the filename (line 260). These are real design traps that Haiku inherited and had to work around. If pane is going to support live queries, the protocol design should learn from these specific failure modes.
> 
> **The BMessageFilter pattern deserves explicit treatment.** The architecture spec (line 256) mentions filters briefly: "BMessageFilter could add cross-cutting behavior without subclassing." But this is more important than the passing mention suggests. Filters were how BeOS did middleware — intercepting, transforming, or redirecting messages before they reached their handler. In pane's session-typed world, the equivalent would be typed middleware that can observe or transform protocol conversations. This is a powerful composition mechanism that the spec's routing model partially addresses but doesn't fully capture. Routing handles content dispatch; filters handle protocol-level concerns (logging, rate limiting, access control, message transformation). These are different things.
> 
> **The BArchivable serialization system.** BeOS's ability to archive any object into a BMessage and reconstruct it elsewhere was foundational — not just for Replicants (which had problems) but for clipboard operations, drag-and-drop, session save/restore, and undo systems. The architecture spec mentions session save/restore (line 247) but doesn't discuss the serialization model. In a session-typed world, the equivalent would be a way to serialize session state — pause a conversation, persist it, resume it later. This connects directly to session persistence and crash recovery.
> 
> **The observer pattern on BHandler.** Looking at `headers/os/app/Handler.h` lines 63-81, BHandler had a built-in observer system: `StartWatching()`, `StopWatching()`, `SendNotices()`. This was separate from the message dispatch system — it was a pub/sub mechanism layered on top of messaging. The architecture spec mentions reactive signals (line 319) but doesn't connect this to the BeOS precedent. The observer pattern in BHandler was how components stayed synchronized without polling or tight coupling. It's worth studying how this worked and what it got right and wrong.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 5. What the Be engineers would think
> 
> **Jean-Louis Gassee** would be delighted by the framing of rendering power versus expressive power, which is correctly attributed to his thinking. He would nod at the infrastructure-first philosophy and the rejection of monolithic design. He would be skeptical of the category theory language — not because it's wrong, but because he always pushed for accessibility of communication. He'd say something like "this is right but it needs to be said more simply or nobody will build for it." He would love the agent-as-user model and would immediately see its commercial potential.
> 
> **Dominic Giampaolo** would read Section 7 (Infrastructure-First Design) and the email composition example and feel genuinely understood. The filesystem-as-database commitment via xattrs is the right translation of BFS attributes to Linux. He would worry about the xattr size limitations on ext4 (which are real — ext4 xattrs share a single 4KB block) and would push hard on whether pane-store's in-memory index can truly replace BFS's on-disk B+ tree indices for large collections. He would want to see benchmarks. He would also point out that BFS's typed attributes were more powerful than opaque xattr bytes — the filesystem knew the types, which made query optimization possible. Pane's `user.pane.type` metadata attribute for typing is a workaround, not an equivalent.
> 
> **Peter Potrebic** wrote some of the most important Be Newsletter articles about the scripting protocol and application automation. He would notice immediately that the scripting protocol is absent from this document. He would say that automation was not a nice-to-have — it was the mechanism by which the messaging system's power became accessible to users and tools. Without the equivalent of `ResolveSpecifier()` and `GetSupportedSuites()`, pane's applications are black boxes. The filesystem projection helps, but it's one-dimensional — you can read state but you can't query an application's capabilities or invoke operations through a structured, discoverable protocol. He would push for this hard.
> 
> **George Hoffman** would appreciate the aesthetic section and the commitment to visual identity. He cared about the app_server's rendering quality. He would be intrigued by the Frutiger Aero direction and would immediately start thinking about how the compositor should implement it. He would also point out that BeOS's rendering consistency came from app_server doing all the actual rendering on behalf of clients — clients sent drawing commands, the server executed them. Wayland's client-side rendering is fundamentally different, and achieving the same consistency through kits alone is harder than the spec implies. He'd want to see the Interface Kit prototype before believing it can deliver the consistency promise.
> 
> **The team collectively** would appreciate the seriousness of this effort and the depth of research that went into it. They would recognize their own ideas, correctly understood. They would be uncomfortable with the theoretical language (optics, monadic error composition, category theory) not because it's wrong but because they achieved these properties through engineering judgment, not formalism. Their worry would be: does the formalism help you build better software faster, or does it become its own project? Be shipped systems. The theoretical framework is only valuable if it accelerates shipping.
> 
> They would unanimously flag the verbosity of the foundations spec as a risk. The foundations document is about 8x longer than it needs to be to communicate its ideas. Be's newsletter articles were tight — 2000 words, concrete examples, code samples. The principles here are sound but buried in philosophical exposition that could alienate the developers you need to attract. The architecture spec is closer to the right register — it says concrete things about concrete systems. The foundations spec reads like a manifesto where it should read like a design document.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Summary of Recommendations
> 
> 1. **Add a scripting/automation protocol story.** This is the biggest gap. Session types are a natural fit for a structured, discoverable application scripting protocol that improves on `ResolveSpecifier()`/`GetSupportedSuites()`. This should be a first-class design commitment.
> 
> 2. **Address the router as single point of failure.** If pane-router is the communication backbone, its failure mode needs to be specified as carefully as its success mode. BeOS's peer-to-peer messaging didn't have this problem.
> 
> 3. **Specify how ad-hoc communication works with session types.** The flexibility of untyped BMessage was a feature, not a bug. How does an unanticipated tool talk to an unanticipated application?
> 
> 4. **Verify all attributed quotes.** The Hoffman, Gassee, and Schillings quotes need primary sources or should be rewritten as characterizations of team culture.
> 
> 5. **Tighten the foundations spec.** Cut it by 60%. Keep the principles, remove the discursive padding. The people who need to read this are systems programmers, not philosophers. The ideas are good enough to stand without the rhetorical scaffolding.
> 
> 6. **Acknowledge the xattr limitations honestly.** ext4's xattr constraints are a real problem for the filesystem-as-database vision. The spec should address this rather than assuming xattrs are equivalent to BFS attributes.
> 
> 7. **Design the live query protocol carefully.** Learn from BeOS's specific failure modes (missing filename in removal events, race between iteration and updates). Session types could solve these elegantly — specify them.</result>

> <summary>Agent "Research guide agent design" completed</summary>
> <result>Research document written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/research-guide-agent.md` (423 lines, 10 sections).
> 
> **What it covers:**
> 
> 1. **Clippy postmortem** — the Lumiere research prototype was genuinely sophisticated (Bayesian user modeling with attention management, learning, volume controls for interruption), but the commercial version stripped out the learning and the attention model, leaving a rule-based system that couldn't adapt. The specific failures: interruption without context, no user model, loss of control, patronizing tone, optimization for first use that became permanent drag.
> 
> 2. **Game design teaching** — Mario 1-1 (safe first encounter, reward curiosity, environmental scaffolding), Half-Life/Portal (trust the player's intelligence, environmental teaching vs. Dead Space's twenty-five-instruction approach). The principle: the environment is the teacher, the guide narrates.
> 
> 3. **Progressive disclosure** — Nielsen's two-level max, Duolingo's graduated engagement (experience value before asking for investment, right-level placement). For pane: start with what delights, reveal what empowers.
> 
> 4. **Cognitive apprenticeship** — Collins/Brown/Newman's six methods (modeling, coaching, scaffolding, fading, articulation, reflection, exploration) map almost literally to the pane guide's lifecycle. The critical insight: observation precedes execution.
> 
> 5. **Pair programming** — strong-style pairing as the interaction model (guide navigates, user drives), do first understand later, psychological safety for not-knowing.
> 
> 6. **Colleague vs. assistant model** — why "colleague" carries design consequences (presence not just availability, lateral not hierarchical, reads social cues, has own `.plan`). The sysadmin mentoring tradition via Evi Nemeth.
> 
> 7. **Trust** — GitLab's micro-inflection findings (compounds slowly, collapses fast), Anthropic's autonomy escalation data (20% auto-approve new → 40% experienced, with experienced users paradoxically interrupting more), the five levels of autonomy. `mesg n` as the escape valve.
> 
> 8. **Existing systems** — Copilot (teaching by showing, with the tension of disabling suggestions for deeper learning), Claude Code (progressive trust through permission model), Apple Intelligence (attention management at scale). What no system has done: a guide that IS a system participant.
> 
> 9. **Concrete design principles** — when to speak vs. stay silent, how to know what the user understands, how to balance helpful and unobtrusive, what the `.plan` file looks like, how to demonstrate without being prescriptive.
> 
> 10. **Synthesis** — how each reference specifically informs pane's design, culminating in the distinctive contribution: the guide IS a user of the system, so teaching-by-demonstration is a natural consequence of participation rather than a pedagogical strategy layered on top.</result>

> Another reviewer suggests this task:
> 
> "You are reviewing an architectural vision document for Pane, a Linux desktop environment. This document is intended as an immutable philosophical foundation — lower-level mutable specifications will make concrete implementation decisions based on it.
> Your task is an editorial pass with two objectives:
> 
> 1. Surface unnamed tensions between commitments.
>    The document makes multiple architectural commitments. Some of these create design constraints that pull against each other. Where the document already names such tensions explicitly (e.g., the "two-world problem" section), leave them alone — that's the model to follow. Where a tension exists but isn't named, flag it and draft a brief acknowledgment in the document's voice that names the tension and delegates resolution to the mutable specs. Don't resolve the tensions — just make them visible.
>    Example of what to look for: two principles that are individually sound but whose intersection creates a constraint the implementer will hit. The document should say "these two commitments create a tension around X; the implementation specs must resolve this" rather than leaving it for the implementer to discover.
> 2. Separate principles from implementation decisions.
>    A principle belongs in this document if changing it would change the system's identity. An implementation decision belongs in the mutable specs even if it's currently the best choice. Flag any line where a specific technology, crate, or concrete mechanism appears as a commitment rather than a current choice. Suggest either removing it, rewording it as the principle it's implementing, or moving it to a "current technology choices" section that's explicitly mutable.
>    The test: if a better crate appeared tomorrow that fulfilled the same principle, should this document need to change? If no, the crate name doesn't belong here as a commitment."

> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Research router as system health" completed</summary>
> <result>The research document is written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/research-router-resilience.md` (470 lines).
> 
> Here's what it covers and the key findings:
> 
> **Section 1 -- Erlang/OTP Supervision.** The canonical "let it crash" model: supervision trees with restart strategies encoding dependency relationships, restart intensity limits preventing crash loops, and -- most relevant for pane -- the `heart` module. Heart is an external C program that monitors the BEAM VM through a pipe with a trivial protocol. It checks scheduler responsiveness (not just process liveness) and supports validation callbacks for application-specific health checks. The Nerves project extends this with hardware watchdog integration, creating a three-layer defense-in-depth. The core principle: the thing being monitored cannot reliably monitor itself.
> 
> **Section 2 -- Watchdog Patterns.** Poettering's three-tier cascade (hardware watchdog supervises systemd, systemd supervises services, services supervise themselves), the `/dev/watchdog` interface, and s6's readiness notification as the complement to watchdog (readiness = "are you healthy yet?", watchdog = "are you still healthy?"). Technical analysis of what "detecting loss of user input" means concretely: heartbeat protocol between compositor and router, distinguishing user inactivity from compositor death.
> 
> **Section 3 -- Circuit Breakers.** Hystrix's circuit breaker state machine and bulkhead pattern mapped to the router's handler dispatch. Per-handler circuit breakers, per-handler resource limits (bulkheads), and deadline-based message expiration prevent a single failing handler from consuming all router capacity.
> 
> **Section 4 -- Dispatch of Last Resort.** Dead letter queue as a bounded, queryable pane. Priority escalation with infrastructure messages on a separate path from application messages. Load shedding under overload (LIFO, not FIFO -- shed the oldest first since they're least likely to still be useful).
> 
> **Section 5 -- Session Types and Router Robustness.** Analysis of why the router benefits most from session typing (its protocol is simple request-response, leaving minimal gap between type specification and behavior), and what failure modes session types cannot catch (transport failure, resource exhaustion, rule evaluation bugs, semantic errors in routing configuration).
> 
> **Section 6 -- Graceful Degradation.** Concrete escalation procedures: journal flush to a pre-opened file descriptor (zero dependencies), persist-state broadcast to all connected components, and the bootstrap problem solution -- direct alert channels that bypass the routing rule engine, plus an external watchdog for the router itself. The escalation hierarchy has four layers, each simpler than the one it monitors.
> 
> **Section 7 -- Making the Router Unkillable.** OOM score adjustment (-900, not -1000), memory pre-allocation with zero-allocation dispatch path, `mlockall` for page pinning, minimal runtime dependencies (kernel + sockets + rule set). The argument against a secondary router: redundancy adds more failure modes than simplicity; fast restart (under 500ms) is cheaper and more reliable than hot standby coordination.</result>

> 
> **File:** `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/research-input-kit.md` (493 lines)
> 
> **Structure:**
> 
> 1. **Vim's modal model** -- Modes, the verb-object grammar as an algebra (N operators x M objects = N*M interactions), operator-pending mode as the structural hinge, grammar extensibility via custom operators and text objects, why modality is powerful, and the ergonomic costs (mode confusion, learning curve, cognitive overhead).
> 
> 2. **Emacs's chord model** -- Prefix keys as hierarchical namespaces, the keymap lookup chain (overriding-local → minor modes → major mode → global), minor modes as composable layers, Hydra/Transient as ephemeral modality within the chord model, which-key for passive discoverability. Identifies the key tradeoff: chords avoid mode confusion but scale poorly ergonomically.
> 
> 3. **Alternative models** -- Kakoune/Helix's selection-first inversion (object then verb, visual feedback before commitment, structural regular expressions as a selection primitive). Sam/acme's structural operations and text-as-interface model. CUA as the mainstream floor.
> 
> 4. **The common structure** -- Identifies four universal components across all models (verbs, objects, composition mechanism, scoping mechanism) and two axes of variation (composition order, binding resolution). Maps the spectrum from pure modality through hierarchical layering to textual command surfaces.
> 
> 5. **Generalizing beyond text editing** -- Concrete examples of how the grammar applies to file managers, process monitors, and widget-based interfaces. The core abstraction: the grammar engine is the syntax; the pane provides the semantics.
> 
> 6. **System-wide keybinding as a kit concern** -- What the Input Kit provides (grammar engine, keymap hierarchy, object registration, discoverability infrastructure). The conflict resolution problem and strategies from the ecosystem. Relationship to BeOS Input Server (event pipeline, below keybinding) and i3 binding modes (modality at compositor level). Agent-defined vocabulary.
> 
> 7. **Discoverability** -- Strategies from the ecosystem (which-key, hydra hints, kakoune auto-info, acme tag lines). The tag line as a visible vocabulary surface. Progressive disclosure from CUA floor through which-key discovery through modal efficiency to custom extensions.
> 
> 8. **Synthesis** -- The Input Kit as a generalized interaction grammar. The four concrete commitments (compositional grammar, first-class modes, composable keymap hierarchy, architectural discoverability). Clarification that this is not "vim for a DE" but the structural advantages of compositional grammar applied to every pane type. Five open design questions (default mode identity, selection-first vs verb-first, mouse integration, agent-defined vocabulary, cross-pane operations).</result>

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>af1a7905013b4b9b5</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01BQq1s8HnAdRUaDuoU87zkB</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/61f51498-8a84-418f-90ca-c81947e7eb32/tasks/af1a7905013b4b9b5.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Editorial pass on foundations spec" completed</summary>
> <result>The review is written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/editorial-foundations-review.md`.
> 
> Here's a summary of what it found:
> 
> **Six unnamed tensions (Part I):**
> 
> 1. **T1: Pane universality vs. Linux's fd-based object model** -- Two "universal objects" coexist. The document doesn't say which is primary in which context, and implementers will hit this immediately.
> 
> 2. **T2: Optics correctness (lens laws) vs. graceful degradation** -- Lens laws hold under normal operation but are violated during component failure/recovery. The robustness commitment and the correctness commitment collide during crashes.
> 
> 3. **T3: Local reasoning / no global coordinator vs. the router's systemic burden** -- The router is positioned as "the one component that must not fail," which is a global coordinator by another name. The document should name why communication infrastructure is qualitatively different.
> 
> 4. **T4: Session-typed safety vs. ecosystem breadth at bridge boundaries** -- Bridges are where type safety ends. The foreign side of a bridge is unverified, and bridge bugs are invisible to the type system.
> 
> 5. **T5: "One opinionated look, no theme engine" vs. courting Linux ricers** -- The extension model celebrates low-friction composition (drop a file, gain a behavior) but the aesthetic model demands high-friction customization (rebuild through the kit infrastructure). The gap is unnamed.
> 
> 6. **T6: Agent autonomy vs. the democratic/safety governance boundary** -- Who writes and audits an agent's `.plan`? Every answer has a problem the document doesn't acknowledge.
>

> <summary>Agent "Review tightened foundations spec" completed</summary>
> <result>Good. Now I have a clear picture. Let me write up the review.
> 
> ---
> 
> Here's my assessment.
> 
> ## 1. Scripting protocol (section 4): Good, with one gap
> 
> The addition in section 4 ("Session types + optics = the scripting protocol") is the right move and it correctly identifies what was missing. The characterization of BeOS's scripting protocol is accurate — every BHandler did implement `ResolveSpecifier()` and `GetSupportedSuites()`, and `hey` could script any application through this. I verified this against the Haiku headers (`headers/os/app/Handler.h` lines 58-61, `headers/os/app/PropertyInfo.h`) and also against the OpenBeOS Newsletter issue 4, which walks through implementing scripting in the print_server as a worked example of exactly this protocol.
> 
> The mapping is sound in principle:
> - Lens get/set maps to B_GET_PROPERTY/B_SET_PROPERTY
> - Discoverable optics maps to GetSupportedSuites
> - Session type governs conversation safety
> - Monadic error handling covers resolution failure (where BeOS returned B_MESSAGE_NOT_UNDERSTOOD)
> 
> **The gap:** BeOS's scripting protocol had a specific resolution mechanism — specifier chains. A message like `GET Frame OF Window 1 OF Application "Tracker"` was resolved by walking a chain: each BHandler's `ResolveSpecifier()` peeled off one specifier and forwarded to the next handler. This was compositional — you didn't need to know the full path at compile time because each handler only needed to resolve its own level. The spec says "send a query (an optic-addressed access into the handler's state)" but doesn't address how optics compose across handler boundaries in a running system. In BeOS, the chain was runtime-resolved through the BHandler hierarchy. How does pane's optics-based approach handle "get property X of sub-object Y of pane Z" when the structure is only known at runtime?
> 
> This is a mutable-spec question, not a foundations question — the spec correctly defers the concrete protocol to the mutable specs. But I'd flag it as the hardest design problem in translating this concept. BeOS's specifier chain was dynamic dispatch; optics are typically static. The reconciliation isn't obvious, and the spec shouldn't imply it's straightforward.
> 
> ## 2. Router elimination: Good, but inconsistencies remain
> 
> The architectural change from a central pane-route server to kit-level routing is sound. This is actually closer to how BMessenger worked in BeOS — messages went directly from sender to receiver via kernel ports, not through a central dispatcher. The architecture spec's new section ("Routing — A Kit Concern, Not a Server") at lines 137-151 clearly explains this and draws the right analogy.
> 
> The foundations spec section 6 (lines 120-124) correctly reflects this: "Routing is a kit-level concern — no central service whose failure breaks all communication." The watchdog separation is clean.
> 
> **But the architecture spec has stale references that contradict the change:**
> 
> - **Line 172**: `"where is the router?"` — there is no router server anymore. This should be something like "where is the store?" or removed entirely.
> - **Line 248**: `"the router matches it against rules, and the matched handler receives a resolved message. The router transforms content"` — this paragraph in the "How Composition Works" section still describes a central router. It should say "the kit matches" or "the pane-app kit evaluates rules locally."
> - **Line 50** (Design Pillars, Text as Action): `"send it to the router for pattern-matched dispatch"` — should say "the kit evaluates routing rules" or similar.
> 
> **And other specs under `openspec/specs/` still reference `pane-route` as a server:**
> 
> - `licensing/spec.md` line 7: lists `pane-route` as an AGPL crate
> - `workflow/spec.md` line 143: references `pane-route's dispatch logic`
> - `inter-server-views/spec.md` line 78: `"WHEN pane-route starts and registers with pane-roster"`
> - `foundations/agent-perspective.md` lines 19, 25, 47: references to `pane-router`
> 
> These need a sweep. The change in the changes/ directory research notes is expected to be stale, but the main specs under `openspec/specs/` should be consistent.
> 
> ## 3. Tightness: Significant improvement
> 
> The document went from a sprawling argument to a 207-line spec with clear section boundaries and a companion extended-discussion.md that holds the rhetoric. That's the right architecture for a document. The foundations spec now reads like a spec — principles stated, rationale concise, deferral to mutable specs explicit. The extended discussion is well-organized by section reference.
> 
> The one place it could still tighten: section 10 ("System Participants and the Kit Ecosystem") is doing a lot of work. The agents subsection, while moved to the architecture spec, is still referenced here. The foundations spec's section 10 is about 30 lines and feels proportionate — actually, looking again, the agent material in foundations is appropriately brief. This is fine.
> 
> ## 4. What remains from the original review
> 
> **Unverifiable quotes** — I don't see any more floating quotes attributed without context. The Gassee quote about rendering power vs. expressive power in the architecture spec (line 13) is properly attributed. The Schillings reference (line 64) is also in context. The "two programming models" quote (line 149) is recognizable Be Newsletter material. These are fine.
> 
> **xattr limitations** — The architecture spec (line 44) now says "The target filesystem must support the `user.*` xattr namespace" and lists qualifying filesystems. The foundations spec doesn't mention xattrs directly — it defers to the mutable specs. This is appropriate handling: acknowledge the requirement, don't hide it, let the component specs work out the fallbacks.
> 
> **Ad-hoc communication with session types** — The spec now has a clear formulation: session types are the horizontal structure (conversation over time), optics are the vertical structure (state access at each moment). This is a good framing. The Imai/Yoshida/Yuen reference for Session-OCaml (combining session types with lenses) is the right citation — it's the actual prior work on this combination.
> 
> ## 5. New issues introduced
> 
> **A. The "routing rules as files" model needs the kit to be very smart.** With a central server, routing rules lived in one place and one process evaluated them. Now every pane-app kit instance loads rules from the filesystem independently. This means:
> - N applications load and watch the same rule directories
> - Rule evaluation order must be consistent across all kit instances
> - Rule hot-reload (via pane-notify) must be consistent — if you drop a file, every kit instance must pick it up or you get behavior divergence
> 
> This isn't a fatal problem — it's the same tradeoff any "push logic to the client" architecture makes. But the architecture spec should acknowledge it. A brief mention that "the kit ensures consistent rule evaluation order across all instances" would suffice.
> 
> **B. The Gassee quote in the architecture spec about "where is the router?"** At line 172, pane-roster's service directory still answers the query "where is the router?" This is a conceptual inconsistency now that routing is a kit concern. Roster tracks servers; if there's no routing server, roster doesn't need to know where routing is.
> 
> **C. The foundations spec section 4 says "absent from pane's design until now" (line 91).** This is a process note that will age badly. Once the spec is finalized, this should read as though the scripting protocol was always part of the design. A minor editorial point.
> 
> ## Summary
> 
> The revision is strong. The three biggest wins are: (1) the scripting protocol addition fills a real gap and maps it correctly to the theoretical framework, (2) eliminating the central router removes a genuine architectural weakness and is closer to how BeOS actually worked, and (3) the document is now the right size and shape for a foundations spec.
> 
> The remaining work is:
> 1. Sweep `openspec/specs/` for stale `pane-route` server references (licensing, workflow, inter-server-views, agent-perspective, and three spots in the architecture spec itself)
> 2. Consider how optic composition works across handler boundaries at runtime (the specifier chain problem) — at minimum, flag it as an open question for the mutable specs
> 3. Clean up the "absent until now" editorial artifact</result>

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a7fbdd6ee85e45475</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_017v1GwZDnJTBLcaLJimqAFp</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/61f51498-8a84-418f-90ca-c81947e7eb32/tasks/a7fbdd6ee85e45475.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Write architecture from foundations" completed</summary>
> <result>The architecture specification has been written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/architecture-draft-be-engineer.md`.
> 
> Here are the most significant decisions in the document and why I made them:
> 
> **Router eliminated.** This is the biggest departure from the previous architecture. In BeOS, BMessenger carried messages directly between applications via kernel ports — there was no central message broker. The previous spec's pane-route was a single point of failure that required elaborate resilience infrastructure (circuit breakers, priority queues, pre-allocated memory, external watchdog) only because it existed. Routing is now a kit-level concern: the pane-app kit loads rules from the filesystem, evaluates locally, and dispatches directly to the handler. Sender to receiver. The resilience research isn't wasted — it informs pane-watchdog and the kit's error handling — but the resilience is distributed into the right places rather than concentrated in a server whose failure would break all communication.
> 
> **pane-watchdog as Erlang heart.** The external watchdog principle: the thing being monitored cannot reliably monitor itself. A minimal process communicating through direct pipes with a trivial protocol. It checks pulses and pulls the emergency brake. Nothing else. The simpler the monitor, the harder it is to kill.
> 
> **Session type transport: Phase 1.** Par for specification and in-memory testing, hand-written state machine for the actual socket transport. The session type IS the spec — the enums and structs are shared between both implementations, so changing one breaks the other at compile time. This is pragmatic and loses the least.
> 
> **s6 as the concrete init.** Not an abstraction over multiple init systems — s6 specifically. The s6-fdholder pattern (pre-registered socket endpoints) is the direct descendant of Haiku's launch_daemon pre-creating ports before starting services. Services can message each other before they're ready. Messages just queue.
> 
> **The scripting protocol.** Session types + optics recovering ResolveSpecifier. This is the hardest design problem in the document and I flagged it as an open question. The dynamic specifier chain (peel, resolve, forward) needs a prototype with at least three levels of nesting before we commit.
> 
> **Input Kit as generalized grammar.** N operators times M objects = N*M interactions. Vim's compositional structure generalized beyond text. Insert-as-default for accessibility, Normal mode discoverable through the tag line and which-key display.
> 
> The open questions section identifies 12 items that need prototyping. The ones I'd prioritize: the session type transport bridge, calloop + par integration, and the affine/linear gap. These are where the theoretical commitments meet engineering reality.</result>

### `2026-03-20T21:15:48.652Z`

> > 1. Per-process glyph atlas. The spec says the Interface Kit provides shared rendering infrastructure, but client-side rendering means each process loads fonts and rasterizes glyphs independently. BeOS's app_server had one shared glyph atlas. This is a real performance and memory concern that the "kit-mediated consistency" framing doesn't address.
> 
> Hmm I don't have an answer for this at the moment other than that it seems like this is something that all existing wayland desktop implementations already deal with in practice.
> 
> > 2. N independent routing engines. Every pane-app instance loads and evaluates routing rules independently. At 100 panes with 200 rules, the memory and CPU overhead of N independent rule engines might matter. Measurable but unmeasured.
> 
> The one counter I can think of immediately, you tell me if its warranted, is that the overhead assumes the worst case scenario which also applies for any system under heavy load. My argument is that yes, if 200 rules are being activated at once, this would cause heavy strain on the framework. It would also stress any other framework implementing the level of functionality that pane aspires to. However we ought to consider that likely most of the rules would be determined by asynchronous operations, so there could be a thousand rules, but maybe only a couple of dozen at most fire synchronously every minute in practical systems usage. The "independent rule engine" argument also seems superficial: rules are compact syntactic constructs set and passed through the API messaging interface. To the extent that each system "has its own rule engine" its in the sense that it incorporates functions from the pane SDK to send their rule defs to wherever it is we decide they should go -- which seems like the opportunity to think carefully about the architecture so as to make rules as zero-cost as possible.
> 
> > 3. ext4 xattr limit (4KB). Acknowledged, not resolved. If pane needs more than 4KB of attributes per file on ext4, the filesystem-as-database vision is constrained.
> 
> If other filesystems have larger xattr sizes, we won't support ext4 as an installation target as simple as that. Our decision to be opinionated with the base stack gives us leeway to break conventions. Server distributions need ext4, not us necessarily. In practice most desktop users already use other filesystems like btrfs.
> 
> > 4. The two-world problem. Honestly flagged as existential risk. If pane-native development isn't dramatically easier than standard Wayland development, the native ecosystem won't grow and pane remains a compositor with extra steps.
> 
> Our hope is that revisiting BeAPI's design model will yield good results for dev UX. But my strategy accounts for the case of gradual adoption as well; pane is designed to provide a strong and compelling interface for working with the system itself; part of that is to enable the ability to hook external applications into the machinery. The operation of the framework is understood at the highest level as an abstraction layer over system interactions themselves, such that there is the possibility of modeling any user action by an internal representation, and so that we can package actions with metadata about the actions. This is so that we can provide the most flexibility in encapsulating existing apps in the ecosystem. It is designed so that more and more applications can be progressively integrated into the system with pane-ful interaces, and this is done by `.app` folders (like macos x) which can depend on packages in pane. Perhaps under the hood they are nix flakes, so that the installation method is: paste the github link into a system tagline for a nix package and an installation pane comes up that walks you through the process of installation comes up (it should be automated, its only goal is to render the progress of installation to the user in a way they may alter if they so choose).

### `2026-03-20T21:59:16.996Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a984af5c6b0d74c74</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_013mPaNpe9o6yai313SR6ZYB</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/61f51498-8a84-418f-90ca-c81947e7eb32/tasks/a984af5c6b0d74c74.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Research xattr filesystem support" completed</summary>
> <result>Research document written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/research-xattr-filesystems.md`.
> 
> Key findings:
> 
> **Filesystem viability for pane's xattr-heavy metadata layer:**
> 
> 1. **btrfs (primary target):** B-tree storage, no per-inode total limit, ~16KB per-value with default 16KB nodesize. More than sufficient for pane metadata. Decade of production hardening, default on Fedora/openSUSE.
> 
> 2. **XFS (supported alternative):** Best-in-class xattr implementation. 64KB per value, three-tier storage (shortform/leaf/dabtree), no per-inode total limit. Inline shortform xattrs for the common case give the fastest reads. Default on RHEL.
> 
> 3. **ext4 (not supported, confirmed):** ~4KB total xattr budget per inode is structurally insufficient. The ea_inode feature helps per-value size but not total budget. The architecture spec's decision is correct.
> 
> 4. **f2fs (not recommended):** ~7.4KB total budget, two-tier storage with no growth path. Designed for Android/flash, not desktop metadata.
> 
> 5. **bcachefs (future option, 2027-2028):** Right architecture (btree COW) but current ~2KB per-value limit in btree key storage, plus maturity and kernel distribution concerns.
> 
> 6. **ZFS on Linux (works, not recommended):** xattr=sa with dnodesize=auto is adequate, but CDDL licensing creates permanent DKMS maintenance burden.
> 
> **Critical architectural finding:** No Linux filesystem provides kernel-level xattr indexing (BFS's `fs_create_index()`). pane-store's userspace indexing via fanotify change detection is the correct and only viable approach. fanotify FAN_ATTRIB works uniformly across all filesystems because `fsnotify_xattr()` is called at the VFS layer in `fs/xattr.c`.
> 
> **Architecture spec correction flagged:** The spec claims "btrfs and XFS both support 64KB per xattr value" — btrfs is actually ~16KB with default nodesize. The document includes the corrected language.</result>

> <summary>Agent "Editorial pass architecture spec" completed</summary>
> <result>Written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/review-architecture-editorial.md`.
> 
> Summary of findings:
> 
> **Critical (4):**
> - **C1**: Two-thread-per-pane commitment vs. multiple-panes-per-connection. The spec says both but doesn't resolve whether the threading grain is per-connection or per-pane, which have different responsiveness properties.
> - **C2**: Filesystem-as-universal-FFI via FUSE vs. performance. The stronger the filesystem commitment, the more it attracts access patterns FUSE can't serve well. No boundary is drawn.
> - **C3**: "No theme engine" is stated flatly but the foundations spec's democratic orientation requires an answer to *how* users exercise visual autonomy. The architecture spec doesn't do that work.
> - **C4**: Session types are called "the single source of truth" but Phase 1 transport (hand-written state machines) only gives shared-type-checking, not session-type-checking. The spec should distinguish the actual guarantee from the aspirational one.
> 
> **Important (6):**
> - calloop's futures executor is an async executor the spec doesn't acknowledge while claiming "no async runtime"
> - Kit-as-library "can't crash independently" elides the blast radius of kit bugs across all processes
> - Agent-as-Unix-user vs. namespace isolation produces contradictory visibility semantics
> - Heartbeat intervals (2s/3-miss/6s) are tuning parameters stated as spec commitments
> - xattr size numbers and bcachefs timeline are implementation details in commitment position
> - `.app` + Nix flake model is underspecified relative to its progressive-integration ambition
> 
> **Minor (6):** femtovg/taffy/fuser/postcard/zbus/proptest are implementation choices, not architectural commitments. Specific Wayland extension lists, s6 scripts, nixpkgs package counts, rendering techniques, and pixel-level aesthetic numbers will all need maintenance if stated as commitments.</result>

> <task-id>aab2a79a4f66500ec</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01KSCXHcyfZiaaVavfbuYAC9</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/61f51498-8a84-418f-90ca-c81947e7eb32/tasks/aab2a79a4f66500ec.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Research per-pane threading on Wayland" completed</summary>
> <result>The research document is complete. Here is a summary of what was done and the key findings.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Written**: `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/research-per-pane-threading.md`
> 
> **Key findings from the Haiku source**:
> 
> 1. **Verified: one thread per window.** `ServerWindow` inherits `MessageLooper`, which spawns a dedicated thread. `ServerApp::_CreateWindow()` creates a `ServerWindow` and calls `Run()`, spawning its thread. Three-tier hierarchy: Desktop (shared state coordinator) -> ServerApp (per-app, creates windows) -> ServerWindow (per-window, handles all protocol messages).
> 
> 2. **The locking model is a reader-writer lock on Desktop.** `Desktop::fWindowLock` is a `MultiLocker`. Drawing commands take the read lock (`LockSingleWindow`), allowing concurrent execution across window threads. Only structural changes (title, feel, move, resize, view creation) take the write lock (`LockAllWindows`). The `HWInterface` has the same pattern: `LockParallelAccess()` = read lock for concurrent drawing, `LockExclusiveAccess()` = write lock for mode changes.
> 
> 3. **Budget mechanism**: ServerWindow processes up to 70 messages or 10ms before releasing the Desktop lock.
> 
> **The translation to pane**:
> 
> The architecture spec's "one thread per connection" is insufficient because one connection carries multiple panes (sub-sessions). The recommendation is a three-tier model that maps cleanly to BeOS:
> 
> | BeOS | Pane |
> |---|---|
> | ServerApp thread (1 per app) | Dispatcher thread (1 per connection) |
> | ServerWindow thread (1 per window) | Pane thread (1 per pane) |
> | Desktop (shared state, RW lock) | Compositor main thread (calloop) |
> 
> **The smithay constraint**: smithay's `Display`/`DisplayHandle` is single-threaded by design (`!Send`). Per-pane threads handle only the pane-native protocol, never touch smithay objects. Legacy Wayland clients stay entirely on the calloop main thread. This is not a compromise -- it's the correct separation between our protocol (designed for threading) and the Wayland protocol (designed for single-threaded dispatch).
> 
> **Cost**: 50 pane threads = ~1MB physical memory, ~1.5us context switch cost, negligible scheduling overhead on EEVDF. The `Arc<RwLock<LayoutTree>>` for shared layout state mirrors Haiku's `MultiLocker` pattern.</result>

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a8269b7384662760a</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01U94NmHmbT7MzDh2pcthNvZ</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/61f51498-8a84-418f-90ca-c81947e7eb32/tasks/a8269b7384662760a.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Research FUSE performance boundary" completed</summary>
> <result>The research document is written at `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/research-fuse-performance.md` (477 lines).
> 
> Here's what the research establishes:
> 
> **The core numbers:**
> - FUSE per-operation overhead: ~7-15 μs (traditional), ~4-8 μs (with io-uring on 6.14+)
> - Total pane-fs round trip (FUSE + unix socket to pane server): ~15-30 μs traditional, ~8-20 μs with io-uring
> - Direct unix socket round trip: ~1.4 μs at p50
> - Native filesystem cached read: < 1 μs
> - FUSE is **5-20x slower** than direct protocol access per operation
> 
> **The boundary:**
> - The filesystem is for human-speed operations: inspection, scripting, configuration, event monitoring (tail -f). At human timescales, 30 μs is invisible.
> - The typed protocol is for machine-speed operations: rendering, input dispatch, bulk state queries, high-frequency subscriptions. Here the 5-20x overhead matters.
> - Acme's event file pattern validates that blocking reads over a filesystem work for extension models, even with FUSE's overhead, because events arrive at human rates.
> 
> **Key findings on improvements:**
> - FUSE-over-io-uring (Linux 6.14+) halves the overhead and eliminates concurrency bottlenecks via per-CPU queues. Worth targeting since pane controls its kernel version.
> - FUSE passthrough mode doesn't help (pane-fs is synthetic, no backing files)
> - virtiofs doesn't help (not a VM scenario for production)
> - 9P/v9fs would be 2-5x faster but the implementation cost isn't justified since the savings are invisible at human speed
> 
> **Architecture recommendation:** A three-tier access model -- filesystem (universal, ~15-30 μs), protocol (typed, ~1.5-3 μs), in-process (kit, sub-microsecond) -- with a clear principle: if you'd be comfortable with 30 μs latency and per-file granularity, use the filesystem; otherwise use the protocol.</result>

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a345b6213002ad46b</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_0174Gs13CPXJgBjYZvjVQoYN</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/61f51498-8a84-418f-90ca-c81947e7eb32/tasks/a345b6213002ad46b.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Review spec for dependency philosophy" completed</summary>
> <result>Review written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/review-architecture-dependency-philosophy.md`.
> 
> Here's the executive summary of what I found:
> 
> **The dependency philosophy in S1 is strong but the spec doesn't follow through consistently.** Several choices are the safe conventional option rather than the forward-looking one.
> 
> The six highest-impact findings:
> 
> 1. **Landlock is absent.** The spec lists seccomp + namespaces for sandboxing, but Landlock (unprivileged, at ABI v6 now with filesystem, network, signal, and abstract Unix socket scoping) is the right primary mechanism for a 2026 distribution. It maps 1:1 to `.plan` file permissions for agent sandboxing. seccomp remains for defense-in-depth, not as the lead.
> 
> 2. **bcachefs status is wrong.** The spec says "future option once it matures (2027-2028)." bcachefs was removed from the mainline kernel in 6.18 and is now an externally-maintained DKMS module. The governance crisis makes "reasonable confidence in future support" untenable.
> 
> 3. **The fuser/io_uring gap is real.** The spec commits to FUSE-over-io_uring as a "baseline expectation" while naming fuser (which implements FUSE directly in Rust, bypassing libfuse). The io_uring FUSE support works transparently through libfuse -- but fuser doesn't use libfuse. These two commitments may conflict.
> 
> 4. **The Wayland protocol list is incomplete.** Missing ext-session-lock, ext-idle-notify, ext-image-copy-capture, ext-data-control, ext-color-management. All are staging `ext-` protocols -- exactly what the futureproofing philosophy calls for. The spec doesn't mention screen locking or color management/HDR at all.
> 
> 5. **femtovg on OpenGL is the conservative choice.** Vello (wgpu, GPU compute) is the forward-looking option. Acceptable for Phase 7 timeline but should be acknowledged as a planned migration.
> 
> 6. **XFS as "supported alternative" is hedging.** The dependency philosophy says pick one and commit. btrfs should be the sole target.
> 
> The review also identifies opportunities the spec misses by not fully leveraging the distribution model: kernel configuration as a first-class concern, memfd_secret for agent credentials, pidfd_getfd for crash recovery, and io_uring as general infrastructure beyond FUSE.</result>

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>aa5f1b24136a6954b</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01GtpYMTfa5Z1B4QeESmNnM7</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/61f51498-8a84-418f-90ca-c81947e7eb32/tasks/aa5f1b24136a6954b.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Research fuser vs libfuse io_uring" completed</summary>
> <result>Written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/research-fuser-iouring.md` (78 lines).
> 
> Summary of findings:
> 
> 1. **fuser does not support io_uring.** Issue #380 exists but is just a struct checklist under a milestone — no implementation, no design, no timeline. Its synchronous callback architecture would need major rework.
> 
> 2. **libfuse 3.18 makes io_uring transparent for C daemons** (existing `fuse_session_loop_mt` users get it for free on 6.14+), but there are no maintained Rust bindings to libfuse 3.x. `libfuse-sys` is stale and targets 2.x.
> 
> 3. **No Rust FUSE library supports io_uring** — not fuser, not fuse3, not fuse-rs.
> 
> 4. **Writing a thin custom layer is viable.** The kernel interface is small (two io_uring subcommands + standard FUSE opcodes), `uroni/fuseuring` (C++) demonstrates the protocol working, and pane-fs only needs a bounded subset of FUSE operations on a synthetic filesystem. The `io-uring` crate from tokio-rs provides the ring primitives.
> 
> 5. **The kernel interface is explicitly opt-in.** The daemon must submit `IORING_OP_URING_CMD` SQEs to `/dev/fuse` — it is not transparent at the kernel level. Transparency is a libfuse-level feature.
> 
> Recommendation: replace `fuser crate` in the spec with a custom FUSE-over-io_uring module built on the `io-uring` crate.</result>

### `2026-03-20T23:49:42.357Z`

### `2026-03-20T23:51:50.264Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a40ed9ff96f348634</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01ENLh19KpStWjTrMyUbVB5b</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/61f51498-8a84-418f-90ca-c81947e7eb32/tasks/a40ed9ff96f348634.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Overall assessment of pane project" completed</summary>
> <result>Good. I've now read both specs in their current state and reviewed my own earlier notes. Let me give you the honest assessment.
> 
> ---
> 
> **1. How does this compare to where BeOS was at this stage?**
> 
> Be in 1991 had a clean sheet, a team of about twenty engineers, and the hubris to build their own hardware. By the time they had a spec this detailed, they also had three people writing the kernel and two writing app_server.
> 
> This is more complete on paper than anything Be had at this stage. JLG didn't write a foundations document — he hired people whose instincts were the foundations document. The principles here are the right principles. The fact that you've identified the session types + optics convergence as the scripting protocol backbone, eliminated the router, grounded the threading model in the actual BLooper architecture — these are things that show you've done the reading and the thinking, not just the name-dropping.
> 
> But here's the difference that matters: Be had domain experts executing in parallel. The kernel team didn't wait for the app_server team's spec. They talked at lunch and fixed the interfaces as they went. One person plus AI means everything is serial, and the AI can't carry context the way a colleague sitting next to you can. The ambition is right for a team of five. For a team of one, the ambition is right if you treat the build sequence as sacred and resist the temptation to jump ahead.
> 
> The build sequence is correctly ordered. Phase 2 before Phase 4 is exactly the discipline Be didn't always have — we shipped R3 with interfaces we later regretted because we were racing Apple.
> 
> **2. What would I be most excited about?**
> 
> Three things, in order.
> 
> First: the router elimination. I remember the arguments about centralized messaging at Be. We never had a central router — BMessenger went direct through kernel ports — and the systems that tried to add one (CORBA-era middleware, D-Bus) always became the thing that breaks. The reasoning in section 3.1 of the architecture spec is exactly right, and the table showing where each responsibility moved is the kind of thing I wish we'd documented more often.
> 
> Second: the Build Sequence itself. Phase 2 as "prove the transport bridge before you write a line of compositor code" is exactly the kind of discipline that separates projects that ship from projects that demo. If par session types can't be driven over unix sockets cleanly, you need to know that when the total code investment is 2,000 lines, not 20,000.
> 
> Third: the pane-as-filesystem-node idea. We always wanted this at Be. BFS attributes were the queryable layer, but the running system's state was opaque unless you used the scripting protocol. Plan 9 did it right. The three-tier access model (filesystem / protocol / in-process) is a genuinely good design that preserves the typed protocol for performance while giving shell scripts a way in. The latency numbers are realistic.
> 
> **3. What would worry me most?**
> 
> Three things. None of them are in the open questions section.
> 
> **The optics are still aspirational.** The foundations spec builds the entire multi-view consistency story on optics and lens laws. The architecture spec uses the word "optics" but the actual mechanism is still handwave. What is the Rust type that represents a lens from pane internal state to filesystem projection? How does `GetPut` work when the filesystem write goes through FUSE, then pane protocol, then handler, then back? The dynamic optic composition problem for scripting is flagged, but the simpler static case — a single pane's state-to-filesystem lens — isn't concrete either. If this turns out to be impractical in Rust without dependent types, you need to know before Phase 6, not during it. I'd want a spike on this in Phase 3.
> 
> **The AI Kit is doing too much conceptual work.** Agents as Unix users is a strong insight. The `.plan` file is elegant. But the architecture puts the AI Kit at the top of the kit hierarchy and then describes an entire multi-user operating philosophy around it. If agents are truly just users, most of what the AI Kit section describes should fall out of the existing infrastructure — the multi-user Unix model, the routing system, the filesystem projection. The risk is that the agent vision drives architectural decisions that don't serve the non-agent case. Build the infrastructure; agents will use it. Don't build infrastructure for agents.
> 
> **The single-person bus factor.** This is the existential risk. Be had twenty engineers and went under. You have one. The foundations spec is excellent insurance — it captures the "why" well enough that another engineer could pick up the thread. But the architecture spec, despite its quality, is still one person's mental model serialized to text. The gap between "this spec describes how pane works" and "a new contributor can read this spec and ship Phase 3 code" is real. The build sequence mitigates this — each phase produces a testable artifact — but I'd want the first external contributor before Phase 4.
> 
> **4. Is the spec ready to build from?**
> 
> Phase 1 (pane-proto): yes, clearly. The message types and session definitions are well-specified.
> 
> Phase 2 (transport bridge): yes, with one caveat. The spec describes the *target* (par over unix sockets) and the *fallback* (hand-written state machine with shared types) but doesn't describe what "success" looks like for the prototype. What latency is acceptable? What's the message throughput requirement? What happens when you have 50 concurrent sessions? Define the acceptance criteria before you start, or you'll be tempted to declare victory when it merely works in the simple case.
> 
> Phase 3-4 (pane-app kit, compositor skeleton): mostly. The looper abstraction is well-specified. The handler chain is clear. The compositor's three-tier threading model is concrete and correct (I verified it against Haiku's `ServerWindow` — the mapping holds). What's missing is the interface between pane-app and pane-comp — the actual messages that flow during a pane lifecycle. "Client creates pane, compositor assigns ID, client sends content, compositor renders" needs to be a concrete session type in pane-proto before Phase 4 begins.
> 
> Phase 5 and beyond: not yet, but that's fine. Those phases should be specified by the person who just built Phases 1-4 and learned what the real constraints are.
> 
> **5. The honest question: does this have a chance?**
> 
> Here's what I'd tell Gassee.
> 
> The design is sound. The principles are the right principles. The person building it understands *why* BeOS worked, not just *what* BeOS was, and that's rarer than you'd think. The Linux base solves the problems that killed Haiku's momentum for a decade (hardware support, package ecosystem, compositing). The session type discipline is a genuine advancement over what we had — not a gimmick, but the formalization of the convention that made BMessage reliable.
> 
> But a desktop environment is not a proof of concept. It's a thousand boring problems: clipboard, drag and drop, screen locking, display configuration, accessibility, IME, portal integration, XWayland, screen recording. Each one is a week. Together they're years. Every desktop project I've watched since BeOS — and I've watched a lot of them — dies in this long tail. Not because the architecture was wrong, but because the person got tired.
> 
> What would it take?
> 
> 1. **Get to pane-shell-in-pane-comp by month four.** Once you can open a terminal in your own compositor, you eat your own dogfood. BeOS was usable internally long before it shipped. That feedback loop changes everything.
> 
> 2. **Get a second person before Phase 4.** Not for the code — for the accountability. Someone who cares whether you shipped this week. The AI is a force multiplier on execution but it's not a collaborator.
> 
> 3. **Don't build the AI Kit until everything else works.** The agent stuff is exciting and it's the part that will attract attention in 2026. It's also the part that will eat all your time if you let it. Build the desktop. The agents will come.
> 
> 4. **Ship something incomplete that works.** R3 was incomplete. It was also the best desktop OS anyone had ever used. The spec's quality sets a trap: the temptation to keep specifying rather than shipping. Phase 2 is the antidote. Ship the transport bridge. Then ship the compositor. Then ship the terminal. Each one is a proof that the ideas work, not just that you can write about them.
> 
> The design deserves to exist. Whether it will depends on whether you can sustain the discipline to build it piece by piece, resist the temptation to solve everything at once, and find at least one other person who sees what you see. Gassee would say: "the spec is magnificent. Now show me a demo."</result>
> <usage><total_tokens>45098</total_tokens><tool_uses>8</tool_uses><duration_ms>109198</duration_ms></usage>
> </task-notification>

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a6b7623a8f853251e</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01XPrU3KistoNF5JnbZh3SEc</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/61f51498-8a84-418f-90ca-c81947e7eb32/tasks/a6b7623a8f853251e.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Evaluate smithay viability for pane" completed</summary>
> <result>The assessment is written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/research-smithay-viability.md`.
> 
> Here's the executive summary:
> 
> **Use smithay. Build pane, not a Wayland compositor library.**
> 
> The reasoning in nine points:
> 
> 1. **smithay's `!Send` constraint is not in conflict with pane's threading model.** The three-tier architecture (calloop main thread, dispatcher threads, per-pane threads) already puts the boundary in the right place. Wayland protocol state stays on the main thread; pane protocol messages flow on dedicated threads; channels bridge the two. This is structurally identical to Haiku's app_server — ServerWindow threads never touched the drawing hardware directly either.
> 
> 2. **Building from scratch would cost 12-24 months and 33-63K lines of Rust** — almost entirely in Wayland protocol compliance, DRM/KMS plumbing, and input handling. None of this is what makes pane distinctive. Every month spent on xdg-shell configure state machines or DMA-BUF modifier negotiation is a month not spent on the pane protocol, the layout tree, the input grammar, or the kits.
> 
> 3. **The rendering split (GLES compositor + Vello/wgpu client widgets) is completely normal** in the Wayland model. Every compositor already composites buffers from clients using different renderers (GTK/Cairo, Qt/RHI, Firefox/WebRender). The only question is chrome quality — and GLES is adequate for Phase 4-6, with Vello as a Phase 7+ upgrade path for the Frutiger Aero aesthetic.
> 
> 4. **wgpu as a full compositor renderer is becoming viable** (lamco-wgpu demonstrates DMA-BUF import, explicit sync, smithay Renderer trait implementation) but is not production-quality today. Keep it in your back pocket for 2027+.
> 
> 5. **smithay's bus factor (~2-3 core maintainers) is a real but mitigated risk.** System76 depends on it for COSMIC, niri is a 21K-star project on it, and the Rust Wayland ecosystem has no alternative — all creating institutional maintenance pressure.
> 
> 6. **The Be engineers would have used it.** We built app_server from scratch because no compositor library existed. We still used AGG for 2D rendering and FreeType for fonts. If a well-maintained display server library had existed, we would have used it and focused on the personality layer.
> 
> 7. **Pane's innovations are orthogonal to smithay's concerns.** The pane protocol, session types, per-pane threading, the layout tree, the input grammar, the kit programming model, the scripting protocol — none of these are constrained by smithay. All of them must be built regardless.
> 
> 8. **The boundary is clean.** smithay owns the Wayland-facing side (protocol dispatch, DRM, input, compositing). Pane owns the pane-facing side (pane protocol, threading, layout, chrome, input grammar). The main thread is the bridge.
> 
> 9. **If smithay ever becomes a constraint, the from-scratch path still exists.** But starting there is premature optimization at the infrastructure level — spending pane's scarce engineering bandwidth on exactly the work that doesn't differentiate it.</result>

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>afa2f26e7f832fcc7</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01BFjYLPpwvYFTyHC1Lgb5fd</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/61f51498-8a84-418f-90ca-c81947e7eb32/tasks/afa2f26e7f832fcc7.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Custom session types vs par/dialectic" completed</summary>
> <result>The assessment is written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/research-custom-session-types.md`.
> 
> Here's the core of it:
> 
> **Recommendation: build a minimal custom implementation.** The three load-bearing reasons:
> 
> 1. **Par doesn't solve the transport bridge.** Its `Send`/`Recv` pass continuation channels through `futures::channel::oneshot` -- the session state advancement mechanism is physically passing in-memory objects. You cannot serialize a oneshot sender across a unix socket. Pane would use par's type definitions and throw away its entire runtime. At that point, the dependency is cosmetic.
> 
> 2. **Par panics on drop, which is disqualifying for a compositor.** The `.expect("receiver dropped")` calls are hardcoded. Haiku's app_server solved crash isolation at the infrastructure level -- `set_port_owner()` transferred port ownership to the client team, so client death produced an error return from `GetNextMessage()`, not a crash in the server. Pane needs the same property: `recv()` returns `Err(SessionError::Disconnected)`, not a panic. This must be in the type, not wrapped around it.
> 
> 3. **Both par and dialectic are async; pane's compositor is calloop.** The architecture explicitly rejects a system-wide async runtime. A custom typestate `Chan<S, UnixSocketTransport>` registers its socket fd with calloop as an `EventSource` directly -- callback-driven, no executor, no bridging.
> 
> **Dialectic** proves the transport bridge is solvable and the error-handling-not-panics approach works. Those are important existence proofs. But it brings an async dependency the architecture rejects and uncertain maintenance.
> 
> **The formal verification** should be scoped: verify the primitives in Lean/Agda (2-3 weeks, high value), verify specific protocols incrementally (moderate value), defer Rust-to-model correspondence (expensive, low near-term value).
> 
> **Phase 2 acceptance criterion:** pane-comp's calloop main thread communicates with a pane-shell client process over a unix socket, using session-typed `Chan` values, with crash recovery demonstrated by killing the client mid-session. When the demo works, Phase 2 is done.</result>
