### `2026-03-21T00:07:57.966Z`

> [interrupted]

### `2026-03-21T00:26:09.317Z`

> I don't know if these deserves to be written somewhere, but I wanted to get your feedback (don't take offense to some of the language): there is an explicit strategy as far as my usage of AI to write pane. The basic conception is: spend careful time thinking about the protocol, the system itself in abstract terms, designing the architecture with explicit lessons drawn from the vetted design philosophy of the 90s systems engineering we reference. To rapidly prototype this system, we will use AI coding assistants. We leverage the fact that modularity and well chosen abstractions entail that the code surfaces for the most commonly used and critical infrastructural components will be small and well defined. This allows us to use AI coding assistants to rapidly prototype the system, and pieces can be independently verified, refactored, or outright rewritten as needed -- all that is important is that they faithfully implement the specification. The rapid prototyping will allow us to see which design decisions that sounded good initially are actually good in practice, and which ones need to be revised. The modularity and well chosen abstractions will allow us to make changes to the system without having to rewrite large swaths of code, and will allow us to quickly iterate on the design until we have a system that is robust, efficient, and easy to maintain. It will also allow us to get an early stage alpha release out the door much sooner. We are leveraging good design and type systems to constrain the margin of error of our systems. We also get a sense of how much of the resilience of the architecture is due to the design itself, or how much is due to the implementation. We can then use this information to make informed decisions about where to focus our efforts on improving the system. If we get our design right, an AI will be naturally led to make good implementation decisions.
> 
> There is a meta-layer to this hinted at above, which is to say: under the assumption that AI is a fair representation of the average human programmer, when time comes to refine the UX kits, we can use AI coders to study _how easy it is to program within our system_. In fact, this serves two roles: we get live testing of the ergonomics of our system by observation of written programs and observations about an agent's development process (how easy it was, whether intended semantics of the programs emerged sooner or later, concrete bottlenecks from bad API design, and so on). But also the 'ease of use' for agents is a critical component to get right, especially because our whole AI-kit-as-a-service strategy for helping users build pane functionality requires that the system be easy to use for agents. This is a critical part of our development process, and we will be tracking it closely as we iterate on the design.

This is the mechanism that actually populates `/etc/pane/` — without it, the spec describes the runtime behavior but not how the config files get there in the first place. > 
> **Problems found in the original spec:**
>

The architecture spec (§3, "Why no router server" and §4, pane-app kit) is clear: routing and plugin discovery are kit-level concerns — each process loads and watches its own directories through the pane-app kit using pane-notify. The rewrite makes this explicit.
> 
> 2. **Missing plugin types.** The original only covered translators, input methods, and plumber rules. Added:
>    - **Routing rules** (`~/.config/pane/route/rules/`) — the pane-app kit loads these for local evaluation, per architecture §4.
>    - **`.app` directories** (`~/.config/pane/apps/`) — application bundles, per architecture §13 (the two-world problem).
>    - Explicitly noted that agent `.plan` files are not plugins — they live in agent home directories and are discovered by pane-roster, not the plugin directory mechanism.
> 
> 3. **Translation Kit pattern absent.** The original mentioned translators but not the quality-based selection pattern that the architecture spec references in four places (§4 pane-roster, §4 pane-app, §4 pane-ai, §8 Composition Model). Added a full requirement with scenarios for quality-rated multi-handler selection and drop-in format extensibility.
> 
> 4. **xattr metadata without btrfs context.** The original mandated xattrs but didn't reference the btrfs-only commitment from architecture §pane-store and §13 (resolved open question). The rewrite ties the xattr requirement to btrfs explicitly and adds the pane-store indexing connection — plugins are queryable through pane-store, not just introspectable via `getfattr`.
> 
> **Also renamed** "plumber rules" to "routing rules" throughout, matching the architecture spec's terminology (the pane-app kit evaluates routing rules, not plumber rules — the plumbing metaphor was from Plan 9 but the architecture spec uses "routing" consistently).

> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Audit pane-notify spec" completed</summary>
> <result>Here is the assessment and what changed:
> 
> **Problems found in the original spec:**
> 
> 1. **calloop presented as the universal delivery mechanism.** The original spec said consumers "receive events through a calloop-compatible event source" and had a "Calloop integration" scenario. The architecture spec (sections 3 and 6) is explicit: calloop is compositor-only, other servers use `std::thread` + channels. pane-notify sits in the kit layer -- most consumers are looper-based servers or pane-app clients, not the compositor.
> 
> 2. **No looper integration.** The BLooper model (architecture spec section 3) says each server processes messages sequentially from a message queue. pane-notify events should arrive as messages in the looper, not as calloop callbacks. The original spec was silent on this.
> 
> 3. **FAN_ATTRIB not mentioned.** The architecture spec and the xattr research (`/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/research-xattr-filesystems.md`, line 155+) confirm FAN_ATTRIB is the specific kernel event mask. The original spec only said "attribute changes" without naming the mechanism. Since pane-notify is the abstraction layer that owns fanotify, this is its concern to specify.
> 
> 4. **Missing: FAN_ATTRIB's disambiguation limitation.** The kernel doesn't tell you which xattr changed -- chmod, chown, and utimes produce the same event. Consumers must re-read and diff. This is an important contract detail for pane-store.
> 
> 5. **Missing: capability requirements.** fanotify FAN_MARK_FILESYSTEM requires CAP_SYS_ADMIN (research-linux-stack.md, line 356). This constrains which consumers can request mount-wide watches.
> 
> 6. **No stale references** to removed components -- the original was too short to have accumulated any.
> 
> **What the rewrite does:**
> 
> - Splits the old "Calloop integration" scenario into two: a Looper integration scenario (the common case) and a Compositor integration scenario (the exception), with a note that calloop is compositor-scoped.
> - Adds an explicit requirement for FAN_ATTRIB as the xattr detection mechanism, including the VFS-level uniformity guarantee.
> - Adds a disambiguation scenario documenting that consumers must diff on Attrib events.
> - Adds a capability awareness requirement covering the CAP_SYS_ADMIN constraint and the unprivileged fallback.</result>

>

> - Hardcoded pixel values ("1px highlight edges," "3-4px radius") replaced with design token language. At fractional scales these pixel-locked values are meaningless. The spec now says "thin edge" and "standard control radius" as named tokens resolved by the kit per-output.
> - "cell grid pane" terminology eliminated throughout.
> 
> **Missing content added:**
> - **Kit-mediated aesthetic enforcement** -- new requirement establishing the chrome/body rendering split and how consistency is achieved architecturally (matching architecture spec §10 and §4).
> - **Design tokens** -- new requirement formalizing the token vocabulary, with three stated purposes: HiDPI correctness, constrained configurability, and rendering backend independence.
> - **GPU-accelerated rendering** -- new requirement acknowledging Vello as the rendering backend (matching architecture spec §11's technology choice and §4's pane-ui description).
> - **HiDPI/fractional-scale** -- integrated into the design tokens requirement with a dedicated scenario for fractional scaling, matching architecture spec §10's HiDPI section.
> - **Client-side rendering model** -- the chrome vs. body distinction from architecture spec §2 is now explicit in the aesthetic spec.
> - **Native vs. legacy visual coherence** scenario, reflecting the architecture spec's "two-world" awareness.
> 
> **What was kept intact:**
> - All six core design principles (Frutiger Aero, depth through lighting, selective translucency, warm palette, typography split with Inter/Monoid, no theme engine).
> - The opinionated-and-fixed commitment.
> - The `/etc/pane/comp/` configuration surface for constrained adjustments.

> - `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/specs/aesthetic/spec.md` (rewritten)

> <tool-use-id>toolu_017Y975zar1CvjxTWMKCTcid</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/61f51498-8a84-418f-90ca-c81947e7eb32/tasks/a30b2716e9a66a226.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Audit pane-compositor spec" completed</summary>
> <result>Done. Here's the assessment and what changed:
> 
> **Assessment of the old spec**: A 31-line stub with three basic requirements. It had no threading model, no protocol list, no rendering model distinction, no access tier description, no shared state/locking discussion, and calloop was presented as "the event loop" with no scoping. It wasn't actively wrong about centralized rendering (it only mentioned chrome), but it was so incomplete that it failed to establish the client-side body rendering model at all.
> 
> **What the rewrite covers** (at `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/specs/pane-compositor/spec.md`):
> 
> 1. **Rendering model** (SS1): Explicit client-side body rendering, compositor-side chrome rendering, the division of labor stated upfront. No ambiguity about who renders what.
> 
> 2. **Wayland protocol extensions** (SS2): Full ext- protocol list (ext-session-lock, ext-idle-notify, ext-image-copy-capture, ext-data-control, ext-color-management), compositor-specific protocols (layer-shell, xdg-decoration, wlr-output-management, input method), and the pane protocol.
> 
> 3. **Three-tier threading model** (SS3): Desktop/ServerApp/ServerWindow mapped to main thread/dispatcher/pane thread. calloop explicitly scoped to the main thread. Per-pane threads use std::thread + channels. The two-thread pattern (client-side looper + server-side pane thread) documented. Cost analysis preserved.
> 
> 4. **Shared state and locking** (SS4): RwLock on layout tree, mirroring Haiku's `Desktop::fWindowLock`. Read lock for concurrent geometry queries, write lock for structural mutations. Channel boundary between pane threads and main thread.
> 
> 5. **Three-tier access model** (SS7): Filesystem/Protocol/In-process tiers with latency figures, use cases, and the compositor's role as the protocol tier's primary server.
> 
> 6. **calloop scoping** (SS3, SS9 requirements): calloop explicitly confined to the main thread's Wayland/DRM/input polling. Stated as an implementation detail, not a system-wide pattern.
> 
> The original requirements (boot, chrome, shutdown) are preserved and expanded with new requirements for client-side rendering, per-pane threads, RwLock on layout tree, and calloop scoping — each with testable scenarios.</result>

> 
> **Removed (references to eliminated concepts):**
>

The architecture uses typed Rust enums where field access is compile-time verified by definition. Replaced with a **message type discipline** requirement focused on what actually matters: enum conventions and proptest serialization coverage.

### `2026-03-21T21:43:49.003Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>aa459c5cb93e46eb5</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01TkDodobZGqDNBkSbKDKs3s</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/61f51498-8a84-418f-90ca-c81947e7eb32/tasks/aa459c5cb93e46eb5.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Review updated foundations spec" completed</summary>
> <result>Good. I've read the full foundations spec and checked the manifesto for context. Here's my assessment.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Overall: this is a strong document.** It reads as a coherent statement of intent from someone who has genuinely thought about what these systems were and why they worked. An engineer reading this would know what pane stands for. That's the bar, and it clears it.
> 
> Now, the specifics.
> 
> ### 1. Coherence
> 
> The document flows well from sections 1-6. There's a clear arc: what pane is, the universal object, protocol discipline, optics as the consistency mechanism, concurrency model, error handling. These sections build on each other and the forward references (like §4, §6) work.
> 
> The seam is at sections 7-8. Section 7 (Infrastructure-First) shifts register from precise technical principles to something more essayistic. Section 8 (Protocol Harmonizes Distinct Interfaces) is the longest section and covers three distinct topics — protocol uniformity, bridge trust, and a substantial pitch about power users and novice users that belongs more in the manifesto than in a foundations spec. The document is strongest when it states a principle and grounds it in a concrete mechanism. It's weakest when it advocates.
> 
> Section 9 (Aesthetic Commitment) and 10 (System Participants) tighten back up. Section 10 in particular is excellent — the framing of agents as system users through Unix's multi-user model is the single most distinctive idea in the document.
> 
> ### 2. Buildable Clarity
> 
> Yes, mostly. Sections 2-6 give an engineer enough to work from. The pane-as-universal-object, the optics discipline, the BLooper-derived concurrency model, monadic error composition — these are concrete commitments with clear implications.
> 
> Two gaps:
> 
> - **Section 4's open design question** (static optics vs. runtime specifier resolution) is the most important unsolved problem in the spec, and it's acknowledged honestly. Good. But it's buried at the end of §4 as a paragraph. Given how much load-bearing work this does — it's where scripting, optics, and session types all converge — it deserves more prominence. An engineer would want to know this is the hard problem, not discover it in passing.
> 
> - **Section 6, last two paragraphs.** "We assess that one central reason uniformity enabled by consistent interfaces..." — this sentence is doing too much syntactic work and not enough semantic work. It's trying to state that uniform APIs reduce edge cases, which is a good point, but the sentence is nearly unreadable. The "Modularity as resilience" subsection is better but still somewhat vague about what "congruence with the Linux ecosystem's inherent modularity" means in practice.
> 
> ### 3. Contradictions and Ambiguities
> 
> No outright contradictions. Two ambiguities worth flagging:
> 
> - **§2 vs §8 on the fd seam.** Section 2 says "the mutable specs must establish conventions for which direction governs in each context." Section 8 says bridges translate at boundaries with a trust model per bridge. These are compatible but not quite the same issue. The fd ontology question (who's primary, pane or file?) is about internal consistency; the bridge trust question is about external boundaries. They're related but distinct, and conflating them would be a mistake downstream.
> 
> - **§10 on agents.** The spec says agents are users of the system, not applications. It also says their behavioral specification is "a human-readable, editable, version-controllable artifact." There's a quiet tension here: if the agent's behavior spec is in its home directory and editable by the agent's owner (or the agent itself?), the governance question isn't just about trust models — it's about whether agents can modify their own behavioral specifications. That's a fundamental question the spec gestures at but doesn't quite name.
> 
> ### 4. Voice
> 
> Sections 1-6 have a consistent voice: precise, grounded, technically confident. The BeOS references are specific enough to be credible without being nostalgic. The theory references (session types, optics, monadic composition) are used as tools, not displayed as credentials. This is good.
> 
> Sections 7 and especially 8 shift. The second half of §8 — "we wish to set a high bar in the full expectation that power users will leap over it" through "we seek to grow their ranks" — reads as manifesto material that wandered into the spec. The sentiment is fine. The placement is wrong. It's making an argument to an imagined skeptic about whether pane can serve both novices and power users. A foundations spec shouldn't be making arguments; it should be stating positions. The manifesto exists precisely for this kind of extended case-making.
> 
> Section 9 returns to the right register. "A computer interface should invite interaction rather than demand it, and indulge curiosity rather than evade it" — that's the spec voice at its best. Clear, committed, not arguing.
> 
> There are also a few typos: "represetation" (§6), "advaned" (§8), "curisoity" (§9), "response" where "respond" is meant (§6, "how we gracefully response to").
> 
> ### 5. The Tensions
> 
> These are the best part of the document. They read as honest acknowledgments, not hedging. Specifically:
> 
> - **Pane vs fd ontology** (§2): Correctly identified, correctly deferred. This is real.
> - **Optics vs crash recovery** (§4, though stated in §6): The acknowledgment that lens laws may be "temporarily violated" under failure is exactly right. This is the hardest practical problem and the spec doesn't pretend it's solved.
> - **Bridge trust** (§8): Named clearly, deferred to mutable specs. Right call.
> - **Aesthetic customization effort gap** (§9): Honestly stated. "Perhaps requiring the rebuild of components in the kit" is a real cost and the spec doesn't minimize it.
> - **.plan governance** (§10): The governance tension around agent behavioral specs is well-named but, as I noted above, slightly under-specified. The question of self-modification is the sharp edge.
> 
> **One tension that should be named but isn't:** the tension between the session type commitment (compile-time protocol verification, deadlock freedom as theorem) and the reality that pane sits on Linux where half the system speaks D-Bus, talks to PipeWire, and otherwise communicates through channels that session types can't reach. Section 3's "enforce design principles strictly at the API level for the most critical system processes, and weather the elements gracefully everywhere else" is the right instinct, but it deserves to be elevated to a named tension rather than buried in a paragraph. The boundary between the typed world and the untyped world is where most of pane's day-to-day engineering friction will live.
> 
> ### Summary
> 
> The document is solid. The theoretical framework (session types + optics + monadic errors) is well-motivated and the BeOS/Plan 9 grounding keeps it from feeling academic. The tensions are honest. The voice is mostly consistent with two sections (7, 8) that need tightening — move the advocacy to the manifesto, keep the principles here.
> 
> The single most impactful edit would be to trim §8's second half back to its core principle (the novice-to-power-user pipeline is architectural, not a UX veneer) and let the manifesto carry the extended argument. The second most impactful would be elevating the typed/untyped boundary tension to a named tension in §3.</result>

> 
> Here's the shape of what I found:
> 
> **6 critical issues** — things that would cause an implementer to build the wrong thing or hit a wall:
> - **C1** is the worst: the architecture spec says `par` for session types, the agent-perspective doc says custom typestate. These are incompatible implementations.
> - **C2**: Which rendering engine renders compositor chrome (GLES vs Vello) is ambiguous.
> - **C3-C6**: Four servers described in the architecture (pane-dbus, pane-watchdog, pane-roster, pane-store) have no downstream specs with requirements and scenarios. These are the components that make the ecosystem work — the store is how BFS attributes happen, the roster is how application lifecycle works, the D-Bus bridge is where the typed/untyped boundary lives.
> 
> **9 important issues** — things that should be fixed before building starts in earnest:
> - **I1** is the one I'd worry about most from a Be perspective: the scripting protocol (optics + session types) is identified as "one of BeOS's most important features" but never becomes concrete in any spec. ResolveSpecifier/GetSupportedSuites was what made every BeOS application automatable. If this stays permanently aspirational, pane loses one of the core things that made Be great.
> - **I2**: "routing" is overloaded across content routing, AI model routing, and the eliminated router. Needs disambiguation.
> - The daily driver claim at Phase 5 is misleading — no Firefox, no Chromium, no Electron apps until Phase 8 when legacy Wayland lands.
> 
> **10 minor/editorial items** including numbering collisions, undefined terms, font inconsistencies, and the observation that the foundations spec claims "one communication model" but the architecture necessarily has two (pane protocol + Wayland).
> 
> The named tensions from the foundations are tracked against the architecture and downstream specs. The main gap: bridge trust and the typed/untyped boundary are named in the foundations but have no downstream spec addressing them concretely.</result>

> <summary>Agent "Assess and refactor Rust codebase" completed</summary>
> <result>The proposal is written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/proposal-codebase-refactor.md`.
> 
> Here's the high-level picture:
> 
> **The codebase is roughly half stale.** pane-proto has good bones — the wire framing, input types, tag types, and color palette are solid and stay as-is. But about half the code (by line count and by conceptual weight) was built for an architecture the spec supersedes: a runtime state machine instead of session types, a central router with verb+attrs messaging, and a compositor-rendered-body model instead of client-side rendering.
> 
> pane-comp is a demo, not a foundation. Only the glyph atlas concept carries forward.
> 
> **The plan has three beats:**
> 
> 1. **Phase 1 (half-day):** Delete the stale code from pane-proto. Remove `ProtocolState`, `Value`/`Compute` traits, `PaneMessage<T>` wrapper, the entire `server/` module (ServerVerb, TypedView, route, roster), `WidgetNode`/`WidgetEvent`, and the unused `optics` dependency. What remains is a clean, minimal wire types crate.
> 
> 2. **Phase 2 (the critical path):** Create `pane-session` — the custom session type crate with `Chan<S, Transport>`, five primitives, crash-safe error handling, unix socket transport, and calloop integration. Then add session-typed protocol definitions back to pane-proto.
> 
> 3. **Phase 4 onward:** Rewrite pane-comp from scratch on the session-typed foundation. The existing compositor code was never meant to be the real thing.
> 
> The hardest call in the proposal is the CellGrid question — the spec is right that client-side rendering is the correct architecture, but it delays the "pane-shell working" milestone because you need pane-ui's text engine first. I flag the pragmatic compromise (CellGrid as temporary bridge) but recommend against it becoming permanent.</result>

> Six proposed additions, each grounded in a specific Be engineering experience:
> 
> 1. **API deprecation protocol** -- the BMessage(BMessage*) lesson. Three-phase deprecation cycle with minimum two release cycles between introduction and removal. Treats trait bound expansions and enum variant changes as breaking.
> 
> 2. **Protocol evolution protocol** -- when session types change, all consumers update in the same commit. The key insight: wildcard match arms silently absorb new variants, defeating the compiler enforcement that justifies session types over Be's convention approach.
> 
> 3. **Performance regression detection** -- Schillings measured at microseconds, Hoffman identified 20x performance cliffs between sync and async. The workflow currently has no performance discipline at all. Proposes annotated hot paths with criterion benchmarks and regression thresholds.
> 
> 4. **Sync-in-hot-path detection** -- Hoffman's single most actionable insight: sync calls force cache flushes and round-trips. The review checklist should explicitly flag blocking calls in compositor main loop, per-pane threads, and looper threads. Generalizes the existing filesystem caching invariant to all blocking operations.
> 
> 5. **Wildcard match arm discipline** -- not from a specific newsletter article, but the structural consequence of replacing BMessage `what` codes with typed enums. A wildcard arm on a protocol enum is the exact regression back to Be's convention-based safety.
> 
> 6. **Two-failure stop rule** -- codifies the CLAUDE.md working contract rule into the workflow spec where agents will actually follow it. The benaphore story as positive exemplar: Schillings didn't keep making semaphores faster, he recognized the wrong approach.
> 
> The existing requirements are solid. The gaps are in what happens *around* correctness -- performance, evolution, and the specific ways typed safety gets accidentally undermined.</result>

### `2026-03-22T02:53:12.251Z`

> I reached out to the author of the par crate: 
> Your idea sounds really exciting! Sessions types may be the right choice, but I wanna push back a little bit (ironic as my language is all about them) 😄 Are you planning to have inter process communication typed and type checked? Because session types are really a type system feature more than anything else. Without end-to-end type checking of the whole system, they don't really add that much, I think. However, if it is end-to-end type checked, well, that's exciting!
> 
> But there's also another thing: I don't think we've really figured out the programming paradigm yet. At least, as it comes to pure session types, by which I mean no workarounds via potentially-deadlock-introducing other features, which you certainly can do in Rust alongside the par crate. What I mean is, yes, a lot is figured out, but for example the right way of doing nondeterminism isn't really. I've just recently figured a really solid approach to handling client-server (as agent entities) nondeterminism (feel free to read about it here: https://faiface.github.io/par-lang/nondeterminism/index.html), which a part of the whole nondeterminism space concerned with many(clients)-to-one(server) client-initiated communication. It's a powerful subset! But still a subset. Yet, my approach managed to subsume multiple previous approaches, which also quite well demonstrates that the area has a lot to figure out.
> 
> There are other kinds of nondeterminism too. For example, my approach is still not powerful enough (on its own) to implement a chat room where clients don't have to periodically fetch new messages, but are notified by the server. How to do that with session types? The correct approach is not yet clear.
> 
> What I mean is unless you're planning to go around session types for a bunch of applications (definitely possible with good taste), the paradigm itself should still be considered more research than truly ready for practice. Of course, I'm trying to change that with my language by making something we can actually put our hands on and figure this all out! But it's still some way to go.
> 
> What do you think? And again, very happy to hear from you 🙂

> <summary>Agent "Code review of pane-session" completed</summary>
> <result>Review written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/review-pane-session-code.md`.
> 
> Here's the executive summary:
> 
> **The core abstraction is sound.** `Chan<S, T>` correctly prevents invalid protocol sequences at compile time. The duality derivation is correct. Crash safety holds — every path where a peer can die produces `Err(SessionError::Disconnected)`, not a panic. The memory transport and unix transport both deliver this guarantee. This is the property that justified building custom over par, and it works.
> 
> **Two critical issues to fix now:**
> 
> 1. **Unbounded allocation on malicious length prefix** — `recv_raw` trusts the 4-byte length prefix and allocates accordingly. A `0xFFFFFFFF` prefix attempts a 4GB allocation. Needs a `MAX_MESSAGE_SIZE` guard. Files: `unix.rs:37`, `calloop.rs:109`.
> 
> 2. **The calloop `set_nonblocking` toggle affects the wrong fd** — `try_clone()` creates a new fd pointing to the same file description, so toggling O_NONBLOCK on the reader also affects the fd inside calloop's `Generic`. This can block the compositor main loop on partial messages. The fix is non-blocking reads with a per-source accumulation buffer. File: `calloop.rs:76-78`.
> 
> **The deeper architectural point:** the calloop integration currently bypasses session types entirely (raw bytes, manual deserialization). This is fine for Phase 2 proof of concept. The production path — per-pane threads with `Chan`, calloop only for connection accept and draw loop — is already in the spec and is the right design. The calloop rework should happen as part of Phase 3 threading, not as a standalone patch.
> 
> The bones are right. The primitives are solid. Fix the max-size guard, plan the calloop rework for Phase 3, and build on this.</result>

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a6ccd98e00b1b6682</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01URXogbv49UAMSt6qz8aqUu</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/b9e338f8-c13e-41f4-82d0-4e94d64f7b27/tasks/a6ccd98e00b1b6682.output</output-file>

> <summary>Agent "Reassess tensor as implicit property" completed</summary>
> <result>I need file write permission to create `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/be-engineer-tensor-equivalence.md`. Both the Write tool and Bash (via cat/heredoc) were denied. Could you grant write access so I can create the file?
> 
> In the meantime, here's the substance of what I found and what I'd put in it:
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Was BeOS honest about composition structure?
> 
> No. This was a real gap, and the Haiku source makes it concrete.
> 
> **Stack & Tile is the clearest case.** Haiku's `SATGroup` (in `src/servers/app/stackandtile/SATGroup.h`) maintains a rich internal model: windows grouped into `WindowArea` objects, areas sharing `Tab` constraints through a `LinearSpec` solver, spatial relationships tracked precisely. It's a real composition structure — a constraint-solving tiling model inside the app_server.
> 
> But none of it is visible through the scripting protocol. `BWindow::GetSupportedSuites()` (in `src/kits/interface/Window.cpp`, line 2679) reports `suite/vnd.Be-window` with properties: Frame, Title, Feel, Flags, Look, Hidden, Workspaces, Minimize, View, MenuBar, TabFrame. No "Group." No "Neighbors." No "TiledWith." Zero hits for `ResolveSpecifier` or `GetSupportedSuites` anywhere in the `stackandtile/` directory.
> 
> The `BWindowStack` API exists (`src/kits/interface/WindowStack.cpp`), but it's a private link-protocol API — it talks to app_server through `AS_TALK_TO_DESKTOP_LISTENER` with magic identifiers, completely bypassing the scripting protocol. You can't `hey` your way to discovering that two windows are tiled together.
> 
> The app_server knows. The user can see it on screen. But the scripting protocol — the very tool built for introspection — cannot discover the relationship. Two views disagree about what relationships exist.
> 
> **Workspaces are subtler.** BWindow exposes a `Workspaces` property (a bitmask), get/settable through `hey`. But workspace membership is a flat property on individual windows — there's no way to ask "which windows share workspace 3?" through the scripting protocol. You enumerate every window of every application and filter. The app_server's Desktop tracks this directly internally. The structure exists in one view but the other gives you only raw material to reconstruct it.
> 
> **BApplication scripting illustrates the hierarchy gap.** `BApplication::ResolveSpecifier()` resolves "Window" specifiers by index or name — `hey Tracker get Window [0]`. The scripting hierarchy is: application owns windows, windows own views. Spatial relationships between different applications' windows cross that boundary. The scripting protocol structurally cannot represent them.
> 
> ## What goes wrong
> 
> 1. **Automation breaks on layout.** Script moves window A; doesn't know B is tiled with it. Group breaks. The script did exactly what was asked — the system never told it about the relationship.
> 
> 2. **Recovery loses composition.** Stack & Tile groups existed only in volatile app_server memory. Crash recovery restored individual window positions; groups were gone. The filesystem knew nothing about them.
> 
> 3. **Tools can't compose.** Every tool that needs group awareness must build its own model by scraping individual properties. Each builds a slightly different model with slightly different bugs. Exactly the failure the scripting protocol was designed to prevent.
> 
> 4. **Tracker's per-directory view state.** `_trk/focusedView`, `_trk/recentQuery` — stored as file attributes, per-directory. Two windows showing the same directory in different sort orders: last one closed wins. Filesystem thinks there's one view state, runtime knows there are two.
> 
> ## Yes, it's an invariant, not a feature
> 
> The principle: **if two panes are in a composition relationship in any view, that relationship must be discoverable through every view.**
> 
> Not identically represented — the layout tree says "horizontal split," the filesystem says "sibling directories under a split node," the protocol says "sub-session containing two child sessions." The representations differ. The structure they encode must not.
> 
> This is GetPut/PutGet lifted from individual state to composition structure. Violations are either intentional-and-documented or bugs.
> 
> ## Constraints on the architecture
> 
> **pane-fs.** Must encode composition relationships, not just individual pane state. A split containing A and B is a directory containing `A/` and `B/` plus attributes encoding the split's own properties (orientation, ratio). The filesystem tree mirrors the layout tree's nesting.
> 
> **The protocol.** Must emit composition events, not just geometry events. "A was added to split S alongside B" — not just "A moved to (x,y)." Otherwise protocol consumers reconstruct relationships from geometry, which is lossy. The session type for a compound pane should be a product of its children's sessions.
> 
> **The layout tree.** Must not contain relationships inexpressible in the other views. If the compositor supports a composition primitive (tabbed stacking, etc.), it must have filesystem and protocol representations before it ships. No internal-only composition modes.
> 
> **The practical test.** For every composition operation: can a script (a) discover the relationship exists, (b) query its properties, (c) modify or dissolve it — all through the standard protocol, without special-casing? This is the test BeOS would have failed for Stack & Tile. Pane must pass it.
> 
> ## The deeper point
> 
> This is what distinguishes "everything is a pane" from "everything is a BHandler." BHandler made individual objects uniformly scriptable. Composition was ad hoc. Pane's commitment means relationships between panes are also pane-like: they have state, they're projected through views, the projections must be consistent. A split is not an implementation detail of the compositor — it's an object in the system, subject to the same discipline as the panes it composes.
> 
> That's harder than individual-object consistency. It means the filesystem, protocol, and compositor must agree not just on *what exists* but on *how things relate*. But it's the constraint that makes the system actually composable rather than just claiming to be.</result>

> Here's the content I've drafted — four insertion points, one paragraph each:
> 
> ---
> 
> # Compositional Equivalence — Spec Language
> 
> Concrete language to insert into the foundations and architecture specs, codifying the invariant: composition structure between panes is preserved across all views. Derived from the analysis in `be-engineer-tensor-equivalence.md`.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 1. Foundations §2 — The Pane as Universal Object
> 
> Insert after the paragraph beginning "Panes compose. Two panes viewed together form a compound structure...":
> 
> > The optics discipline (§4) applies to composition structure, not only to individual pane state. If two panes are in a composition relationship — a split, a stack, a group — that relationship is visible through every view: as spatial arrangement on screen, as directory structure under `/srv/pane/`, as sub-session nesting in the protocol, as a containment relation in the accessibility tree. The representations differ — each view expresses the relationship in its own terms — but the structural fact that the relationship exists is consistent across all of them. A relationship visible in one view and absent from another is a bug, not a feature gap.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 2. Foundations §4 — Multiple Views Through Optics
> 
> Insert after the "Composition" subsection ("Optics compose. The projection from internal state to protocol messages..."):
> 
> > The lens laws extend to composition structure. GetPut and PutGet govern not only the state of individual panes but the relationships between them. Read a composition relationship through the filesystem view and write it back unchanged — the layout tree is unchanged. Create a split through the protocol and read the filesystem — the split is there. Violations under failure are temporary, subject to the same recovery semantics as individual-state violations. Intentionally lossy projections — a view that elides nesting depth, a protocol that batches structural changes — are documented, not silent. The invariant is: every view agrees on what compositions exist, even when they represent those compositions differently.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 3. Architecture §2 — Pane Composition
> 
> Insert after the paragraph beginning "Panes compose spatially...":
> 
> > **Compositional equivalence.** The layout tree, pane-fs, and the pane protocol must encode composition relationships consistently. A split in the layout tree has a corresponding directory in pane-fs and emits structural events through the protocol. No composition primitive exists in one view without a representation in the others. Concretely: introducing a new composition mode (tabbed stacking, linked groups, transient overlays) requires filesystem and protocol representations before it ships. The test is automation-complete: for any composition relationship, a script must be able to discover that it exists, query its properties, and dissolve it through the standard protocol without special-case APIs.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 4. Architecture §2 — pane-fs
> 
> Insert after the existing pane-fs node structure (`tag`, `body`, `attrs/`, `ctl`):
> 
> > **Composition in the filesystem.** When panes are composed, pane-fs reflects the composition structure as directory nesting. A split containing panes A and B appears as a directory under `/srv/pane/` with its own `attrs/` (encoding orientation, ratio, and split type) and child entries `A/` and `B/`. Independent panes are top-level entries; composed panes are nested under their container. The filesystem tree mirrors the layout tree's nesting — not as a consequence of the compositional equivalence invariant, but as the filesystem's native expression of it. Reparenting a pane (moving it into or out of a split) changes its position in the filesystem hierarchy. Tools that walk `/srv/pane/` see composition structure directly; they do not need to reconstruct it from per-pane geometry attributes.
> 
> ---
> 
> A few notes on the drafting choices:
> 
> **Section 1** connects back to §4 explicitly so the reader knows this isn't a new principle — it's the optics discipline applied to a new subject (relationships, not just state). The last sentence ("a bug, not a feature gap") is deliberate. Haiku's Stack & Tile was treated as a feature gap. The spec needs to say it's a correctness violation.
> 
> **Section 2** stays parallel to the existing lens law language. GetPut/PutGet on composition structure, same recovery semantics, same "up to semantic equality" escape valve (here: "intentionally lossy projections"). The key addition is the documentation requirement — if a view is lossy about structure, that's a design decision that must be written down, not a silent elision.
> 
> **Section 3** is the implementation constraint. The "automation-complete" test is the operational version of the invariant — it tells an implementor exactly how to verify they haven't violated it. The clause about new composition modes is forward-looking: it prevents someone from adding tabbed stacking to the compositor without building the filesystem and protocol representations first.
> 
> **Section 4** makes the filesystem concrete. The critical choice here is that a split is a directory, not an attribute on its children. This means the layout tree's nesting is directly reflected in the directory hierarchy. The alternative — flat `/srv/pane/` with relational attributes — would make composition discoverable but not structurally visible. Directory nesting is stronger: `ls` shows you the composition structure. `find` traverses it. The filesystem IS the layout tree, projected.</result>

> 
> # Research: Multiparty Session Types for Pane
> 
> ## Part 1: Paper Analysis
> 
> ### What Maty Is
> 
> Maty (Fowler & Hu, OOPSLA) is the first actor language with static multiparty session typing where actors can participate in multiple sessions simultaneously. It resolves a fundamental tension: session types were designed for channel-based languages (easy to type, hard to distribute), while actor languages (Erlang, Akka) use a different model (easy to distribute, hard to type). Maty bridges this by enforcing session types through a flow-sensitive effect system and event-driven programming rather than explicit channels.
> 
> ### Event Actors: The Core Idea
> 
> An "event actor" participates in sessions without blocking receive operations:
> 
> 1. Actors **register** with **access points** to join sessions (providing a role and initialization callback).
> 2. Actors can **send** (non-blocking), but to **receive** they **suspend** -- installing a first-class **message handler** and yielding to the event loop.
> 3. The event loop multiplexes across all installed handlers, dispatching whichever session has a pending message.
> 
> The critical insight: **there is no receive primitive**. Reception is implicit through the event loop. This eliminates inter-session deadlocks -- a blocking receive on session A can't prevent servicing session B because the actor never blocks.
> 
> ### Multiparty vs. Binary
> 
> Pane currently has binary session types (`Send<A,S>`, `Recv<A,S>`, `End`, `Dual<S>`). MPSTs extend this to N parties via:
> 
> - **Global types**: bird's-eye protocol specification (`Client -> Server : { IDRequest . ... }`)
> - **Local types**: per-role projections with `&` (offer/receive) and `(+)` (select/send), annotated with target role
> - **Compliance**: replaces binary duality -- all local types must be compatible (safe + deadlock-free)
> - **Protocols**: maps from role names to local types
> 
> ### How Maty Handles Nondeterminism
> 
> This is the paper's critical contribution for pane's needs. Standard MPST spawns a process per session; inter-session coordination breaks deadlock freedom. Maty's solution: one actor participates in N sessions via the event loop. Internal state mediates between sessions.
> 
> The ID server example: one actor, N client sessions, shared `locked` flag. The branching type `Client (+) { IDResponse(Int).G, Unavailable().G }` says the server CHOOSES which branch. The type guarantees both parties agree on the continuation; the actor's internal logic determines which branch. **The types guarantee protocol shape, not business logic.**
> 
> ### Failure Handling
> 
> Maty extends to affine sessions with cascading failure:
> - `raise` cancels an actor and all its session endpoints
> - "Zapper threads" propagate cancellation: discard queued messages for dead roles, invoke failure continuations on waiting peers
> - `suspend` takes three arguments: handler, state, and **failure continuation** (invoked if the sender crashes)
> - `monitor(a, f)` installs crash callbacks (Erlang-style supervision)
> - Metatheory (preservation, progress, global progress) holds with failure
> 
> ### Key Theorems
> 
> - **Preservation**: well-typed configurations stay well-typed under reduction (no type-incorrect messages)
> - **Progress**: well-typed configs either reduce or are terminal (no stuck sessions)
> - **Global Progress**: if handlers terminate, every ongoing session eventually advances
> - All three hold with failure extension (sessions advance or get cancelled)
> 
> Condition: protocol **compliance** (safety + deadlock-freedom). Undecidable for async in general, but decidable via global type projection or bounded model checking.
> 
> ### Chat Server / "Server Pushes to Clients"
> 
> Decomposed into three protocols:
> 1. **ChatServer(C,S)**: client-registry binary protocol
> 2. **ChatSessionCtoR(C,R)**: client-to-room unidirectional
> 3. **ChatSessionRtoC(R,C)**: room-to-client unidirectional
> 
> Bidirectional room interaction is **two separate unidirectional protocols**. Broadcasting uses `ibecome` -- freeze current session, switch to each RtoC session to send, resume.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Part 2: Implications for Pane
> 
> ### The Compositor IS a Maty Actor
> 
> calloop + per-client SessionSource + shared layout state IS the Maty event actor model. The compositor main thread multiplexes across N client sessions. Its internal state (layout tree, focus tracking) determines what it sends to each client.
> 
> **Verdict: Pane needs multiparty thinking, not multiparty type constructors.** The N is dynamic, there's no meaningful N-party global type, and each client relationship is binary. Maty handles this exact case via repeated registration on access points and internal state mediation.
> 
> ### Active Phase Decomposition
> 
> The bidirectional compositor-client active phase should be decomposed following the chat server pattern:
> - **ClientToComp enum**: ContentUpdate, TagUpdate, RequestClose, ScriptingQuery
> - **CompToClient enum**: Resize, Focus, InputEvent, TagRoute, ScriptingResponse
> 
> Two unidirectional typed message streams on the same socket. Session types for structured phases (handshake, negotiation); typed enums for the repeating active phase.
> 
> ### Per-Pane Threads Map Exactly
> 
> Per-pane server-side threads are actors. The compositor main thread is the event loop. Message flow via channels. This is Maty's E-React: idle actor, installed handlers per session, event loop dispatches pending messages.
> 
> ### Crash Safety Already Matches
> 
> | Maty | Pane |
> |------|------|
> | `raise` | Thread panic + channel drop |
> | zapper thread | `SessionError::Disconnected` |
> | E-CancelH | `recv() -> Err(Disconnected)` |
> | `monitor(a,f)` | pane-watchdog heartbeat |
> | per-handler failure continuation | No equivalent (uniform `Result::Err`) |
> 
> The gap: Maty's per-state failure callbacks vs. pane's uniform Err. Defer upgrading unless per-state cleanup proves necessary.
> 
> ### Scripting Protocol: Binary Is Sufficient
> 
> Specifier chains use binary session types with handler switching (Maty's `become` pattern). Dynamic composition happens at the handler/optic level, not the session type level.
> 
> ### Compositional Equivalence
> 
> Use global types as a **specification language** for multi-client coordination (splits, focus handoff, drag). Implement as N binary sessions mediated by the compositor. Don't generate code from global types.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Part 3: Concrete Recommendations
> 
> ### Don't Move to Multiparty Types
> 
> Keep binary. Reasons: dynamic N, implementation complexity disproportionate to benefit, event-driven calloop already provides inter-session deadlock freedom (Maty's key insight).
> 
> ### Add Select/Branch (Branching)
> 
> ```rust
> pub struct Select<L, R>(PhantomData<(L, R)>);  // internal choice (sender picks)
> pub struct Branch<L, R>(PhantomData<(L, R)>);  // external choice (receiver handles both)
> 
> // Wire: 1-byte tag prefix
> // Dual: Select<L,R> <-> Branch<Dual<L>, Dual<R>>
> // N-ary: nest as Select<A, Select<B, Select<C, End>>>
> ```
> 
> ### Phase the Protocol
> 
> ```rust
> type Handshake = Send<ClientHello, Recv<ServerHello, Send<PaneConfig, Recv<PaneAck, Negotiation>>>>;
> type Negotiation = Send<Capabilities, Branch<Recv<Accepted, Send<Ready, End>>, Recv<Rejected, End>>>;
> // After End: switch to typed enum streams for active phase
> ```
> 
> ### Don't Add Recursive Session Types
> 
> Rust's type system makes `Rec`/`Var` impractical. Use typed message enums for repeating phases. This IS Maty's approach -- the handler dispatches on a typed enum, exhaustively.
> 
> ### Adopt/Adapt/Ignore Summary
> 
> | Concept | Decision | Why |
> |---------|----------|-----|
> | Event-driven actor model | **Adopt** | calloop IS this |
> | Global types | **Adapt** as spec language | Protocol documentation, not codegen |
> | Choose/Offer | **Adopt** | Add Select/Branch |
> | Protocol decomposition | **Adopt** | Unidirectional typed enums for active phase |
> | Multiparty type constructors | **Ignore** | Binary + event loop achieves same guarantees |
> | Compliance checking | **Ignore** | Protocols small enough to hand-verify |
> | Flow-sensitive effects | **Ignore** | Rust's typestate pattern suffices |
> | Affine sessions | **Already done** | `Err(Disconnected)` IS this |
> | Per-handler failure callbacks | **Defer** | Add if uniform Err proves insufficient |
> | Recursive types | **Ignore** | Typed enums for repetition |
> 
> ### Key Takeaway
> 
> Maty validates pane's existing architecture. The paper's strongest contribution for pane is the **decomposition strategy**: session types for structured phases, typed message enums with event-driven handlers for steady state. The multiparty coordination happens in the compositor's internal logic, exactly as Maty prescribes.
> 
> ---
>

> 
> # Maty Integration Plan
> 
> ## Part 1: Spec Language to Integrate
> 
> ### 1a. Architecture S7 — Protocol Design: Session Type Enrichments
> 
> Insert after the paragraph beginning "Pane uses a custom session type implementation" and before "The transport bridge":
> 
> ---
> 
> **Event actor validation.** Fowler and Hu's Maty (OOPSLA) proves that event-driven actors participating in multiple sessions through a single event loop are deadlock-free across sessions, provided handlers terminate. Pane's compositor main thread — a calloop event loop with per-client `SessionSource` registrations — is this model. Each connected client is a session. The calloop callback is Maty's handler. The compositor never blocks on a single client; it processes whichever session has a pending message and yields back to the event loop. This is not an analogy — it is the same architecture, discovered empirically by BeOS's app_server (one Desktop thread multiplexing N ServerWindows) and proven safe by Maty's metatheory (Theorems 2 and 5: progress and global progress, with and without failure). The calloop integration is the event actor model realized in Rust without the formal effect system.
> 
> What this validation covers: inter-session deadlock freedom. A compositor servicing 50 client sessions cannot deadlock on session A while session B has a pending message, because the event loop multiplexes — no handler blocks the loop. What it does not cover: intra-session correctness, which is the province of the binary session types on each client channel.
> 
> **Why binary session types suffice despite multiparty interactions.** The compositor mediates N client sessions, but N is dynamic (clients connect and disconnect at runtime) and there is no meaningful global type spanning all client interactions. Maty handles the same pattern — the paper's ID server and chat server both service N clients — not through N-party type constructors but through repeated binary session registration on a single access point, with the actor's internal state mediating cross-session coordination. Pane follows this pattern exactly: each compositor-client relationship is a binary session; the compositor's shared state (layout tree, focus tracking, tag index) coordinates between them. Multiparty thinking informs the protocol design — cross-client coordination scenarios (split creation, focus handoff, drag between panes) should be specified as global types for documentation and design validation. But the implementation remains binary sessions mediated by the compositor actor, which is what Maty prescribes for the dynamic-N case.
> 
> **Active phase decomposition.** The pane protocol has a nondeterminism problem: during the active phase, either side can send at any time. A strict session type like `Send<Content, Recv<Resize, ...>>` forces alternation that doesn't match reality. The resolution follows Maty's chat server pattern (Section 5.2): decompose into phases with different typing strategies.
> 
> - **Structured phases** (handshake, negotiation, teardown) use binary session types: `Send`/`Recv`/`Select`/`Branch`/`End`. The conversation has a fixed structure — message ordering matters, choices branch the protocol, and the session type captures this precisely.
> - **The active phase** uses typed message enums (`ClientToComp` / `CompToClient`) dispatched through event-driven handlers. Both sides send when they have something to send. The guarantee shifts from ordering (session types) to exhaustive handling (Rust's `match` over the enum). This is Maty's handler pattern: the actor receives a typed message and pattern-matches all variants.
> 
> This decomposition resolves the nondeterminism question that arises from attempting to type the full bidirectional active phase as a single binary session. Session types apply where they provide value (structural phases with fixed ordering); typed enums with exhaustive matching apply where session types fight the protocol's natural shape (the repeating bidirectional phase). The boundary is explicit: the session type's terminal state transitions the channel into the active phase, after which the transport carries tagged enum messages.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 1b. Architecture S7 — New Subsection: Protocol Phasing
> 
> New subsection within S7, after "Async by default" and before "Crash handling":
> 
> ---
> 
> **Protocol phasing.** The pane protocol decomposes into three phases, each with a typing strategy matched to its structure.
> 
> **Phase 1: Handshake (session-typed).** Client sends `ClientHello`; compositor responds with `ServerHello`; client sends capabilities; compositor selects accept or reject. This is a strict sequence with a branching choice — the natural domain of session types. The session type for the client side:
> 
> ```
> Send<ClientHello, Recv<ServerHello, Send<Capabilities, Branch<Recv<Accepted, ActiveTransition>, Recv<Rejected, End>>>>>
> ```
> 
> The compositor's side is the dual. `Select`/`Branch` encode the accept/reject choice: the compositor selects, the client offers both branches. The type guarantees exhaustive handling of both outcomes.
> 
> **Phase 2: Active (typed enums, event-driven).** After handshake, both sides communicate via typed message enums on the same socket, demultiplexed by a direction tag (1-byte prefix: 0x00 = client-to-compositor, 0x01 = compositor-to-client). Each direction has its own enum:
> 
> - `ClientToComp`: `ContentUpdate`, `TagUpdate`, `RequestClose`, `ScriptingQuery`, ...
> - `CompToClient`: `Resize`, `Focus`, `InputEvent`, `TagRoute`, `ScriptingResponse`, ...
> 
> Both sides send when ready. The compositor dispatches via calloop handler; the client dispatches via its looper thread. Rust's exhaustive `match` guarantees every message variant is handled. There is no session-type ordering constraint during this phase — the constraint is type safety of each individual message, not sequencing between messages.
> 
> The active phase enums are extensible via negotiated capabilities from Phase 1. A client that negotiated a capability (e.g., `CAP_DIRECT_SCANOUT`) may send enum variants that a basic client cannot. The handshake captures this: the `Accepted` payload includes the resolved capability set, and the active phase enum is the union — unknown variants from a future protocol version are handled by a catch-all that logs and ignores, preserving forward compatibility.
> 
> **Phase 3: Teardown (session-typed).** Graceful close or crash boundary. Graceful: the active phase enums include `RequestClose` (client-initiated) and `CloseAck` (compositor-confirmed). Upon receiving `CloseAck`, the client closes the socket. The compositor removes the pane's state and continues. Crash: the socket drops. The compositor's calloop source fires `SessionEvent::Disconnected`. Cleanup proceeds identically to graceful close minus the acknowledgment. This is Maty's affine session model: a session can be abandoned at any point, and the surviving party handles the cancellation through its failure path (`Err(SessionError::Disconnected)` in pane's case, the failure continuation in Maty's).
> 
> The three-phase model makes the typing boundary explicit. Session types govern the edges (handshake, teardown) where protocol structure is load-bearing. Typed enums govern the middle (active phase) where bidirectional freedom is load-bearing. Neither strategy is compromised by being forced into the other's domain.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 1c. Foundations S3 — Session Type Enrichment
> 
> Append to the paragraph ending "...the theoretical framework that lets the compiler verify it.":
> 
> ---
> 
> Fowler and Hu's event actor model (Maty, OOPSLA) provides a third leg: formal proof that an event-loop-based actor servicing multiple sessions is deadlock-free across those sessions, validating the architectural pattern BeOS's app_server proved empirically — a single event-driven coordinator multiplexing N client sessions, each independently session-typed.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 1d. Sources Addendum
> 
> ---
> 
> - **Fowler & Hu (OOPSLA)**: "Speak Now: Safe Actor Programming with Multiparty Session Types." Maty — the event actor model that proves deadlock freedom for event-loop-based actors participating in multiple sessions. Validates pane's calloop + per-client SessionSource architecture.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Part 2: pane-session Phase 3 Implementation Roadmap
> 
> ### 2.1 Select/Branch Type Constructors
> 
> Internal choice (`Select`) and external choice (`Branch`). Required for handshake branching and protocol negotiation.
> 
> Wire encoding: 1-byte tag prefix. Selector sends 0x00 (left) or 0x01 (right), then continues with the chosen branch's protocol. Offerer reads the tag and dispatches.
> 
> Types for `types.rs`:
> 
> ```rust
> /// Internal choice: this endpoint selects one of two continuations.
> /// In linear logic: A (+) B (plus — the selector decides).
> pub struct Select<L, R>(PhantomData<(L, R)>);
> 
> /// External choice: this endpoint receives the peer's selection.
> /// In linear logic: A & B (with — the offerer handles both).
> pub struct Branch<L, R>(PhantomData<(L, R)>);
> 
> /// Result of receiving a branch selection.
> pub enum BranchResult<L, R> {
>     Left(L),
>     Right(R),
> }
> ```
> 
> Chan impls:
> 
> ```rust
> impl<L, R, T: Transport> Chan<Select<L, R>, T> {
>     pub fn select_left(mut self) -> Result<Chan<L, T>, SessionError> {
>         self.transport.send_raw(&[0x00])?;
>         Ok(self.advance())
>     }
>     pub fn select_right(mut self) -> Result<Chan<R, T>, SessionError> {
>         self.transport.send_raw(&[0x01])?;
>         Ok(self.advance())
>     }
> }
> 
> impl<L, R, T: Transport> Chan<Branch<L, R>, T> {
>     pub fn offer(mut self) -> Result<BranchResult<Chan<L, T>, Chan<R, T>>, SessionError> {
>         let tag = self.transport.recv_raw()?;
>         match tag.as_slice() {
>             [0x00] => Ok(BranchResult::Left(self.advance())),
>             [0x01] => Ok(BranchResult::Right(self.advance())),
>             _ => Err(SessionError::Codec(postcard::Error::DeserializeBadEncoding)),
>         }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> Duality for `dual.rs`:
> 
> ```rust
> impl<L: HasDual, R: HasDual> HasDual for Select<L, R> {
>     type Dual = Branch<Dual<L>, Dual<R>>;
> }
> impl<L: HasDual, R: HasDual> HasDual for Branch<L, R> {
>     type Dual = Select<Dual<L>, Dual<R>>;
> }
> ```
> 
> N-ary choice via nesting: `Select<A, Select<B, Select<C, End>>>`. Add a flat N-ary macro later if the ergonomics demand it, not before.
> 
> Tests for `session_smoke.rs`: Select/Branch round-trip (memory + unix), duality compile-time check, invalid tag byte, disconnect-before-tag.
> 
> ### 2.2 `close_and_take` + Transport Extraction
> 
> After the session-typed handshake reaches `End`, the caller needs the underlying transport to continue with active-phase enum messaging on the same socket.
> 
> ```rust
> impl<T: Transport> Chan<End, T> {
>     /// Close the session and return the underlying transport.
>     /// Used for phase transitions: handshake -> active phase.
>     pub fn close_and_take(self) -> T { ... }
> }
> ```
> 
> Requires `ManuallyDrop<T>` wrapping the transport field inside `Chan`. `ManuallyDrop` is repr(transparent) — zero cost on the send/recv hot path. Implement `Drop` for `Chan` to handle panic-induced abandonment (drops the transport if not already taken). The existing `#[must_use]` on `Chan` makes abandonment a compiler warning.
> 
> Also add:
> 
> ```rust
> impl UnixTransport {
>     /// Extract the underlying stream for calloop registration.
>     pub fn into_stream(self) -> UnixStream { self.stream }
> }
> ```
> 
> ### 2.3 Transport Trait: `try_recv_raw`
> 
> Non-blocking receive, needed for calloop integration during active-phase polling.
> 
> ```rust
> pub trait Transport: Sized {
>     fn send_raw(&mut self, data: &[u8]) -> Result<(), SessionError>;
>     fn recv_raw(&mut self) -> Result<Vec<u8>, SessionError>;
> 
>     /// Non-blocking receive. Returns Ok(None) if no data available.
>     fn try_recv_raw(&mut self) -> Result<Option<Vec<u8>>, SessionError> {
>         Ok(None)
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> `UnixTransport`: non-blocking socket read. `MemoryTransport`: `mpsc::Receiver::try_recv`. No breaking changes to existing code.
> 
> ### 2.4 Calloop SessionSource: No Changes Needed
> 
> The current `SessionSource` (length-prefixed message accumulation, non-blocking reads, disconnect detection) is correct for active-phase dispatch. The Phase 3 change is in lifecycle, not in the source itself:
> 
> 1. Per-pane thread runs the session-typed handshake using `Chan<PaneHandshake, UnixTransport>` (blocking, on its own thread).
> 2. Handshake succeeds -> `close_and_take()` -> `into_stream()`.
> 3. Stream registered with calloop as `SessionSource` for the active phase.
> 4. Active-phase `ClientToComp` messages arrive via `SessionEvent::Message`, deserialized in the calloop handler.
> 
> Sessions are born on per-pane threads (session-typed) and graduate to calloop (enum-typed) after handshake. This matches the architecture spec's three-tier threading model and is exactly Maty's handler registration pattern.
> 
> ### 2.5 Task List
> 
> Ordered by dependency.
> 
> | # | Task | Effort | Depends on |

> 
> **Total: 3.5-5 days.** Tasks 1-3 are independent and can be done in parallel.
> 
> **Not in Phase 3 scope:**
> - Recursive session types (Rec/Var) — active phase uses typed enums
> - Per-state failure continuations — defer until `Err(Disconnected)` proves insufficient

> ---
> 
> # pane-session API Ergonomics Review
> 
> Assessed through the Be developer experience lens: "common things are easy to implement and the programming model is CLEAR" (Schillings, Newsletter #1-2).
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 1. Current API: The Ownership-Transfer Pattern
> 
> ### What works
> 
> The `Chan<S, T>` typestate is the right primitive. It does for protocol correctness what BLooper did for threading -- makes the right thing structural rather than opt-in. The consuming-self pattern (`send` takes `self`, returns `Chan<S2, T>`) is the Rust-idiomatic way to express linear resource transfer. A developer who knows Rust will read this and understand it.
> 
> The crash safety property -- `Err(SessionError::Disconnected)` instead of panic -- is correct and load-bearing. This is the property that made us build BMessenger-based locks instead of raw pointer locks: identity-safe failure. The error type is clean, the `From` impls for io::Error and postcard::Error compose well, and the BrokenPipe/ConnectionReset/UnexpectedEof mapping to Disconnected is the right call.
> 
> ### Where it gets painful
> 
> **Variable rebinding accumulates.** Look at the multi-step test (`/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/crates/pane-session/tests/session_smoke.rs` line 69):
> 
> ```rust
> let client = client.send("test-pane".to_string()).unwrap();
> let (id, client) = client.recv().unwrap();
> let client = client.send(vec![1u8, 2, 3]).unwrap();
> let (ack, client) = client.recv().unwrap();
> client.close();
> ```
> 
> Five steps, five `let client = ...` rebindings. For a test this is fine. For a real handshake protocol -- the one in the Maty integration plan has 4+ steps with branching -- this becomes a wall of rebinding that obscures the protocol's shape.
> 
> Compare to what a BLooper-based protocol looked like in BeOS:
> 
> ```cpp
> BMessage reply;
> messenger.SendMessage(&hello, &reply);
> // reply is populated, same variable, check reply.what
> ```
> 
> The BMessage pattern was mutable-in-place. You sent a message, got a reply in an output parameter, checked a status code. No ownership transfer, no rebinding. It was less safe -- nothing stopped you from using a stale reply -- but it was readable at a glance.
> 
> The question for pane: can we preserve the safety of ownership transfer while reducing the visual noise?
> 
> **Error handling doesn't compose over sequences.** Every step is `Result<Chan<S2, T>, SessionError>`, meaning every step needs `?` or `.unwrap()`. For a 5-step handshake, that's 5 potential early returns, each identical in handling (all are Disconnected in practice). The `?` operator helps but still fragments the protocol into individual fallible steps rather than expressing it as a unit.
> 
> **The recv tuple is awkward.** `let (value, chan) = chan.recv()?;` forces destructuring at every receive. The value comes first, the continuation second. This is the right type signature (it matches the linear logic) but it reads inside-out compared to the send direction where you pass the value and get back the continuation.
> 
> **Severity: moderate.** Sound and idiomatic Rust, but fails the Schillings test for protocols longer than 3 steps. For kit-level API, this needs helpers.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 2. Proposed Select/Branch
> 
> ### What works
> 
> The Maty integration plan's `Select<L, R>` / `Branch<L, R>` with `select_left()` / `select_right()` / `offer()` returning `BranchResult<Chan<L, T>, Chan<R, T>>` is clean for the binary case. The `offer()` method returning an enum that must be matched exhaustively gives you what BeOS's `MessageReceived` gave you with message codes, except the compiler enforces it rather than relying on a default clause.
> 
> ### Where it breaks: nested binary choice
> 
> The plan says to nest for N>2: `Select<A, Select<B, Select<C, End>>>`. Here's what 4-way negotiation looks like:
> 
> ```rust
> match chan.offer()? {
>     BranchResult::Left(chan) => { /* accepted */ }
>     BranchResult::Right(chan) => {
>         match chan.offer()? {
>             BranchResult::Left(chan) => { /* fallback */ }
>             BranchResult::Right(chan) => {
>                 match chan.offer()? {
>                     BranchResult::Left(chan) => { /* version mismatch */ }
>                     BranchResult::Right(chan) => { /* rejected */ }
>                 }
>             }
>         }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> This is Peano arithmetic of choice: encoding N as S(S(S(Z))). Theoretically sound and practically hostile. At N=4 the nesting is annoying. At N=6 it's unreadable.
> 
> The plan says "A macro for flat N-ary select!/offer! can be added later." I disagree with deferring it. The handshake is the first thing a pane client developer writes. If their first experience with session types is a nested staircase, they'll conclude the system is academic and work around it. The `offer!` macro is not a convenience -- it is the API.
> 
> **Severity: high for N >= 3.** Binary Select/Branch is fine as the primitive. The kit must provide flat N-ary choice.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 3. Three-Phase Transition
> 
> **Is `close_and_take()` natural or a hack?** Natural. The session type's job is done. Returning the transport is resource reclamation, like `BView::RemoveSelf()` returning the detached view for reuse.
> 
> **But the name is wrong.** `close_and_take` describes mechanism, not intent. It should be `finish` -- "the protocol is complete, give me what I need for the next phase." The Be naming convention was always intent: `PostMessage`, not `WriteToPortAndEnqueue`; `Lock`, not `AcquireSemaphoreOrAtomicIncrement`.
> 
> **The lifecycle gap.** The handshake-to-active transition is 7 manual steps with 3 type transitions (`Chan -> T -> UnixStream -> SessionSource`). In BeOS, the equivalent was invisible -- `BWindow` was created, its thread ran, it processed messages. One object, one lifetime. The pane equivalent needs a single function that collapses the transport unwrapping.
> 
> **Severity: moderate.** Correct mechanism, wrong name, missing lifecycle helper.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 4. What's Missing
> 
> Can a developer who's never seen session types write a correct pane client from the kit API alone? **No.** They'd need to know:
> 
> **a.** How to define a protocol type (the `type Foo = Send<A, Recv<B, End>>` pattern is shown in tests but not documented as a convention).
> 
> **b.** How to pair endpoints correctly. For unix sockets, `Chan::new(transport)` takes any session type -- if you manually write the wrong dual, the error surfaces at runtime as `SessionError::Codec`, not a compile error. (`/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/crates/pane-session/tests/unix_transport.rs` line 26 shows the manual dual annotation.)
> 
> **c.** How to handle errors across steps. `?` propagates `SessionError` but doesn't indicate which step failed. "Peer disconnected" after step 3 means something different from after step 1.
> 
> **d.** How to go from handshake to active phase. The three-phase transition doesn't exist in code yet.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 5. Concrete Recommendations
> 
> ### 5a. `request()` combinator for send-then-recv
> 
> ```rust
> impl<A, B, S, T> Chan<Send<A, Recv<B, S>>, T>
> where
>     A: Serialize,
>     B: DeserializeOwned,
>     T: Transport,
> {
>     pub fn request(self, value: A) -> Result<(B, Chan<S, T>), SessionError> {
>         let chan = self.send(value)?;
>         chan.recv()
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> Before: 4 lines, 2 rebindings. After: 1 line. This is `BMessenger::SendMessage(&msg, &reply)` -- recognize the dominant pattern and make it a single call.
> 
> ### 5b. `offer!` / `choice!` macros for flat N-ary branching
> 
> ```rust
> macro_rules! choice {
>     ($a:ty, $b:ty) => { Select<$a, $b> };
>     ($a:ty, $($rest:ty),+) => { Select<$a, choice!($($rest),+)> };
> }
> 
> macro_rules! offer {
>     ($chan:expr, {
>         $label_a:ident($a:ident) => $body_a:expr,
>         $label_b:ident($b:ident) => $body_b:expr $(,)?
>     }) => {
>         match $chan.offer()? {
>             BranchResult::Left($a) => $body_a,
>             BranchResult::Right($b) => $body_b,
>         }
>     };
>     ($chan:expr, {
>         $label_a:ident($a:ident) => $body_a:expr,
>         $($rest:tt)+
>     }) => {
>         match $chan.offer()? {
>             BranchResult::Left($a) => $body_a,
>             BranchResult::Right(__rest_chan) => {
>                 offer!(__rest_chan, { $($rest)+ })
>             },
>         }
>     };
> }
> ```
> 
> Turns nested staircases into flat, labeled match arms. The labels are documentation-only but make the protocol readable. Ship this with Select/Branch, not after.
> 
> ### 5c. Rename `close_and_take` to `finish`
> 
> ```rust
> impl<T: Transport> Chan<End, T> {
>     /// Complete the session and reclaim the transport for the next phase.
>     pub fn finish(self) -> T { self.transport }
> 
>     /// Close the session, dropping the transport.
>     pub fn close(self) { drop(self); }
> }
> ```
> 
> Two methods, two intents. `finish` = detach for reuse. `close` = done forever.
> 
> ### 5d. Phase transition helper
> 
> ```rust
> pub fn into_active_phase(
>     chan: Chan<End, UnixTransport>,
> ) -> io::Result<(SessionSource, UnixStream)> {
>     let transport = chan.finish();
>     let stream = transport.into_stream();
>     let writer = stream.try_clone()?;
>     let source = SessionSource::new(stream)?;
>     Ok((source, writer))
> }
> ```
> 
> Collapses 4 steps (`finish -> into_stream -> try_clone -> SessionSource::new`) into 1 call. This is the `BWindow::Run()` of session transitions.
> 
> ### 5e. Protocol type documentation convention
> 
> Every protocol type alias gets an ASCII sequence diagram:
> 
> ```rust
> /// ```text
> /// Client                          Compositor
> ///   |-- ClientHello ------------------>|
> ///   |<----------------- ServerHello ---|
> ///   |-- Capabilities ----------------->|
> ///   |<-- [Accept] ---- Accepted -------|  -> active phase
> ///   |<-- [Reject] ---- Rejected -------|  -> close
> /// ```
> pub type PaneHandshake = Send<ClientHello, Recv<ServerHello, ...>>;
> ```
> 
> This is the only way a developer who hasn't internalized session type notation can read the type. Adopt as convention now, enforce in review.
> 
> ### 5f. Typed constructors for unix sockets
> 
> ```rust
> pub fn unix_pair<S: HasDual>() -> io::Result<(
>     Chan<S, UnixTransport>,
>     Chan<Dual<S>, UnixTransport>,
> )> { ... }
> 
> pub fn accept_session<S>(listener: &UnixListener) -> io::Result<Chan<S, UnixTransport>> { ... }
> ```
> 
> Eliminates manual `UnixTransport::from_stream` + `Chan::new` assembly. Can't enforce cross-process duality at compile time (separate compilation units), but makes the intent clear.
> 
> ### 5g. `TrackedChan` for error context (deferrable)
> 
> Newtype wrapper that attaches step names to errors. Build it when handshake debugging hurts, not before.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Priority Table
> 
> | # | Recommendation | Severity | Effort | Ship with Phase 3? |
> |---|---------------|----------|--------|---------------------|
> | 1 | `offer!` / `choice!` macros | High | 1 day | Yes -- blocks readable handshake code |
> | 2 | `request()` combinator | Moderate | 0.5 day | Yes -- covers 80% pattern |
> | 3 | Rename to `finish` | Moderate | 0.5 day | Yes -- naming |
> | 4 | `into_active_phase` helper | Moderate | 0.5 day | Yes -- lifecycle |
> | 5 | `unix_pair` / `accept_session` | Moderate | 0.5 day | Yes -- entry points |
> | 6 | ASCII diagram convention | Low | 0.5 day | Yes -- convention |
> | 7 | `TrackedChan` | Low | 1 day | Defer |
> 
> Items 1-5 are the difference between a session type library and a session type kit. BeOS didn't ship BLooper and tell developers to compose their own message dispatch by calling `ReadMessageFromPort` and `DispatchMessage` manually. It shipped `Run()`. These helpers are the `Run()` of the session type system.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Appendix: The Be Standard Applied
> 
> The current primitives (Chan, Send, Recv, End) are like having BMessage but not BLooper. You have the data container and the type-level protocol, but not the runtime scaffolding that makes common patterns trivial. The recommendations above are the BLooper layer: `request()` is `SendMessage(&msg, &reply)`, `offer!` is the structured `MessageReceived` dispatch, `finish()` is the lifecycle transition, `into_active_phase` is `BWindow::Run()`.
> 
> The session type theory is solid. The crash safety is correct. What's missing is the layer that makes a kit developer productive without reading the theory. That's the layer Be always built -- and the one that made developers love the platform.</result>

> ## B. Stale References
> 
> ### B1. `par` crate references -- architecture/spec.md
> 
> The spec declares a custom session type implementation but retains par-era language:

> is there any point of the design spec that we might wish to incorporate our distinction between invariance of particular views into pane up to a contextually dependent notion of equivalence, which is the underlying principle of our notion of 'tensoring' panes (the tensor is coherently translated across contexts, displayed on screen vs via the fs interface) ? note that this entails another distinction: interactions ought to propagate coherently across all interfaces

### `2026-03-27T01:17:22.432Z`

> is there any point of the design spec that we might wish to incorporate our distinction between invariance of particular views into pane up to a contextually dependent notion of equivalence, which is the underlying principle of our notion of 'tensoring' panes (the tensor is coherently translated across contexts, displayed on screen vs via the fs interface) ? note that this entails another distinction: interactions ought to propagate coherently across all interfaces (hence: the effect needs to establish an appropriate presentation in each context, and these must be determined up to some notion of 'contextual equivalence'. More succinctly, we are asking that despite different *packaging* of the data of a pane, that these packagings are isomorphic

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a830a82b05587a626</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01LE3eb5Uav33q5S6hruyp6A</tool-use-id>
