> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Vet isomorphic packaging principle" completed</summary>
> <result>Written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/be-engineer-isomorphic-packaging.md`.
> 
> The short version of the vet:
> 
> The principle is correct and load-bearing. BeOS achieved genuine view coherence where it was designed in from the start (window properties, BFS attributes, query results) and violated it wherever features were added without projection coverage (Stack & Tile, replicants, workspace position details). The mechanism that made coherence work was node monitoring — a kernel-level pub/sub bus — and its failure modes (exhaustion, latency, lost notifications) are exactly the failure modes pane needs to design around.
> 
> The formalism ("functors preserving monoidal structure") is decorative for 90% of implementers but potentially useful for the one or two people designing the optics composition layer. The English restatement already in the spec ("The principle requires no formalism to apply") is the right escape valve — keep it prominent.
> 
> The principle's real teeth are in two places: it makes missing projections a bug rather than a backlog item, and it forces projection design at feature design time rather than as a follow-up. Both of these would have prevented the Stack & Tile failure mode, which is the canonical example of what goes wrong when you violate the invariant.</result>

### `2026-03-27T01:41:34.910Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a889a1948adca4611</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01EhCsxFmeXKEZVCGeQn4cjH</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/41ae307d-8864-4a1a-ab15-0c5308d4711f/tasks/a889a1948adca4611.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Analyze memx paper for pane AI kit" completed</summary>
> <result>Written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/research-memx-local-memory.md`.
> 
> The headline: MemX validates the right design choices but builds all its own infrastructure because it has no system to lean on. Pane has the system. The analysis identifies one genuine capability gap -- **vector similarity search in pane-store** -- and everything else composes from existing primitives.
> 
> The key takeaways worth flagging:
> 
> **MemX's strongest empirical finding for pane** is the granularity result: fact-level storage doubles retrieval quality over session-level, and this effect is larger than any pipeline-component change. This directly informs the memory consolidation protocol I recommend -- agents should extract atomic facts and write them as individual files with typed attributes, not store raw transcripts.
> 
> **The access/retrieval separation** is a small design decision with measurable ranking impact. It maps cleanly to separate pane xattrs and should be adopted.
> 
> **The rejection rule** (return empty rather than return wrong) should be a default, not an option. It's the memory-retrieval analogue of pane's broader correctness commitment.
> 
> **What MemX can't even see** -- multi-agent memory with graded access, memories as routable content, multi-view consistency, temporal queries via filesystem semantics -- these all fall out of pane's existing infrastructure commitments. The architecture is doing the right thing; it just needs the vector search extension to close the loop.</result>

### `2026-03-27T01:47:10.276Z`

> give be-engineer's analysis of the vector similarity and on what level it ought to be implemented and what design challenges of beos may potentially be clarified or simplified by this new infrastructure?

### `2026-03-27T01:54:05.409Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a871b44c2285c4527</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01UbvAz7zLPvBmMv8JK3f2Q5</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/41ae307d-8864-4a1a-ab15-0c5308d4711f/tasks/a871b44c2285c4527.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Vector similarity in pane-store design" completed</summary>
> <result>Written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/research-vector-similarity-design.md`.
> 
> Here are the key decisions:
> 
> **1. Vector similarity lives in pane-store itself.** Every other option breaks the BFS principle. Client-side recreates the siloed-database antipattern BFS eliminated. A separate service breaks BLooper's self-contained operational semantics and creates coordination hell for hybrid queries and live notifications. FUSE is a category error -- storage layer vs. query layer. pane-store maintains two indices (attribute B+ tree equivalent + HNSW graph) from the same fanotify event loop.
> 
> **2. Two query paths, one fusion point.** The query API is a builder with `.predicate()` and `.similar_to()` that can be used independently or composed. Three fusion modes: `predicate_first` (filter then rank, the default), `vector_first` (rank then filter), and `rrf` (reciprocal rank fusion, opt-in). String predicates preserved for scriptability -- `pane-store query --predicate 'type == "memory"' --similar-to <file>` from the shell, same as `query "MAIL:status == New"` on BeOS.
> 
> **3. The real wins from vector similarity are Tracker search and mail content search.** The Find panel's vocabulary gap (users not knowing attribute names/values) and BFS's inability to search file *content* (only attributes) were genuine limitations. Embeddings fix both. Application matching and MIME sniffing are moderate wins at best -- they work when deterministic, and vector similarity should be a fallback, not a replacement.
> 
> **4. Live queries use threshold, not top-k.** This is the crux of the design. I traced through `_EvaluateLiveUpdate()` in Haiku's QueryParser.h -- the BFS live query mechanism is O(1) per attribute change because predicate evaluation is *local* (depends only on the changed file). Top-k vector queries are *global* (depends on every file). Threshold-based vector queries restore locality: "is this file's similarity to the query vector above 0.6?" is evaluable per-file without considering other files. Top-k remains available for one-shot queries only.
> 
> **5. Embedding lifecycle follows the BFS attribute pattern.** Applications write embeddings as xattrs; pane-store indexes them. Model migration is gradual, not atomic -- analogous to BFS index creation only indexing files written *after* the index was created. The embedding xattr header carries dimensionality and model hash. Stale embeddings (wrong model) are excluded from the HNSW index but remain visible to predicate queries. Standard dimensionality: 1024.</result>

> </task-notification>

### `2026-03-27T02:03:11.132Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a389889dc69c7c661</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01Ni2yiiKBfuaKhRoAK5HsMu</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/41ae307d-8864-4a1a-ab15-0c5308d4711f/tasks/a389889dc69c7c661.output</output-file>

> <summary>Agent "Final sweep of entire spec corpus" completed</summary>
> <result>Written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/final-sweep-recommendations.md`.
> 
> Here's the short version of where I landed:
> 
> **Ready to build from: yes, with blockers.**
> 
> The spec corpus is strong. The foundations spec is genuinely excellent. The architecture spec covers the full system with real depth. The Phase 1-2 pipeline from spec to code has been validated.
> 
> **Four items that block Phase 3:**
> 
> 1. **pane-app kit API sketch** -- the spec describes what the kit does but not what its API looks like. There's no "hello pane" example. An implementer doesn't know whether to build a trait-based dispatch system or a closure-based one, whether handler chaining is explicit or implicit, what creating a pane looks like. This is the hardest design problem in the project and the spec hasn't touched it.
> 
> 2. **Routing rule file format** -- rules are "one file per rule" in well-known directories, but the file format, match language, and transformation model are entirely undefined. Phase 3 builds the evaluator.
> 
> 3. **pane-notify event delivery layering** -- circular dependency between pane-notify delivering to loopers and pane-app defining loopers. Needs explicit statement that pane-notify uses raw channels, pane-app wraps those into looper integration.
> 
> 4. **Phase 3 agent scope** -- "minimal agent infrastructure" is simultaneously too ambitious (Unix communication patterns over pane protocol) and too vague (no acceptance criteria). Risk of Phase 3 becoming an agent project instead of a desktop project.
> 
> **Three biggest Phase 3-5 risks:**
> 
> 1. The pane-app kit API emerging from implementation expedience instead of design intention
> 2. Tag line editing model crossing compositor/client boundary in unresolved ways
> 3. Agent infrastructure scope creep pulling effort before the desktop works
> 
> **The spec's final exam:** Write the 10-20 line "hello pane" example. If it's clear, the programming model is clear. If it's not, the spec isn't done yet.</result>

### `2026-03-27T02:06:24.359Z`

### `2026-03-27T02:14:59.726Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a5b8fe772b6d4b34e</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01BHtJpUB6sRnTaus2nja21X</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/41ae307d-8864-4a1a-ab15-0c5308d4711f/tasks/a5b8fe772b6d4b34e.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Design pane-app kit API from BeOS philosophy" completed</summary>
> <result>The spec is written. Here's what I designed and the reasoning behind the key decisions.
> 
> **The spec is at:** `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/specs/pane-app/spec.md`
> 
> ---
> 
> **The hello-pane example came first** -- 14 lines of application logic. Connect, create a pane with a tag, handle events, exit. No threads, no channels, no session types, no transport unwrapping visible to the developer. That's the bar.
> 
> **Five major departures from BeOS, each grounded in why BeOS worked and where it didn't:**
> 
> **1. App is not a Looper.** BApplication inherited BLooper, giving it its own thread and message queue. This caused constant confusion -- developers never knew whether a message should go to the app looper or a window looper. In pane, `App` is just the connection factory and roster registration. Per-pane loopers handle all message processing. The app thread calls `wait()` and blocks. Simpler, clearer.
> 
> **2. Flat enum replaces handler chaining.** BHandler's `SetNextHandler()` chain-of-responsibility was powerful for two things: scripting delegation (ResolveSpecifier walking the chain) and cross-cutting concerns. In pane, scripting goes through the filesystem (`/srv/pane/<id>/attrs/`), so handler chaining isn't needed for its original purpose. And Rust's exhaustive `match` is strictly stronger than the base-class-forwards-unknown pattern -- the compiler forces completeness. Unhandled message types were a real bug class in BeOS.
> 
> **3. Filesystem IS the scripting protocol.** This is the biggest departure. BeOS scripting required constructing BMessages with specifiers and sending them to specific BMessengers. Pane uses the FUSE mount at `/srv/pane/`. `cat /srv/pane/3/attrs/title` replaces `hey StyledEdit get Title of Window 0`. Any tool, any language, zero kit dependencies for the consumer. Plan 9's gift to the Be model.
> 
> **4. Routing rules as TOML files** in watched directories. This subsumes BeOS's MIME type association, BRoster launch dispatch, and Translation Kit quality-based selection -- all in one mechanism. Drop a file, gain a behavior. Quality ratings resolve ambiguity. The Translation Kit pattern generalized.
> 
> **5. Session types are a private dependency.** The developer never sees `Chan<S, T>`, never writes `send()` or `recv()`, never matches on `BranchResult`. The kit runs the session-typed handshake internally, exactly like Haiku's `BApplication::_ConnectToServer()` runs the AS_CREATE_APP protocol internally. The guarantees exist. The developer doesn't need to know about them.
> 
> **What I preserved from BeOS:** per-pane threads (one looper per pane, just like one BLooper per BWindow), asynchronous message batching (Hoffman's guidance about cache flushing), the `Handler` trait with default implementations for every event (the BHandler pattern of "override what you understand, ignore the rest"), message filters as small composable objects (Adams's praise of BMessageFilter), and transparent connection management (the developer writes their handler as if the connection is permanent).
> 
> **Open questions flagged:** content model (the `set_content(&[u8])` is a placeholder -- text panes, widget panes, and raw panes need different APIs), tag line editing ownership (compositor vs client), and multi-pane shared state patterns (whether the kit needs a coordination primitive beyond Arc<Mutex<T>>).</result>
> <usage><total_tokens>108840</total_tokens><tool_uses>44</tool_uses><duration_ms>479310</duration_ms></usage>
> </task-notification>

### `2026-03-27T02:34:22.331Z`

> educate me on the tagline as you understand it, I think we need to explore concretely what this means now in our architecture such that concrete discussion is possible. I want to tether it to my design vision of what this element means.

### `2026-03-27T02:42:22.733Z`

> okay, so here is the actual usage pattern I envision. I'm inspired by vim mode's ":" and emacs' "M-x" -- basically, what the user should see is a window title for floating windows which is a tab remniscent of the beos design. when a pane is tiled, the tagline will appear as an overlay above the top/bottom/center/etc (think nvim's interface for entering commands, which is amenable to customization in noice.nvim), once the keybinding is triggred on a focused window, upon which users can write text for commands which are specific to the context of that pane, definable by the pane developer. for floating windows i want this to have a visual response experience: the tab is initially the size of the pane title, like beos's tabs are, but when you hit `:` (ill use this for illustration for now) a blinking cursor appears in the tab itself, and you can write commands which will recommend completions as we are used to in textual interfaces, which will display according to the logic witten in the pane itself. this means that the tagline is essentially opt in, and is strategically used to capture the layer of abstraction at which this responsive interface is useful for the user's purposes (for example, a tag line might exist only at some level of containment of subpanes, because interfaces are more useful aggregated into the container)

### `2026-03-27T02:43:46.257Z`

> we ought to refine it, run it by the be engineer for their feedback according to the UX design philosophy referenced in its knowledge base

### `2026-03-27T02:48:40.237Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>af9951ba117081f6e</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_013YmXraGjGDkySz8hNefBmr</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/41ae307d-8864-4a1a-ab15-0c5308d4711f/tasks/af9951ba117081f6e.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Vet tag line modal command surface" completed</summary>
> <result>Written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/review-tagline-ux.md`.
> 
> The short version of the seven assessments:
> 
> **1. The BeOS tab was great because it was simple.** Identity, grabbability, zero cognitive load. Making it a command surface changes its nature -- acceptable only if it stays simple at rest.
> 
> **2. The biggest risk is activation discovery.** BeOS solved discoverability with menus (always visible, always browsable). This design hides commands behind an activation key the user must already know about. The mitigation: a visible affordance indicator in the chrome, and a browsable mode when activated with no input.
> 
> **3. Hierarchical commands are a genuine innovation** that i3/sway users would recognize, but scope ambiguity is the trap. Visual feedback (container highlighting, scope labels) must make the command target unambiguous.
> 
> **4. The modality is acceptable.** It passes every test the Be engineers applied: doesn't block the system, is visible when active, is transient, is user-initiated. It's structurally identical to a BPopUpMenu interaction. The one non-negotiable: Escape must always dismiss instantly.
> 
> **5. Completion beats menus for power users, loses for absolute beginners.** The fix: empty-query browsable mode (show all commands categorized), and shortcuts printed alongside command names in completions.
> 
> **6. Different visual presentations for floating vs tiled is fine** -- BeOS had multiple window_looks too. The interaction semantics must be identical; only the chrome adapts.
> 
> **7. Gassee would like the extensibility but note the missing warmth.** Tiled panes at rest have no identity. A thin name strip at the top would give them the personality the floating tab provides.</result>
> <usage><total_tokens>80207</total_tokens><tool_uses>40</tool_uses><duration_ms>264840</duration_ms></usage>
> </task-notification>

### `2026-03-27T03:01:23.222Z`

> 1: Yes I agree that is sound. 2: it should show the commands available in the context of that pane, yes. 3. think of it this way: 'container' is not ontologically distinct from pane: a container in a visual context is merely the dev-specified way that component panes are tensored in the app-level pane. that is, it is a choice of a parituclar way to present the tensored panes in a display interface. when we decide not to write a tag for a specific pane, it correlates with that pane being a *component* of a higher level pane. here are some use cases: a weather widget whose tag has options to look at different cities that are autocompleted as you type; a notification pane has tag lines describing user-definable responses to various notification types (of which the stock system will offer sensible defaults). The responsiblility for which level being obvious ought to fall to the developer, but the API should make it clear how to follow sensible design guidelines and utilize pane-idiomatic ways to integrate their app's functionality effectively with pane's API

### `2026-03-27T03:03:29.738Z`

> 
> **File:** `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/tagline-revised-spec.md`
> 
> **Structure:**
> 
> 1. **Revised architecture section 2 tag line language** -- replaces the single paragraph. Covers at-rest/activated distinction, floating tab vs. tiled strip, empty-query browsing, opt-in semantics, hierarchical scope, developer responsibility.
> 
> 2. **Revised pane-app API types** -- `PaneTitle`, `CommandVocabulary`, `CommandGroup`, `Command`, `CommandAction`, `BuiltIn`, `Completion`, `CommandSurfaceEvent` on the wire side. `Tag` builder, `CompletionFn`, `CompletionResponder`, `cmd()` convenience constructor on the kit side. Updated `create_pane` signature (accepts `impl Into<Option<Tag>>`), updated `Pane` operations, updated `PaneEvent` variants, updated `Handler` trait methods, updated wire protocol messages.
> 
> 3. **Revised hello-pane** -- 14 lines, same compactness as before, new API.
> 
> 4. **Weather widget example** -- demonstrates dynamic completions, title updates, looper discipline (network call on a spawned thread), the developer-provides-vocabulary pattern.
> 
> 5. **Filesystem representation** -- `/pane/<id>/title`, `/pane/<id>/commands/`, `/pane/<id>/ctl` with activate/dismiss/exec verbs.
> 
> 6. **Self-vet** -- tested against Schillings's clarity test, warmth, discoverability (three-layer model), modality (Be test on all four counts), hierarchical scope, Gassee's creative-use test. Passes all with noted honest costs (loss of permanent visibility, positional muscle memory, pointer-only browsing).
> 
> 7. **Downstream references** -- every line in the architecture spec that needs updating when this language is adopted, with the specific revision for each.
> 
> Key design decisions worth flagging:
> 
> - `Del`/`Snarf`/`Get`/`Put` (acme vocabulary) are replaced with `Close`/`Copy`/`Paste`/`Undo`/`Redo` (conventional vocabulary). The acme naming was coherent in Plan 9 but alien to every modern developer. This is a place where the spirit (built-in compositor actions) is preserved while the letter (acme naming) is discarded.
> 
> - The `TagRoute` event is gone. Routing is now triggered as a `CommandAction::Route` variant within the command vocabulary, which means routing is just another command action rather than a parallel dispatch path. This simplifies the event model.
> 
> - The tag typography changes: tag titles can now be proportional (they are display text, not executable text), while the command input field stays monospace. This follows from the shift from acme's "text IS the interface" to "the command surface is a modal input." The title is chrome; the command input is where precision matters.</result>

> <task-notification>

> <tool-use-id>toolu_01RbUbe4ERBVi3AX1EM1jDZJ</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/41ae307d-8864-4a1a-ab15-0c5308d4711f/tasks/a80e88821b36cbd93.output</output-file>

> <summary>Agent "Code review: pane-session + pane-notify" completed</summary>
> <result>Review written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/review-phase3-code.md`.
> 
> The short version: **the foundation is ready. Start building pane-app.**
> 
> The six findings worth highlighting in order of severity:
> 
> 1. **Moderate: delete tag.rs now.** The current TagLine/TagAction/TagCommand types are incompatible with the pane-app spec's Tag builder model. They'll cause confusion if pane-app imports pane-proto and sees two competing models. Replace with the wire-level type that matches the spec before starting pane-app.
> 
> 2. **Minor: framing is implemented four times.** UnixTransport, SessionSource, write_message, and pane-proto's wire.rs all do length-prefixed framing independently. They're consistent today but will drift. Consolidate into wire.rs.
> 
> 3. **Minor: no MAX_MESSAGE_SIZE test.** The 16MB guard is a security boundary. It should have a test that verifies rejection. Easy to add.
> 
> 4. **Minor: stub polling thread never terminates.** WatchHandle's "drop to unregister" contract isn't honored. Fine for macOS dev, but the pattern needs fixing before the Linux inotify implementation lands.
> 
> 5. **Minor: no partial-read test for calloop.** The accumulation buffer state machine looks correct but is never exercised with fragmented reads. Worth one test that writes a length prefix and body in separate writes.
> 
> 6. **Low: no try_recv.** Not needed for the initial pane-app looper (blocking recv on a dedicated thread is the BLooper pattern), but worth noting for future multiplexing needs.
> 
> Everything else -- the typestate mechanics, duality, transport abstraction, error classification, crash safety, calloop integration, macro ergonomics, naming, documentation -- is clean. This is the kind of foundation you can build on with confidence.</result>

### `2026-03-27T04:48:27.095Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a8ce1c97d55c83b1b</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_012T3dqYUdw6zALWrDMiYDg3</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/41ae307d-8864-4a1a-ab15-0c5308d4711f/tasks/a8ce1c97d55c83b1b.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Plan pane-app implementation" completed</summary>
> <result>Here's the plan.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## pane-app Implementation Plan
> 
> ### The Core Tension
> 
> There's no compositor yet. BeOS solved this by building BApplication and app_server in parallel -- same building, same whiteboard. We have a layered build sequence where Phase 4 (compositor) comes after Phase 3 (kits). So we need a testing seam.
> 
> The answer: a `MockCompositor` that speaks the protocol over in-memory transport. pane-session already provides `memory::pair()` for exactly this. The kit's public API gets exercised end-to-end; the mock replaces the real compositor. When Phase 4 arrives, swap the `Connection` enum variant from `Memory` to `Unix` and the same code paths run against the real compositor.
> 
> ### What Exists Already
> 
> **pane-session:** Fully functional. `Chan<S, T>`, `MemoryTransport`, `memory::pair()`, blocking send/recv with crash safety. This is the testing seam.
> 
> **pane-proto:** Wire types only. Has `PaneTitle`, `CommandVocabulary`, `Command`, `CommandAction`, `BuiltIn`, `Completion`, `KeyEvent`, `MouseEvent`, wire framing, `PaneId`. Does NOT have `ClientToComp`, `CompToClient`, `PaneGeometry`, `PaneHints`, handshake types, `CommandSurfaceEvent`, or `ScriptOp`. There's a placeholder comment in `message.rs` where these belong.
> 
> **pane-notify:** Working (stub on macOS, real on Linux). Channel-based event delivery.
> 
> **pane-app/Cargo.toml:** Exists with correct deps.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Stage 0: Protocol Types in pane-proto
> 
> No pane-app code yet. First we need the active-phase message types that both the kit and the compositor consume.
> 
> **Files:**
> 
> - `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/crates/pane-proto/src/protocol.rs` (new) -- `ClientToComp`, `CompToClient`, `PaneGeometry`, `PaneHints`, `CreatePaneTag`, `CommandSurfaceEvent`, `ScriptOp`, handshake types (`ClientHello`, `ServerHello`, `ClientCaps`, `Accepted`, `Rejected`)
> - `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/crates/pane-proto/src/tag.rs` (modify) -- add `Shell(String)` variant to `CommandAction` per revised tag spec
> - `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/crates/pane-proto/src/event.rs` (modify) -- add `KeyEvent::is_escape()` convenience (hello-pane needs it)
> - `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/crates/pane-proto/src/lib.rs` (modify) -- declare and re-export protocol module
> 
> **Key design choice:** `CompToClient` carries `PaneId` on every variant (multiplexed wire format). `PaneEvent` in the kit will not -- the kit demuxes before the developer sees it. These are separate types by design, not a newtype wrapper.
> 
> **Tests:** Extend proptest roundtrip for all new types. Handshake types, PaneGeometry, CommandSurfaceEvent, the whole lot.
> 
> **Done when:** `cargo test -p pane-proto` passes. All new types serialize/deserialize.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Stage 1: Tag Builder + PaneEvent + Error Types (Pure, No I/O)
> 
> This is the developer-facing surface. Zero threads, zero transport, zero connections. Just types and builders.
> 
> **Files:**
> 
> - `crates/pane-app/src/tag.rs` -- `Tag`, `CommandBuilder`, `cmd()` convenience function
> - `crates/pane-app/src/event.rs` -- `PaneEvent` enum, `from_comp()` conversion
> - `crates/pane-app/src/error.rs` -- `Error`, `ConnectError`, `PaneError`, `RouteError`, `Result<T>`
> - `crates/pane-app/src/lib.rs` -- module declarations, re-exports
> 
> **Key signatures:**
> 
> ```rust
> // tag.rs
> pub struct Tag { title: PaneTitle, vocabulary: CommandVocabulary }
> pub fn cmd(name: impl Into<String>, description: impl Into<String>) -> CommandBuilder
> impl Tag {
>     pub fn new(title: impl Into<String>) -> Self
>     pub fn short(self, short: impl Into<String>) -> Self
>     pub fn commands(self, commands: Vec<Command>) -> Self
>     pub fn groups(self, groups: Vec<CommandGroup>) -> Self
>     pub(crate) fn into_wire(self) -> CreatePaneTag
> }
> impl CommandBuilder {
>     pub fn shortcut(self, s: impl Into<String>) -> Self
>     pub fn client(self, data: impl Into<String>) -> Command
>     pub fn built_in(self, action: BuiltIn) -> Command
>     pub fn route(self, expr: impl Into<String>) -> Command
>     pub fn shell(self, cmd: impl Into<String>) -> Command
> }
> ```
> 
> **Design detail:** `Tag::commands(vec![...])` wraps the flat list in a default `CommandGroup { label: "Commands" }` because the wire type requires groups. `Tag::groups()` is for explicit categorization. This is the progressive complexity pattern: the common case is one line, the advanced case adds one method.
> 
> **PaneEvent is not CompToClient.** It's flatter:
> - `CompToClient::Command { pane, event: CommandSurfaceEvent::Executed { .. } }` becomes `PaneEvent::CommandExecuted { command, args }` -- PaneId stripped (already demuxed), nesting eliminated
> - `PaneEvent::Disconnected` and `PaneEvent::Reconnected` don't come from CompToClient at all -- they're generated by the transport layer
> - A `from_comp(msg: &CompToClient, pane: PaneId) -> Option<PaneEvent>` conversion filters by PaneId and flattens
> 
> **Tests:**
> 
> ```rust
> fn tag_builder_minimal()        // Tag::new("Hello") -> correct wire PaneTitle
> fn tag_builder_with_commands()  // commands wrapped in default group
> fn tag_builder_with_groups()    // explicit groups preserved
> fn cmd_all_action_types()       // client/built_in/route/shell all produce correct Command
> fn pane_event_from_comp()       // CompToClient variants map to correct PaneEvent
> fn pane_event_strips_pane_id()  // wrong PaneId -> None from from_comp
> ```
> 
> **Done when:** `cargo check -p pane-app` passes. Tag declarations from hello-pane work in isolation.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Stage 2: Handler Trait + Filter Chain + Looper
> 
> The dispatch machinery. Internal channels but no real transport -- we test by pushing events directly into the looper.
> 
> **Files:**
> 
> - `crates/pane-app/src/handler.rs` -- `Handler` trait with all defaulted methods
> - `crates/pane-app/src/filter.rs` -- `Filter` type, `FilterAction`, `FilterChain` (internal)
> - `crates/pane-app/src/looper.rs` -- `Looper` (internal) with `run_closure` and `run_handler`
> 
> **Handler trait methods (all with `-> Result<bool>`, all defaulted):**
> 
> `ready`, `resized`, `focused`, `blurred`, `key`, `mouse`, `command_activated`, `command_dismissed`, `command_executed`, `completion_request`, `close_requested`, `disconnected`, `reconnected`, `script_query`, `unhandled`
> 
> The `close_requested` default returns `Ok(false)` -- accept the close. All others return `Ok(true)` -- keep running.
> 
> **The looper is the heart.** It's a tight loop:
> 
> ```
> loop {
>     msg = receiver.recv()?;           // block on per-pane channel
>     event = PaneEvent::from_comp(msg, pane_id)?;
>     event = filter_chain.apply(event);
>     if consumed { continue }
>     keep_going = dispatch(event, handler)?;
>     if !keep_going { break }
> }
> ```
> 
> The looper is internal (`pub(crate)`). Developers interact with it through `Pane::run`, `Pane::run_with`, etc.
> 
> **Tests:**
> 
> ```rust
> fn filter_consume_prevents_handler()
> fn filter_modify_reaches_handler()   // key remap filter
> fn handler_close_exits_loop()
> fn handler_trait_dispatch_all_variants()  // every PaneEvent variant reaches the right Handler method
> fn closure_api_equivalent_to_handler()   // same events, same behavior
> ```
> 
> For these tests we don't need MockCompositor yet -- create a raw mpsc channel, push CompToClient messages into it, run the looper on the current thread, verify handler calls. A `Pane` struct can be faked with a dummy PaneId and a sender that goes nowhere.
> 
> **Done when:** Looper processes events correctly. Filter chain works. Handler trait dispatches to the right methods. The `Ok(false)` contract exits the loop.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Stage 3: MockCompositor + App/Pane + Threading
> 
> This is where it all comes together. Threads start. Messages flow. hello-pane runs.
> 
> **Files:**
> 
> - `crates/pane-app/src/app.rs` -- `App` type with connect, create_pane, wait, quit
> - `crates/pane-app/src/pane.rs` -- `Pane` type with run, spawn, set_title, etc. + `PaneHandle`
> - `crates/pane-app/src/connection.rs` -- `Connection` enum (Memory | Unix), wire read/write
> - `crates/pane-app/src/mock.rs` -- `MockCompositor` (cfg(test) only)
> 
> **The threading architecture:**
> 
> ```
> Main thread                   Mock thread
>     |                             |
>   App::connect_test() ---------> MockCompositor::run()
>     |                             |
>   [Dispatcher thread spawned]     |
>     |                             |
>   create_pane() --[CreatePane]--> responds with PaneCreated
>     |     ^--- oneshot blocks     |
>     |         until PaneCreated   |
>     |                             |
>   pane.run() on current thread    |
>     |                             |
>   Looper <---- per-pane channel   |
>     |      (fed by dispatcher)    |
> ```
> 
> **The dispatcher thread** reads CompToClient from the connection, looks up the PaneId in a shared map, and forwards to the appropriate per-pane mpsc channel. It's the client-side equivalent of Haiku's `BApplication::_PortLink` dispatcher. Special case: `PaneCreated` goes to a oneshot for the blocking `create_pane` call.
> 
> **MockCompositor** is simple:
> - Receives CreatePane -> assigns PaneId(N), sends PaneCreated with default 800x600 geometry
> - Receives RequestClose -> sends Close then CloseAck
> - Records SetTitle/SetVocabulary/SetContent for test assertions
> - Exposes `inject(CompToClient)` for test scenarios (send Key, Resize, etc.)
> 
> **Create_pane synchronous response:** `create_pane` sends CreatePane, then blocks on a oneshot channel. The dispatcher recognizes PaneCreated (which has no per-pane channel yet -- the pane doesn't exist) and sends it to the oneshot. After receiving PaneCreated, `create_pane` registers the per-pane channel in the map and returns the Pane.
> 
> Timeout: if the response doesn't arrive within 5 seconds, return `PaneError::Refused`. Prevents infinite hang if the mock or compositor is broken.
> 
> **`App::connect` (the real one):** For now, always returns `ConnectError::NotRunning` since there's no compositor. Tests use `App::connect_test` which accepts an in-memory connection. When Phase 4 arrives, `connect` will look up the compositor socket and do the real handshake.
> 
> **One spec issue I found:** `PaneHandle::post` is typed as `post(&self, msg: ClientToComp)` in the spec. This is wrong. `ClientToComp` is what the kit sends *to* the compositor. `post` should deliver a message to the pane's own looper -- it's `BLooper::PostMessage`, not `BMessenger::SendMessage`. The correct type is either `PaneEvent` or a kit-internal enum. For now, implement it as `post_event(&self, event: PaneEvent)`. Flag the spec discrepancy.
> 
> **Tests (the acceptance tests):**
> 
> ```rust
> #[test]
> fn hello_pane_lifecycle() {
>     // THE test. Must pass.
>     let (conn, mock) = MockCompositor::pair();
>     let mock_handle = std::thread::spawn(move || mock.run());
> 
>     let app = App::connect_test("com.example.hello", conn).unwrap();
>     let pane = app.create_pane(
>         Tag::new("Hello").commands(vec![
>             cmd("close", "Close this pane")
>                 .shortcut("Alt+W")
>                 .built_in(BuiltIn::Close),
>         ]),
>     ).unwrap();
> 
>     // Mock will inject a Close event after a small delay
>     pane.run(|event| match event {
>         PaneEvent::Key(key) if key.is_escape() => Ok(false),
>         PaneEvent::Close => Ok(false),
>         _ => Ok(true),
>     }).unwrap();
> }
> 
> #[test]
> fn multi_pane_independent_threads()  // two panes, verify events route correctly
> #[test]
> fn set_title_reaches_compositor()    // call set_title, mock verifies receipt
> #[test]
> fn spawn_and_wait()                  // spawn panes, app.wait() blocks until done
> #[test]
> fn handler_trait_with_mock()         // weather widget pattern: Handler impl with state
> ```
> 
> **Done when:** hello-pane compiles and runs against MockCompositor. Multi-pane threading works. `cargo test -p pane-app` passes.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Stage 4: API Completeness (Stubs for Deferred Features)
> 
> Fill out the full public API surface with clearly-marked stubs.
> 
> **Files:**
> 
> - `crates/pane-app/src/routing.rs` (new) -- `RouteTable` (stub: always NoMatch), `RouteResult`, `RouteCandidate`, `ContentHandler`
> - `crates/pane-app/src/scripting.rs` (new) -- `ScriptQuery`, `ScriptOp`, `ScriptReplyToken`, `PropertyDecl` (all stubbed)
> - Modify `app.rs` -- add `connect_with(signature, LaunchMode)`, `register_types()`, `is_primary()`
> - Modify `pane.rs` -- add `route()`, `declare_properties()`, `handle()` (for cross-thread PaneHandle)
> - Add `ConnectConfig`, `LaunchMode`
> 
> Every stub gets a `// TODO(phase-N): implement when <dep> exists` comment so there's no ambiguity about what's real and what's scaffolding.
> 
> **Tests:** Smoke tests that stubs don't panic. `route()` returns `NoMatch`. `declare_properties()` is a no-op.
> 
> **Done when:** Every public type and method from the pane-app spec exists. `cargo doc -p pane-app` generates complete API documentation.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Build Order Summary
> 
> | Stage | What | I/O? | Threads? | Key Test |
> |-------|------|------|----------|----------|
> | 0 | Protocol types in pane-proto | no | no | proptest roundtrip |
> | 1 | Tag builder, PaneEvent, errors | no | no | builder correctness |
> | 2 | Handler, Filter, Looper | channels | no | dispatch logic |
> | 3 | App, Pane, MockCompositor | channels | yes | hello-pane lifecycle |
> | 4 | Routing/scripting stubs | no | no | API completeness |
> 
> Each stage compiles and tests independently. The risk concentrates in Stage 3 (threading + mock). Stages 0-2 are pure logic.
> 
> ### Known Issues to Resolve
> 
> 1. **PaneHandle::post typing.** The spec says `ClientToComp` but it should be an event delivered to the pane's looper, not to the compositor. I'll implement it as `post_event(PaneEvent)` and note the discrepancy.
> 
> 2. **CompletionFn / CompletionResponder.** Deferred. The `on_completion` method on `Tag` requires a callback that runs on the looper thread and responds to the compositor. This needs the dispatcher + pane writer plumbing from Stage 3 to work. Add the types in Stage 4 as stubs, implement when the compositor exists.
> 
> 3. **Handshake types.** Defined in Stage 0 but not exercised until Phase 4. The mock skips the handshake (it just starts sending active-phase messages). When the real compositor exists, `App::connect` will run the session-typed handshake using the types from pane-session.
> 
> 4. **Pane batching.** The spec describes send-buffer batching with auto-flush after each handler invocation. This is an optimization. Stage 3 sends immediately (no batching). Add batching as a Stage 4+ optimization.</result>

> </task-notification>

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a30c74221e2b1dc8a</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01VKyrdJMg7Yxc5dHASCfPK3</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/41ae307d-8864-4a1a-ab15-0c5308d4711f/tasks/a30c74221e2b1dc8a.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Explore rust-nix-template structure" completed</summary>
> <result>Based on my comprehensive exploration of the rust-nix-template repository and its dependencies, here's a complete report:
> 
> ## COMPREHENSIVE RUST-NIX-TEMPLATE ARCHITECTURE REPORT
> 
> ### 1. INPUTS (Dependencies)
> 
> The template uses 6 main Nix flake inputs:
> 
> - **nixpkgs** (`github:nixos/nixpkgs/nixpkgs-unstable`) - Provides the standard package repository with the latest unstable packages
> - **flake-parts** (`github:hercules-ci/flake-parts`) - Module system for organizing the flake configuration into composable parts
> - **systems** (`github:nix-systems/default`) - Standard system definitions (x86_64-linux, aarch64-linux, etc.)
> - **rust-flake** (`github:juspay/rust-flake`) - A Nix flake module specifically for Rust development that wraps Crane
> - **git-hooks** (`github:cachix/git-hooks.nix`) - Pre-commit hook integration for code quality checks (set as `flake = false` since it's not a flake itself)
> 
> Input dependencies are carefully managed - `rust-flake` and `git-hooks` both follow the root nixpkgs to ensure version consistency.
> 
> ### 2. FLAKE-PARTS MODULE SYSTEM
> 
> The template uses flake-parts to organize configuration into reusable modules. Instead of monolithic flake.nix, the template dynamically imports all `.nix` files from the `./nix/modules/` directory. This approach:
> 
> - Keeps concerns separated (rust config, devshell, pre-commit, etc. in their own files)
> - Allows modules to be composed through the flake-parts module system
> - Handles complexity of managing per-system outputs automatically
> - Mirrors how NixOS manages system configuration through modules
> 
> ### 3. MODULE STRUCTURE (nix/modules/)
> 
> **devshell.nix** - Defines the development environment:
> - Inherits from `self'.devShells.rust` (the Rust-specific shell from rust-flake)
> - Adds `inputsFrom` to include the pre-commit devShell hooks
> - Packages included:
>   - `just` - Command/task runner for the justfile
>   - `nixd` - Language server for Nix code editing
>   - `bacon` - Rust development utility for hot-reloading during development
> - Creates a composite shell that combines Rust tooling with development utilities
> 
> **pre-commit.nix** - Sets up automated code formatting:
> - Imports the git-hooks.nix flake module
> - Enables `nixpkgs-fmt` for formatting Nix files
> - Enables `rustfmt` for formatting Rust code
> - These run automatically before git commits to maintain consistent code style
> 
> ### 4. RUST-FLAKE & CRANE INTEGRATION
> 
> rust-flake is a flake-parts module that wraps Crane (a Rust-specific build tool for Nix). It provides:
> 
> **What Crane Does:**
> - Builds Rust projects with Nix while maximizing incremental compilation
> - Creates binary and library outputs for Rust crates
> - Handles multi-crate workspaces automatically
> - Manages dependency caching at the Cargo level for efficiency
> 
> **rust-flake's Role:**
> - Provides sensible defaults for Rust projects (toolchain management, crate discovery)
> - Reads `rust-toolchain.toml` to determine the Rust version
> - Auto-discovers crates in workspaces by parsing `Cargo.toml` files
> - Merges outputs (packages and checks) from all configured crates
> - Applies rust-overlay to provide the Rust toolchain
> - Exports `flakeModules` with two modules:
>   - `default` - Main Rust configuration using crane
>   - `nixpkgs` - Integrates rust-overlay into nixpkgs
> 
> **rust-flake Configuration Options:**
> - `rust-project.crates` - Maps crate names to their paths containing Cargo.toml
> - `rust-project.crane-lib` - Provides crane library functions (automatically computed)
> - `rust-project.toolchain` - Specifies Rust toolchain with extensions (rust-src, rust-analyzer, clippy)
> - `rust-project.src` - Source directory filtering (includes Cargo files and optional crate-specific Nix files)
> 
> ### 5. DEVELOPMENT SHELL (devshell)
> 
> The composite development shell provides:
> - **Rust Toolchain** - From rust-flake/crane integration (rustc, cargo, rust-analyzer)
> - **Build/Development Tools**:
>   - `cargo` - Rust package manager
>   - `just` - Task automation (from justfile)
>   - `bacon` - Live development environment for hot-reloading
> - **Editor Support**:
>   - `nixd` - Nix language server for IDE integration
>   - `rust-analyzer` - Rust language server
> - **Code Quality**:
>   - `rustfmt` - Rust formatter (via pre-commit)
>   - `nixpkgs-fmt` - Nix formatter (via pre-commit)
> 
> Entering the shell via `nix develop` or `direnv allow` gives access to all these tools automatically.
> 
> ### 6. JUSTFILE TARGETS
> 
> The justfile contains 4 targets:
> 
> - **default** - Lists all available recipes using `@just --list`
> - **pre-commit-all** - `pre-commit run --all-files` - Runs formatters and linters on all project files
> - **run [*ARGS]** - `cargo run [ARGS]` - Executes the Rust project, optionally passing arguments
> - **watch [*ARGS]** - `bacon --job run -- -- [ARGS]` - Starts bacon for live development with automatic recompilation when code changes
> 
> ### 7. DIRENV INTEGRATION (.envrc)
> 
> The `.envrc` file automates environment setup:
> 
> ```
> source_url https://omnix.page/om/develop/omnixrc/v1 'sha256-...'
> watch_file rust-toolchain.toml nix/modules/*.nix
> use omnix
> ```
> 
> **How it works:**
> - Sources a remote omnix script with SHA256 verification for security
> - Watches specific files for changes (Rust toolchain config and Nix modules)
> - Invokes `use omnix` to activate the development environment
> - When you `cd` into the project directory:
>   - direnv automatically evaluates `.envrc`
>   - Downloads/loads omnix (a lightweight Nix environment tool)
>   - Makes the flake's devShell available in your shell
>   - Automatically reloads if watched files change
>   - Requires `direnv allow .envrc` on first use
> 
> This eliminates the need to manually run `nix develop` - the environment is automatically available when you enter the project directory.
> 
> ### 8. WORKFLOW SUMMARY
> 
> 1. User clones the template
> 2. Runs `direnv allow .envrc` (first time)
> 3. When entering the directory, direnv automatically loads the omnix development environment
> 4. omnix evaluates the flake and loads the composite devShell
> 5. Developer has immediate access to:
>    - Rust toolchain + cargo
>    - just for running tasks
>    - bacon for live development
>    - nixd for Nix editing
>    - Pre-commit hooks for code quality
> 6. Can run `just watch` to develop with hot-reload or `just run` to execute the project
> 
> The entire setup prioritizes developer experience by automating environment setup and providing convenient task automation while maintaining reproducibility through Nix.</result>

### `2026-03-27T06:32:40.466Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>af7f72aa9d252ffef</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_012S9XjBZ8xiHHUt7x8s38HX</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/41ae307d-8864-4a1a-ab15-0c5308d4711f/tasks/af7f72aa9d252ffef.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Evaluate iced-rs for pane widgets" completed</summary>
> <result>Written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/research-iced-rs.md`.
> 
> The short version: **don't use iced.** The mismatch is structural, not cosmetic.
> 
> The five problems you asked about all point the same direction:
> 
> 1. **Buffer rendering** — iced assumes it renders to a display surface via winit. No clean path to "render into this wl_buffer I hand you."
> 2. **Event loop ownership** — iced wants `main()`. There's no `update_once()` API. COSMIC works around this by accepting iced's event loop ownership, which is a concession pane shouldn't make.
> 3. **Frutiger Aero styling** — you'd have to reimplement every widget's draw method. Iced's theming is palette-level (colors, border radius), not the per-edge lighting and directional gradients the aesthetic spec requires. At that point you're not using iced's widgets.
> 4. **Per-pane threading** — winit wants one EventLoop on the main thread. Multiple independent iced instances on different threads is explicitly unsupported territory.
> 5. **Control vs speed** — iced ships a demo faster, but the adaptation cost for pane's actual requirements would exceed the cost of building the ~6 initial widgets on Vello+taffy within weeks.
> 
> The BeOS parallel is clean: the Interface Kit wasn't built on someone else's widget toolkit. It *was* the toolkit, designed around app_server's drawing model. Vello+taffy gives pane that same property — the rendering infrastructure and the widget library are one thing, designed for the system's actual architecture. Iced would introduce a permanent impedance mismatch between what the framework wants and what the compositor needs.</result>

### `2026-03-27T06:48:06.621Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a4ecea1c08ea21d91</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_017PyypkMBMepRFfAtHgnPY8</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/41ae307d-8864-4a1a-ab15-0c5308d4711f/tasks/a4ecea1c08ea21d91.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Vet pane-app API v2" completed</summary>
> <result>Review written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/review-pane-app-api-final.md`.
> 
> The three critical findings, in order of impact:
> 
> **P4/P5 -- Handler and closure have no back-channel.** `Pane::run()` consumes self, so neither the closure nor the Handler can send messages back to the compositor. No `set_title()`, no `set_content()`, nothing. The fix is a `PaneProxy` -- a cloneable `Send` handle wrapping `(PaneId, Sender<ClientToComp>)` -- passed as a second argument to the closure and available to Handler methods. This is the `BMessenger` pattern: lightweight, copyable, doesn't own the target.
> 
> **P6 -- `pending_creates` is LIFO.** `Vec::pop()` takes the last element. Two concurrent `create_pane` calls will swap responses. One-line fix: `VecDeque::pop_front()`.
> 
> **P3 -- `impl Into<Option<Tag>>` is the BMessage(BMessage\*) lesson.** Owen Smith deprecated that constructor because implicit conversions create confusion. Either require `Tag` always (with a `Tag::default()`), or provide two explicit methods. Don't rely on the blanket `From<T> for Option<T>` impl.
> 
> Everything else -- the flat `PaneEvent` enum, the closure API, the Tag builder, the Handler defaults, the fire-and-forget `set_*` methods, the Filter/FilterChain design -- is good work. The API is fundamentally sound. It captures the spirit of BLooper/BHandler/BMessageFilter without inheriting their C++ baggage.</result>

> ---
> 
> # pane-app Kit API Review — Final
> 
> **Date:** 2026-03-27  
> **Reviewer:** Be Systems Engineer (consultant)  
> **Scope:** All files in `crates/pane-app/src/`, `tests/hello_pane.rs`
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Fix Verification
> 
> ### Fix 1: PaneProxy as back-channel (was P4 + P5)
> 
> **Status: Applied. One compile error.**
> 
> The design intent is correct and well-executed:
> 
> - `PaneProxy` exists in `proxy.rs` as a `Clone`-able handle wrapping `(PaneId, mpsc::Sender<ClientToComp>)`. Fields are `pub(crate)`.
> - `Handler` trait methods all take `&PaneProxy` as first parameter after `&mut self`. Every method gets it. Consistent.
> - `run()` closure signature is `FnMut(&PaneProxy, PaneEvent) -> Result<bool>`. Proxy first (context before payload) — right convention.
> - `looper.rs` constructs one `PaneProxy` per loop, passes by reference to every dispatch. Clean.
> - `Pane::proxy()` method (pane.rs:81-83) exists for pre-run access — clone it and hand to a background thread before entering the loop.
> - The test (`hello_pane.rs:43`) uses `|_proxy, event|`. Correct.
> 
> The BMessenger analogy is apt. BMessenger held a `port_id` and `team_id` — just enough to `SendMessage()` without owning the target. PaneProxy holds a PaneId and a channel sender. Same ownership discipline.
> 
> **Bug (C1): `PaneProxy::new` doesn't exist.** `pane.rs` calls `PaneProxy::new(id, comp_tx.clone())` on lines 82, 87, and 105. But `proxy.rs` defines no `new` method. The struct has `pub(crate)` fields, so construction via struct literal would work, but the call sites use a named constructor that doesn't exist. This won't compile.
> 
> One-line fix — add to `proxy.rs`:
> 
> ```rust
> pub(crate) fn new(id: PaneId, sender: mpsc::Sender<ClientToComp>) -> Self {
>     PaneProxy { id, sender }
> }
> ```
> 
> ### Fix 2: VecDeque::pop_front for pending_creates (was P6)
> 
> **Status: Applied correctly.**
> 
> `app.rs:3` imports `VecDeque`. Line 27 declares `VecDeque<...>`. Line 66 uses `pop_front()`. Line 115 uses `push_back()`. FIFO ordering preserved. The old `Vec::pop()` was LIFO — would have misrouted PaneCreated responses under concurrent creation.
> 
> ### Fix 3: Explicit create_pane(Tag) + create_component_pane() (was P3)
> 
> **Status: Applied correctly.**
> 
> Two distinct methods, both delegating to `create_pane_inner(Option<CreatePaneTag>)`. No `impl Into<Option<Tag>>`. `create_component_pane` is a better name than the suggested `create_pane_default` — communicates what the pane *is*, not just that it lacks configuration. The doc comment is exactly right.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Overall API Assessment
> 
> ### What's working well
> 
> **1. The ownership model is clean and the mapping to Be is precise.**
> 
> | pane-app | BeOS | Role |
> |----------|------|------|
> | `App` | `BApplication` | Connection owner, pane factory |
> | `Pane` | `BWindow` | Handle, consumed by event loop entry |
> | `PaneProxy` | `BMessenger` | Cloneable send-capability |
> | `looper::run_*` | `BLooper::task_looper()` | Per-pane message dispatch |
> | `Handler` | `BHandler` | Override what you understand |
> | `FilterChain` | `BMessageFilter` chain | Ordered intercept/transform |
> | `PaneEvent` | `BMessage::what` | Typed, exhaustive, compiler-checked |
> 
> The move to consuming `self` on `run()` is *stronger* than BLooper's `Lock()`/`Unlock()` convention. You cannot mutate pane state behind the event loop's back. The proxy is the only escape hatch, and it only lets you send messages — which is the whole point.
> 
> **2. Dual-mode handler is well-designed.** Closures for hello-pane, Handler trait for real apps. Both get `&PaneProxy`. `dispatch_to_handler` is the exhaustive translation layer. Handler defaults are sensible — everything continues except `close_requested` and `disconnected`.
> 
> **3. Tag builder is pleasant API craft.** `CommandBuilder` is an incomplete type; you *must* call a terminal method (`.client()`, `.built_in()`, `.route()`) to get a `Command`. Can't create half-built commands.
> 
> **4. FilterChain improves on BMessageFilter.** `FilterAction::Pass(event)` carries the possibly-modified event, which is cleaner than Be's mutate-through-pointer approach.
> 
> **5. PaneProxy's method surface is exactly right.** `set_title`, `set_vocabulary`, `set_content`, `set_completions`, `id()`. The internal `send()` helper deduplicates error mapping. `Debug` impl omits the sender. Small details that add up.
> 
> ### Issues to fix
> 
> **Critical:**
> 
> **C1. PaneProxy::new doesn't exist — code won't compile.** One-line fix as shown above.
> 
> **Moderate:**
> 
> **M1. lib.rs Quick Start example is stale.** Line 22 shows `pane.run(|event| match event {` — the old single-argument closure. Actual signature requires `|_proxy, event|`. Stale doc examples are worse than no doc examples.
> 
> **M2. Pane duplicates PaneProxy methods.** `Pane` has `set_title()`, `set_vocabulary()`, `set_content()` (lines 116-141), identical to PaneProxy. Once `Pane::proxy()` exists (line 81), these are redundant. Worse: they can't be called after `run()` consumes self, which is when you'd actually want them. Pre-run config goes through Tag. Post-run updates go through the proxy. Remove from Pane.
> 
> **M3. App::wait() is a spin loop.** Polls `pane_count` every 50ms. Should be a condvar or watch-channel. Not blocking for demo phase.
> 
> **M4. MockCompositor::close_first_pane_after takes `delay: Duration` but ignores it.** Dead parameter. Either plumb it or remove it.
> 
> **M5. Dispatcher mutex poisoning.** `app.rs:65-66` uses `.lock().unwrap()`. A panicking handler on another thread could poison the mutex and crash the dispatcher. Use `parking_lot::Mutex` or handle the poisoned case.
> 
> **Minor:**
> 
> **m1. extract_pane_id returns Option\<u64\> but is exhaustive.** All 12 variants listed. Never returns None. The Option is misleading.
> 
> **m2. PaneEvent::from_comp does redundant PaneId filtering.** Dispatcher already routes by PaneId. Belt-and-suspenders is fine but should be documented as such.
> 
> **m3. Handler requires Send + 'static but closures don't.** Both currently run on calling thread. The asymmetry should be documented or resolved.
> 
> **m4. connection.rs and mock.rs are pub modules.** Internal plumbing and test infrastructure should be `pub(crate)` or feature-gated.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Previous review issues — disposition
> 
> | P# | Issue | Status |
> |----|-------|--------|
> | P3 | `impl Into<Option<Tag>>` too clever | **Fixed.** Two explicit methods. |
> | P4 | Handler can't send messages | **Fixed.** PaneProxy passed to all methods. |
> | P5 | Closure can't send messages | **Fixed.** Closure takes `&PaneProxy`. |
> | P6 | LIFO pending_creates | **Fixed.** VecDeque with pop_front. |

> | P11 | Stubs re-exported as public API | **Open.** |
> | P12 | looper should be pub(crate) | **Open.** |
> 
> ---

> 
> The three critical fixes are applied. PaneProxy is the right BMessenger translation. VecDeque is correct. The create_pane/create_component_pane split communicates intent clearly.
> 
> One compile error (C1) needs fixing. After that, the moderate issues are quality-of-life improvements that can be batched. The API is ready for continued development against. The bones are right.

### `2026-03-27T08:25:51.890Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>ab0465801bff3a69e</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_0173fwGTWoAqeTVisge5BAw5</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/fd904e50-fb1c-459f-8adf-ecbbcfa19341/tasks/ab0465801bff3a69e.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Is PaneProxy the right name?" completed</summary>
> <result>Having read the code, here's my take.
> 
> **`PaneProxy` is the wrong name.** You diagnosed it correctly: it doesn't proxy the pane, it sends messages *about* the pane *to* the compositor. A proxy stands in for the thing behind it. This thing is a handle to a channel with a stamped return address.
> 
> **What the Be engineers would have called it:** We would have called it `BMessenger` — but that name made sense because BMessenger was generic. It could target any BLooper/BHandler. Here, it's specialized: it always targets the compositor, always on behalf of one specific pane.
> 
> **My recommendation: `Pane`.**
> 
> Look at the Handler signatures:
> 
> ```rust
> fn ready(&mut self, pane: &Pane, geometry: PaneGeometry) -> Result<bool>
> fn key(&mut self, pane: &Pane, event: KeyEvent) -> Result<bool>
> ```
> 
> That reads exactly right. The handler *handles events*, and it gets a reference to the *pane* it's handling them for. The pane is what you talk to when you want to affect the thing on screen. `pane.set_title(...)` is the most natural call site you could write.
> 
> This is the same instinct that led us to name things like `BWindow` — the object you interact with *is* the window, even though under the hood it's a messaging proxy to the app_server's actual window state. The user doesn't care that it's a handle. They care that it represents the pane.
> 
> You already have a `Pane` type in `pane.rs` — that's the application-side owner that runs the event loop. But that type is internal plumbing; users interact with `App::pane()` to create one and then it disappears into the run loop. The *proxy* is what lives in their hands all day.
> 
> If collapsing the names feels too aggressive right now, **`PaneRef`** is the second choice. It's Rust-idiomatic (think `Arc` → `Weak`, owned → ref), it's short, and it communicates "I don't own this, I refer to it." But I'd push for just `Pane`.
> 
> Avoid `PaneHandle` (vague, Win32 energy), `PaneSender` (too implementation-leaky — it's not *just* a sender, it has methods), and `PaneMessenger` (inherits BMessenger's generality without the generality).</result>

### `2026-03-27T08:27:40.832Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a84c7a65568545df3</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01TZhB1GdT3FbxMT5EcU5nny</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/fd904e50-fb1c-459f-8adf-ecbbcfa19341/tasks/a84c7a65568545df3.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Name the Pane types" completed</summary>
> <result>
> 
> `PaneLooper` is wrong. It exposes mechanism where you want to expose intent.
> 
> In BeOS, `BWindow` was the name. You constructed it, it spawned its thread and ran its loop, and you never thought about those internals — the name said "this is a window." The looper nature was an implementation detail inherited from `BLooper`, not something in the class name.
> 
> The right name is just **`Pane`** for the owner type. And the proxy becomes **`PaneHandle`**.
> 
> The reasoning:
> 
> - **`Pane`** is what the developer creates and what they conceptually own. `App::create_pane(tag)` returns a `Pane`. `Pane::run()` consumes it. This matches `BWindow` — you construct the thing, you interact with the thing, the threading is invisible.
> 
> - **`PaneHandle`** is what you get inside the callback — a borrowed reference to talk to the compositor. This is a well-understood Rust idiom. You see it in `JoinHandle`, `WindowHandle` (winit), `ChildHandle`. It says "I don't own this, but I can operate on it."
> 
> So the API reads:
> 
> ```rust
> let pane = app.create_pane("main")?;
> pane.run(|handle, event| {
>     handle.set_title("hello");
> })?;
> ```
> 
> Handler methods:
> 
> ```rust
> fn key(&mut self, pane: &PaneHandle, event: KeyEvent)
> ```
> 
> Be would have called the pair `BWindow` and... there was no pair. `BWindow` was both because `this` served double duty. But if we'd had to split ownership from operation in C++, we'd have reached for the handle pattern. It's the natural decomposition of what `BWindow` was.</result>
