> <result>I've now read everything. Let me write the plan.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Phase 4: Compositor Implementation Plan
> 
> ## Current State Assessment
> 
> **What exists:**
> - `pane-comp`: ~450 lines. Winit-only smithay backend, hardcoded render of one pane with tag line/borders/body background. No calloop event loop (uses `thread::sleep(16ms)` for frame pacing). No Wayland display. No pane protocol. Glyph atlas exists but text rendering not yet wired to the frame. The `cell.rs` and `pane_renderer.rs` are demo-quality rendering of a hardcoded `CellRegion`.
> - `pane-app`: Complete client-side kit with dispatcher thread, per-pane routing, create/close lifecycle, `PaneHandle` proxy, filter chain, handler trait, routing stubs, scripting stubs. Currently connects only via `App::connect_test()` using in-memory `mpsc` channels. `App::connect()` returns `Err(ConnectError::NotRunning)`.
> - `pane-session`: Working session types (`Chan<S, T>`), unix transport with length-prefixed framing, calloop `SessionSource` for non-blocking message receipt, `write_message()` for sending, `connect_session()`/`accept_session()` for typed handshake channels.
> - `pane-proto`: Protocol enums (`ClientToComp`/`CompToClient`), handshake types (`ClientHello`/`ServerHello`/`ClientCaps`/`Accepted`/`Rejected`), `PaneId`, `PaneGeometry`, serialization via postcard, cell/color/event/tag types.
> 
> **What's salvageable from pane-comp:**
> - `glyph_atlas.rs` — CPU-side glyph rasterization and atlas packing. This is solid work, needs only the GPU upload step (texture creation + `render_texture_from_to` for blitting glyphs). Keep.
> - `pane_renderer.rs` — The chrome layout logic (tag bar, beveled borders, body background) is the right structure but hardcoded to one pane at fixed coordinates. Refactor into a method that takes layout geometry. The `color_to_rgba` mapping is fine. Keep, refactor.
> - `cell.rs` — This is actually in pane-proto, just re-exported. Fine.
> - `main.rs` — The smithay winit init, output creation, and render loop structure is correct but needs to become calloop-driven. Rewrite, but the structure is a reference.
> 
> **What's missing:**
> 1. calloop event loop (the compositor currently spin-sleeps)
> 2. Wayland display (no `Display::new()`, no client protocol handling)
> 3. Pane protocol listener (no unix socket, no handshake, no active-phase dispatch)
> 4. Threading (everything is single-threaded)
> 5. Layout tree (hardcoded single pane)
> 6. Input handling (libinput, xkbcommon)
> 7. Real `App::connect()` on the client side
> 
> **macOS vs Linux:** pane-comp is Linux-only (smithay requires Wayland/DRM/libinput). All compositor stages must be tested on Linux. The winit backend lets you run as a window inside another Wayland or X11 session, which is the dev workflow. pane-app kit tests and pane-session tests work on macOS via the mock/memory transports. The plan marks each test as [Linux] or [cross-platform].
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Stage 1: Calloop Skeleton
> 
> **Goal:** Replace the sleep loop with a real calloop event loop. Add a Wayland display (even with no protocol handlers). This is the foundation everything else plugs into.
> 
> ### Files
> 
> | File | Action | Description |
> |---|---|---|
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/main.rs` | Rewrite | calloop `EventLoop`, winit backend as calloop source, frame dispatch on redraw |
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/state.rs` | Create | `CompState` struct — the compositor's mutable state, passed as `&mut` in calloop callbacks. Holds the smithay `Display`, output list, renderer reference, glyph atlas, layout tree (initially empty) |
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/glyph_atlas.rs` | Keep | No changes needed yet |
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/pane_renderer.rs` | Refactor | Extract `render_chrome()` to take a geometry rect + tag text + focus state instead of hardcoded coords. Keep the same drawing primitives |
> | `crates/pane-comp/Cargo.toml` | Update | Add `wayland-server` if not pulled by smithay features. Ensure `backend_winit` + renderer features |
> 
> ### Key Decisions
> 
> **calloop as the heartbeat.** The winit backend's event dispatch becomes a calloop source (smithay provides this integration). Frame rendering triggers from `WinitEvent::Redraw`, not from a timer. This gives proper vsync-aligned frame pacing on the winit backend.
> 
> **`CompState` is the single mutable state object.** This is smithay's recommended pattern — all handler traits receive `&mut CompState`. It owns everything the main thread needs: display handle, output configuration, layout tree, glyph atlas, and (later) the channel receivers from pane threads.
> 
> **Wayland display creation.** Even in Stage 1, create a `Display::<CompState>::new()`. No protocol handlers yet means no clients can connect, but the display's calloop source gets registered, establishing the pattern for Stage 2.
> 
> ### Acceptance Criteria
> 
> 1. `cargo run -p pane-comp` opens a winit window showing the warm grey background and the hardcoded demo pane — same visual as today.
> 2. Window resize triggers immediate re-render (no 16ms delay).
> 3. Close button triggers clean exit via calloop event loop shutdown.
> 4. The render loop is driven by calloop, not by `thread::sleep`. Verify by logging frame times — they should align with vsync, not with 16ms intervals.
> 5. `tracing` output shows calloop dispatching events.
> 
> ### Tests
> 
> - [Linux] Manual: launch, resize, close. Verify responsiveness improvement.
> - [cross-platform] None — this stage is pure compositor work. pane-app tests still use MockCompositor.
> 
> ### What this stage does NOT do
> 
> No pane protocol. No unix socket. No threading. No layout tree mutations. No input handling. Just the event loop foundation.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Stage 2: Pane Protocol Server
> 
> **Goal:** The compositor listens on a unix socket, accepts pane-app client connections, runs the session-typed handshake, and enters the active phase. This is where `App::connect()` starts working.
> 
> ### Files
> 
> | File | Action | Description |
> |---|---|---|
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/protocol.rs` | Create | The pane protocol server. `PaneListener` wraps a `UnixListener` as a calloop source. On accept: run the 3-phase handshake (blocking on the newly-accepted stream — handshake is fast). On success: transition to active phase, register `SessionSource` on calloop for message dispatch |
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/session.rs` | Create | Session type definitions for the compositor side of the handshake. Type alias for the server handshake: `Recv<ClientHello, Send<ServerHello, Recv<ClientCaps, Select<Send<Accepted, End>, Send<Rejected, End>>>>>`. The dual of whatever the client declares |
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/client.rs` | Create | `ClientConnection` — tracks one connected pane-app client. Holds the `SessionSource` (calloop-registered), the client's signature, next pane ID for this connection, list of pane IDs owned by this client. Methods: `handle_message(&mut CompState, ClientToComp)` — dispatches CreatePane, RequestClose, SetTitle, etc |
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/state.rs` | Modify | Add `clients: HashMap<ClientId, ClientConnection>`, `panes: HashMap<PaneId, PaneState>`, `next_pane_id: NonZeroU32`, `listener_path: PathBuf` |
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/pane_state.rs` | Create | `PaneState` — compositor-side state for one pane. Geometry, tag content, tag bitmask, focus state, owning client ID. No thread yet — that's Stage 3 |
> | `crates/pane-app/src/app.rs` | Modify | Implement `App::connect()`. Discover socket path (`$XDG_RUNTIME_DIR/pane-0` or `$PANE_SOCKET`). Connect via `UnixStream`. Run client-side handshake. Extract stream, make non-blocking, wrap in `Connection` using the framing layer. From there, the existing dispatcher thread + `mpsc` routing works — but now over a real unix socket instead of in-memory channels |
> | `crates/pane-app/src/connection.rs` | Modify | Add `Connection::from_unix(stream: UnixStream)` that wraps a unix stream with the framing protocol, producing the same `Sender<ClientToComp>`/`Receiver<CompToClient>` interface the kit already uses. This is the bridge: underneath it's unix socket I/O; above it's the same `mpsc`-shaped interface |
> | `crates/pane-comp/Cargo.toml` | Update | Add `pane-session` and `pane-proto` dependencies |
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/main.rs` | Modify | Initialize `PaneListener`, register on calloop. Pass socket path via CLI arg or env var |
> 
> ### Key Decisions
> 
> **Handshake is synchronous on the accepted stream.** The stream is blocking during the handshake (it's a fresh connection, the handshake is 4 messages totaling <1KB, takes microseconds). After the handshake succeeds and reaches `End`, call `chan.finish()` to reclaim the `UnixTransport`, extract the `UnixStream` via `.into_stream()`, set non-blocking, wrap in `SessionSource`, register on calloop. This is the phase transition the session types were designed for.
> 
> **Socket path convention.** `$XDG_RUNTIME_DIR/pane-0` for the first compositor instance. The compositor creates the socket; clients discover it. Environment variable `$PANE_SOCKET` overrides for testing.
> 
> **Single-threaded dispatch in Stage 2.** All message handling happens on the calloop thread. This is correct for a small number of panes and lets us validate the protocol before adding threading complexity. The single-threaded path is also the permanent path for legacy Wayland clients, so it's not throwaway work.
> 
> **Connection wrapping for pane-app.** The tricky part is that `pane-app`'s `App` currently uses `mpsc::Sender<ClientToComp>` / `mpsc::Receiver<CompToClient>` for its internal communication. For the real connection, we need a bridge: a thread that reads from the unix socket and pushes into an `mpsc::Sender<CompToClient>`, and another direction where `mpsc::Receiver<ClientToComp>` is drained onto the socket. This is essentially what the dispatcher thread already does — but now the source is a socket instead of an in-memory channel. The cleanest approach: `Connection::from_unix()` spawns a reader thread (socket->mpsc) and the existing `comp_tx` sender writes to a writer thread (mpsc->socket). Two threads per connection on the client side: reader and writer. This matches the existing architecture where the dispatcher thread already reads.
> 
> ### Acceptance Criteria
> 
> 1. Start pane-comp. It creates `$XDG_RUNTIME_DIR/pane-0` (or `$PANE_SOCKET`).
> 2. A pane-app client using `App::connect("com.example.hello")` successfully connects, handshake completes, and `create_pane()` returns a `Pane` with a compositor-assigned `PaneId`.
> 3. The compositor logs: client connected, handshake complete, pane created.
> 4. The pane appears in `CompState.panes` with correct geometry.
> 5. `RequestClose` from the client results in `CloseAck` and pane removal.
> 6. Client process dying mid-session is detected (socket EOF), pane cleaned up, compositor continues.
> 7. `hello_pane` test works against the real compositor (not just MockCompositor).
> 
> ### Tests
> 
> - [Linux] Integration test: start pane-comp in a subprocess, connect pane-app, create pane, close pane, verify clean lifecycle. This is the real-compositor version of `hello_pane_lifecycle`.
> - [cross-platform] `pane-app` unit tests continue to work against `MockCompositor` unchanged — the `Connection` abstraction means the kit doesn't care which transport is underneath.
> - [Linux] Crash test: start client, kill -9 it, verify compositor detects disconnect and cleans up.
> 
> ### What this stage does NOT do
> 
> No rendering of client-created panes (the hardcoded demo pane still renders). No per-pane threads. No input dispatch. The compositor knows about panes in its state, but the render pass doesn't use that state yet.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Stage 3: Per-Pane Threading
> 
> **Goal:** Implement the three-tier threading model. Dispatcher threads demux socket messages to per-pane threads. Per-pane threads handle protocol state. Main thread receives notifications via `calloop::channel`.
> 
> ### Files
> 
> | File | Action | Description |
> |---|---|---|
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/dispatcher.rs` | Create | `Dispatcher` — owns the unix socket read half for one client connection. Spawns as a `std::thread`. Reads framed messages, inspects `PaneId`, routes to the correct pane thread's `mpsc::Sender`. Handles connection-level messages (CreatePane spawns a pane thread, RequestClose signals one). Outbound path: drains a `mpsc::Receiver<(PaneId, CompToClient)>` and writes to the socket |
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/pane_thread.rs` | Create | `PaneThread` — owns per-pane session state. Spawns as a `std::thread`. Message loop: select between inbound (from dispatcher) and commands (from main thread). Sends notifications to main thread via `calloop::channel::Sender`. Does not touch smithay objects |
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/layout.rs` | Create | `LayoutTree` wrapped in `Arc<RwLock<>>`. Initially trivial: a flat list of pane geometries (no splits yet — that's Stage 5). Methods: `read_pane_geometry(id) -> PaneGeometry` (takes read lock), `add_pane() / remove_pane()` (takes write lock, called only from main thread) |
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/state.rs` | Modify | Replace `HashMap<PaneId, PaneState>` with `Arc<RwLock<LayoutTree>>`. Add `calloop::channel::Channel<PaneNotification>` as a calloop source — receives messages from pane threads. Add `HashMap<ClientId, DispatcherHandle>` where `DispatcherHandle` has the sender for outbound messages |
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/protocol.rs` | Modify | After handshake, instead of registering `SessionSource` on calloop, hand the socket off to a new `Dispatcher` thread. The dispatcher owns the socket now |
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/client.rs` | Modify or remove | Client connection tracking moves into the dispatcher. The calloop-registered `SessionSource` per client is replaced by the dispatcher thread. Client metadata (signature, pane list) lives in the dispatcher |
> 
> ### Key Types
> 
> ```
> // Main thread ← pane threads
> enum PaneNotification {
>     TagUpdated { pane: PaneId, title: PaneTitle },
>     ContentUpdated { pane: PaneId },
>     StateChanged { pane: PaneId, /* ... */ },
>     PaneReady { pane: PaneId },
>     PaneDone { pane: PaneId },
> }
> 
> // Main thread → pane threads (per-pane channel)
> enum PaneCommand {
>     Input(CompToClient),      // Key, Mouse, Focus, Blur
>     LayoutChanged(PaneGeometry),
>     Shutdown,
> }
> 
> // Dispatcher → pane thread
> // Raw ClientToComp messages, already demuxed by PaneId
> 
> // Pane thread → dispatcher (for outbound to client)
> // (PaneId, CompToClient) tuples, serialized by dispatcher onto socket
> ```
> 
> ### Key Decisions
> 
> **Dispatcher owns the socket.** Once the handshake completes on the main thread, the raw `UnixStream` is handed to a new `Dispatcher` thread. The main thread never reads from client sockets again. This is the clean separation: main thread owns smithay + calloop, dispatcher threads own client sockets, pane threads own protocol state.
> 
> **CreatePane flows through the main thread.** When the dispatcher receives `CreatePane`, it sends a request to the main thread (via `calloop::channel`). The main thread allocates the `PaneId`, updates the layout tree (write lock), spawns the pane thread, and sends `PaneCreated` back through the dispatcher's outbound channel. This ensures layout tree mutations are exclusive to the main thread.
> 
> **Pane thread message loop.** Each pane thread blocks on a two-source select: its channel from the dispatcher (client messages) and its channel from the main thread (commands). In Rust, this is `crossbeam_channel::select!` or a manual poll with `recv_timeout`. I'd lean toward `crossbeam_channel` here — it gives clean multi-channel select without async, which is exactly the BeOS MessageLooper pattern (block on port, wake on message from any source).
> 
> **Graceful pane thread crash handling.** If a pane thread panics (shouldn't happen, but defense in depth), the dispatcher detects the channel disconnect and notifies the main thread via `calloop::channel`. The main thread removes the pane from the layout tree and sends `CloseAck` to the client. No compositor crash.
> 
> ### Acceptance Criteria
> 
> 1. Create 5 panes from a single client. Each gets its own server-side thread (verify via `tracing` output showing distinct thread names like `pane-1`, `pane-2`, etc.).
> 2. One pane thread sleeping for 2 seconds (simulated slow handler) does not delay message processing on sibling panes. Measure: send a message to all 5 panes simultaneously, verify the 4 non-sleeping panes respond within milliseconds.
> 3. Kill client process. Dispatcher detects EOF, all pane threads shut down, main thread cleans up layout tree. No panics, no leaked threads.
> 4. Layout tree reads from pane threads succeed concurrently (no contention on the read lock).
> 5. `hello_pane` test still works — the threading is transparent to the client.
> 
> ### Tests
> 
> - [Linux] Integration: multi-pane isolation test. Create 3 panes, inject a sleep into one pane's handler, verify others respond promptly.
> - [Linux] Integration: client crash cleanup. Connect, create panes, kill -9, verify all pane state cleaned up.
> - [Linux] Unit: `LayoutTree` under concurrent read + write. Spawn N reader threads + 1 writer thread, verify no deadlocks, readers see consistent state.
> - [cross-platform] `pane-app` tests unchanged — they still use `MockCompositor`.
> 
> ### New Dependencies
> 
> - `crossbeam-channel` (for multi-source select in pane threads). Widely used, well-maintained, fits the model exactly.
> - `calloop::channel` (already available via calloop — no new dep, just using the feature).
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Stage 4: Chrome Rendering
> 
> **Goal:** Render chrome for real panes (not the hardcoded demo). Tag lines with text, beveled borders, focus indicators. The compositor draws chrome around each pane's geometry as determined by the layout tree.
> 
> ### Files
> 
> | File | Action | Description |
> |---|---|---|
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/chrome.rs` | Create | `ChromeRenderer` — takes the layout tree (read lock), the glyph atlas, and a `GlesFrame`, renders chrome for all visible panes. Methods: `render_tag_line(frame, rect, title, focused)`, `render_borders(frame, rect, focused)`, `render_focus_indicator(frame, rect)` |
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/glyph_atlas.rs` | Modify | Add `upload_to_gpu(renderer: &mut GlesRenderer) -> GlesTexture`. This creates the GPU texture from the CPU-side atlas data. Add `render_text(frame, text, position, color, atlas_texture)` that blits individual glyphs from the atlas texture using `render_texture_from_to` |
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/pane_renderer.rs` | Rewrite | Remove the hardcoded demo pane. The render pass now iterates visible panes from the layout tree and calls `ChromeRenderer` for each. Client body buffers are composited in their geometry rects (placeholder: clear to body background color until Stage 2 of the client buffer path is implemented) |
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/theme.rs` | Create | Color constants and chrome dimensions centralized. The Frutiger Aero palette: warm yellow tag lines, warm grey desktop, beveled borders with light/dark edges, focus indicator (bright blue outline or similar). Font size, border width, tag line height as configurable constants |
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/state.rs` | Modify | Add atlas texture handle to `CompState`. Frame render pass reads layout tree (read lock), renders chrome for each visible pane |
> 
> ### Key Decisions
> 
> **Text rendering via glyph atlas texture.** The atlas is already rasterized on the CPU. Upload it as a GLES texture once (and re-upload on atlas growth). For each glyph, `render_texture_from_to` blits the source rect (glyph in atlas) to the destination rect (position on screen). This is how every game engine and terminal emulator does it. smithay's `GlesFrame::render_texture_from_to` handles the UV mapping.
> 
> **Chrome renders per-pane, not per-output.** Each pane in the layout tree gets its own chrome drawn. The render pass is:
> 1. Clear background
> 2. For each visible pane (layout tree read lock):
>    a. Render tag line background + text
>    b. Render beveled borders
>    c. Render focus indicator (if focused)
>    d. Render body (initially: solid color; later: client buffer)
> 3. Finish frame, submit
> 
> **No client buffers yet.** This stage renders chrome around placeholder body areas. Actual client buffer compositing (importing `wl_buffer` or shared memory from pane-app clients) is a separate concern that comes when we have the buffer submission protocol. For now, the body area is a white rectangle — proof that the geometry is correct.
> 
> ### Acceptance Criteria
> 
> 1. Create 2 panes from a test client with different titles. Both render with correct tag lines showing their titles.
> 2. The focused pane has a visually distinct indicator (brighter border, highlight color, etc.).
> 3. Tag line text is legible — glyph atlas renders correctly to GPU. All ASCII characters render without artifacts.
> 4. Resize the compositor window. Pane chrome re-renders at new positions (layout tree provides new geometry).
> 5. The beveled border has visible 3D effect: light edge top-left, dark edge bottom-right.
> 
> ### Tests
> 
> - [Linux] Visual: screenshot comparison (manual initially, automated later). Create panes with known titles, compare rendered output.
> - [Linux] Performance: render 20 panes. Frame time stays under 16ms (60fps).
> - [Linux] Glyph atlas: verify all ASCII characters render. Create a pane titled with the full printable ASCII range.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Stage 5: Input and Layout
> 
> **Goal:** Input events from the winit backend (and later libinput) reach the focused pane. The layout tree supports splits and tag-based visibility.
> 
> ### Files
> 
> | File | Action | Description |
> |---|---|---|
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/input.rs` | Create | `InputHandler` — receives raw input events from the winit backend (keyboard, mouse). Resolves key bindings (in-process — no IPC). Determines focus target from pointer position + layout tree. Translates to `CompToClient::Key` / `CompToClient::Mouse` and sends to the focused pane's thread via its command channel |
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/layout.rs` | Rewrite | Full layout tree: `LayoutNode` enum with `Leaf(PaneId)` and `Split { orientation, ratio, children }`. Tag bitmask per pane (`u32` — 32 tags, matching dwm). Methods: `visible_panes(active_tags: u32) -> Vec<(PaneId, Rect)>`, `pane_at_point(x, y) -> Option<PaneId>`, `split(target: PaneId, orientation, ratio)`, `close(target: PaneId)`, `resize_split(split_id, new_ratio)`. The tree computes pixel rects from the output size and the split ratios |
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/keybinds.rs` | Create | Key binding table. Hardcoded initially (vi-style: `Mod+h/j/k/l` for focus, `Mod+Shift+h/j/k/l` for move, `Mod+1-9` for tags, `Mod+Enter` for new pane, `Mod+Shift+c` for close). Matches against incoming key events before dispatch to panes |
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/state.rs` | Modify | Add `focused_pane: Option<PaneId>`, `active_tags: u32`. Input events from winit backend flow through `InputHandler` |
> | `crates/pane-comp/src/main.rs` | Modify | Handle `WinitEvent::Input` — currently ignored. Route through `InputHandler`. Wire up winit keyboard/mouse events |
> 
> ### Key Decisions
> 
> **Tag bitmask, not named workspaces.** This is the dwm model and it's the right one. A u32 gives 32 tags. Each pane has a bitmask. The compositor displays panes whose bitmask intersects with the active tag set. Viewing multiple tags is `active_tags |= tag_bit`. Simple, fast, composable.
> 
> **Input dispatch goes through the pane thread, not directly to the client.** Main thread resolves focus and keybindings. If the input is for a pane, it sends `PaneCommand::Input(CompToClient::Key {...})` to the pane thread. The pane thread then sends it to the client via the dispatcher. This adds one channel hop but maintains the invariant that pane threads are the sole conduit for per-pane protocol messages.
> 
> **Focus follows pointer for tiled panes.** Click-to-focus for floating panes (when those exist). This is a policy decision that can be made configurable later, but having a default lets us test.
> 
> **Winit backend provides synthetic input.** On the winit backend, keyboard/mouse events come from the host window system, not from libinput. This is fine for development. The `InputHandler` accepts input from either source through a common type. libinput integration is a separate concern (needed for DRM backend, not for winit).
> 
> ### Acceptance Criteria
> 
> 1. Type on a focused pane. Key events arrive at the client's `PaneEvent::Key` handler.
> 2. `Mod+Enter` creates a new pane (compositor handles the binding, sends `CreatePane` internally or triggers a configured launch action).
> 3. `Mod+Shift+c` closes the focused pane.
> 4. `Mod+1` through `Mod+9` switch tags. Panes on the selected tag become visible; others hide.
> 5. Two panes split horizontally. Resizing the split (via keybinding or mouse drag on split handle) changes the ratio.
> 6. Focus indicator moves when `Mod+h/j/k/l` changes focus.
> 
> ### Tests
> 
> - [Linux] Integration: key event round-trip. Connect client, create pane, press key in compositor window, verify `PaneEvent::Key` received by client.
> - [Linux] Layout: create 3 panes, verify they tile correctly. Close the middle one, verify the remaining two fill the space.
> - [Linux] Tags: create panes on tags 1 and 2. Switch to tag 2. Verify only tag-2 panes are visible (rendered).
> - [cross-platform] Unit: layout tree arithmetic. Given an output rect and a series of split operations, verify computed pane rects are correct. This can be a pure Rust test, no smithay needed.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Cross-Cutting Concerns
> 
> ### Socket Path Discovery
> 
> The socket path needs to be agreed between compositor and client. Convention:
> 
> ```
> $PANE_SOCKET                  # explicit override (testing)
> $XDG_RUNTIME_DIR/pane-$DISPLAY  # standard path
> ```
> 
> This should be a shared function in `pane-proto` or a new tiny `pane-common` crate, since both sides need it.
> 
> ### Error Handling Philosophy
> 
> Compositor must never panic on client misbehavior. Every path from client input to compositor action goes through:
> 1. Deserialization (postcard — returns `Err` on malformed data)
> 2. Validation (PaneId exists, operation is legal in current state)
> 3. Action (state mutation, rendering effect)
> 
> A malformed message drops the connection. An invalid but well-formed message gets an error response or is ignored. The compositor logs and continues.
> 
> ### Graceful Degradation Path
> 
> If a pane thread panics (bug in the compositor, not the client): catch it with `std::panic::catch_unwind` in the thread spawn wrapper. Clean up the pane's state. Send `Close` to the client. Log the panic at `error` level. Continue running.
> 
> If the dispatcher thread panics: all panes for that connection are orphaned. Main thread detects via channel disconnect on the `calloop::channel`, cleans up all affected panes.
> 
> ### Build/Test Infrastructure
> 
> pane-comp is Linux-only and outside the workspace default members. The test workflow:
> 
> ```
> # On macOS: develop pane-app, pane-proto, pane-session
> cargo test
> 
> # On Linux: full stack including compositor
> cargo test -p pane-comp
> cargo test -p pane-app --features integration  # real-compositor tests
> ```
> 
> The integration tests need a way to start pane-comp as a subprocess. Consider a `pane-test-harness` crate or a test fixture that:
> 1. Builds pane-comp
> 2. Starts it with a temporary socket path
> 3. Connects pane-app clients
> 4. Runs assertions
> 5. Shuts down cleanly
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Milestone: pane-shell Running Inside pane-comp
> 
> After all 5 stages, the acceptance test for the milestone is:
> 
> 1. Start pane-comp (winit backend for development).
> 2. A terminal emulator pane (pane-shell) connects via `App::connect()`.
> 3. The session handshake completes.
> 4. The compositor renders chrome (tag line with shell title, beveled borders).
> 5. The shell renders its body (terminal content — initially a placeholder grid, later a real PTY).
> 6. Typing in the compositor window sends key events to the shell pane.
> 7. The shell responds (command execution, output rendering).
> 8. Multiple shell panes tile correctly.
> 9. Tag switching hides/shows panes.
> 
> pane-shell itself is a separate implementation effort (terminal emulation, PTY management, the grid model), but it consumes the APIs this plan builds. The minimal pane-shell for this milestone: a pane-app client that creates a pane, receives key events, and sends `SetContent` with a cell grid that echoes what was typed. Not a real terminal — just proof that the pipes connect.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Stage Dependency Graph
> 
> ```
> Stage 1 (calloop skeleton)
>     |
>     v
> Stage 2 (pane protocol server)
>     |
>     v
> Stage 3 (per-pane threading)
>    / \
>   v   v
> Stage 4 (chrome)    Stage 5 (input + layout)
> ```
> 
> Stages 4 and 5 are independent of each other and can proceed in parallel once Stage 3 is complete. Stage 4 is visual; Stage 5 is interactive. Both need the threading model in place because they interact with pane threads (chrome reads layout tree that pane threads also read; input sends commands to pane threads).
> 
> In practice, I'd interleave 4 and 5 because a compositor that renders but doesn't accept input is hard to test, and one that accepts input but doesn't render is equally awkward. The natural development loop: get basic input working (focus, one keybinding), get basic chrome rendering working (one pane), iterate.
> 
> ---
>

> 
> | Stage | New/modified files | Approximate lines | Calendar estimate |

> | 4: Chrome | 4 | ~500 | 2-3 days |
> | 5: Input + layout | 4-5 | ~700 | 3-5 days |
> | **Total** | ~20 files | ~2800 lines | 2-3 weeks |
> 
> This is the compositor's real birth. Everything before was demo scaffolding. After this, pane-comp is a functioning compositor that speaks the pane protocol, manages a layout tree, renders chrome, and dispatches input — the three-tier threading model from the spec, verified against real client connections.</result>

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a8ab9ddc2c02a7558</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01AR6PZf4vsdvTnTufaAisEu</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/fd904e50-fb1c-459f-8adf-ecbbcfa19341/tasks/a8ab9ddc2c02a7558.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Search cross-platform Nix Rust dev" completed</summary>
> <result>Research written to `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/research-cross-platform-dev.md`.
> 
> **The short version:** True cross-compilation from darwin to linux is a dead end for pane-comp. The Wayland/Smithay dependency chain (wayland, libinput, mesa, udev, libdrm, seatd) is Linux kernel-coupled -- nixpkgs cannot provide a darwin-hosted cross-sysroot for these libraries. Both cosmic-comp and niri handle this by simply not supporting macOS development of their compositor at all.
> 
> **Key findings across the five approaches:**
> 
> 1. **nix-darwin linux-builder** -- already in place, works, slow (QEMU without Rosetta). Good for CI gating, not for iteration.
> 
> 2. **Determinate Nix native builder** -- uses macOS Virtualization.framework, but requires Determinate Nix (incompatible with Lix), and their own docs say `cpuCount > 1` is slower than 1. No advantage over option 1.
> 
> 3. **True cross-compilation** -- blocked by native C dependencies. Works only for pure-Rust crates.
> 
> 4. **cross-rs/Docker** -- would require a custom Dockerfile duplicating all the Nix-managed deps. Docker on macOS is a Linux VM anyway.
> 
> 5. **Flakebox** -- same cross-compilation blocker as option 3.
> 
> **Recommended path:** Optimize the existing VM workflow (persistent cargo cache, pre-warmed toolchain, better `just` recipes), use the linux-builder for CI-style `nix flake check`, and consider a dedicated aarch64-linux remote builder for native-speed builds long-term.</result>

### `2026-03-28T02:06:49.824Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a7563d29241493e6f</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_019itbsVc8CtKroV4gRR12x9</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/fd904e50-fb1c-459f-8adf-ecbbcfa19341/tasks/a7563d29241493e6f.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Haiku app kit tests → pane test plan" completed</summary>
> <result>Good. The test plan is accurate — the `from_comp` method handles all those variants, but the test file only tests Resize, Key, Close, CommandExecuted, and PaneCreated-returns-None. The gaps I identified (Focus, Blur, Mouse, CommandActivated, CommandDismissed, CompletionRequest) are real.
> 
> The test plan is at `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/changes/spec-tightening/test-plan-from-haiku.md`.
> 
> Here's the summary of what I found:
> 
> **Haiku's Application Kit test suite contains ~200 test cases across 8 subsystems.** The tests are methodical: every public method gets a null/invalid input test, a valid input test, a looper-locked/unlocked test, and a threading test. This discipline caught real segfaults in BeOS R5 — the Haiku source is full of `#ifndef TEST_R5` guards around null-parameter tests that R5 couldn't handle.
> 
> **pane currently has 19 tests across pane-app**, covering the happy paths well but missing almost all error conditions and edge cases. The three biggest gaps are:
> 
> 1. **PaneHandle has zero tests.** It's the BMessenger equivalent — the most-used API surface for developers — and there's no test that it sends the right messages, handles disconnection, or works correctly when cloned across threads.
> 
> 2. **Filter chain has one test.** Haiku tests null filters, filter ownership, filter-on-unlocked-looper, filter already owned by another looper, and bulk filter replacement. pane tests one happy-path consume.
> 
> 3. **Handler defaults are untested.** The trait has 12 methods with default implementations. No test verifies that `close_requested` defaults to `Ok(false)` or that `key` defaults to `Ok(true)`. This is exactly the kind of thing that breaks when someone refactors the trait.
> 
> **pane-session and pane-proto are well-tested** — the session-type crash recovery tests and proptest roundtrips are better than what Haiku had for the equivalent layer.
>

> 
> **19 findings across all four crates:**
> 
> **4 Critical:**
> - **C-1** — Truncating `as u32` cast in `write_framed` (two locations) silently corrupts frames for oversized messages. The `frame()` function in the same file does this correctly — the other call sites bypass the safety.
> - **C-2** — Framing code duplicated between pane-proto and pane-session. The Gassee DSP lesson: two implementations of the same thing is one too many.
> - **C-3** — `extract_pane_id` returns `Option<u64>` but never returns `None`. The Owen Smith lesson: a type that lies about its contract. Should be `CompToClient::pane_id(&self) -> PaneId`.
> - **C-4** — Race between PaneCreated response and pane channel registration. Events sent between those moments are silently dropped. The mock's 200ms sleep confirms the race exists.
> 
> **7 Moderate** — most actionable for Phase 4: the `HashMap<u64, _>` that should be `HashMap<PaneId, _>`, the `write_message` that blocks in non-blocking mode, unconditional RequestClose on exit, leaked stub watcher threads, `from_comp` cloning on every event dispatch, the Ready event that never fires, and Vec-based attribute lookup.
> 
> **8 Minor** — documentation, derive gaps, test coverage gaps.
> 
> **Overall:** The architecture is sound. The layering (proto/session/notify/app) is correct. The BLooper translation is faithful. PaneHandle is a clean BMessenger. Session types for handshake + typed enums for active phase is the right split. The critical findings are all fixable without design changes — they're implementation bugs, not architecture problems.</result>

> 1. Primary Request and Intent:
>    The user (Lane) is building "pane" — a BeOS-inspired desktop environment, Wayland compositor, and Linux distribution. The project draws from BeOS's message-passing discipline, Plan 9's protocol uniformity, and session type theory. The conversation covers the complete lifecycle from specification through implementation:
>    - Refining the theoretical foundations and architecture specs
>    - Building the core crate infrastructure (proto, session, notify, app)

> 2. Key Technical Concepts:
>    - Custom session types: `Chan<S, T>` typestate pattern with pluggable transport (not par crate - par's types and runtime are inseparable, can't cross process boundaries)
>    - Three-phase protocol model: session-typed handshake → typed enum active phase → session-typed teardown (informed by Maty/Fowler-Hu OOPSLA paper)
>    - Isomorphic packaging principle: views are isomorphic packagings of the same data, graded by monadic permission composition
>    - Compositional equivalence: composition structure preserved across all views (splits are directories in /pane/)
>    - Per-pane server-side threading (faithful to BeOS ServerWindow model)
>    - Tag line as modal command surface (not acme-style persistent text)
>    - PaneHandle (renamed from PaneProxy) as BMessenger equivalent
>    - ExitReason enum distinguishing HandlerExit/CompositorClose/Disconnected
>    - Local-first AI with routing rules as data governance
>    - Agent memory as filesystem + pane-store (not a separate database)
>    - Vector similarity search in pane-store (HNSW, threshold-based live queries not top-k)
>    - btrfs exclusively (ext4 dropped), s6 specifically (not abstraction), Landlock primary sandboxing
>    - FUSE-over-io_uring (custom module, no existing Rust lib supports it)
>    - Vello (not femtovg, not iced-rs) for widget rendering
>    - smithay for compositor (validated - don't build from scratch)
>    - Cross-build workflow: `nix build .#packages.aarch64-linux.pane-comp` via linux-builder

> 5. Problem Solving:
>    - Cross-compilation from macOS to Linux: researched extensively, concluded true cross-compilation is blocked by Wayland's kernel-coupled deps. Solution: linux-builder for builds, VM for visual testing
>    - Session type library choice: par rejected (channel-passing runtime can't cross process boundaries, panics on drop), dialectic rejected (async dependency). Built custom `Chan<S, T>` with transport-aware, crash-safe design
>    - Nondeterminism in active phase: resolved via Maty paper analysis — decompose into typed enums for bidirectional phase, session types for structured phases
>    - iced-rs for widgets: rejected — structural mismatch (wants to own event loop, no clean buffer rendering path, per-pane threading unsupported)

> <task-notification>

> <tool-use-id>toolu_01GCkpdwa38o6o3UrF3iXgKp</tool-use-id>

> <result>Good. I have a thorough picture now. Let me compose the answer.
> 
> ---
> 
> These are great questions. Let me work through them from the perspective of someone who actually lived in the Be codebase and now sees the same structural challenges showing up in pane.
> 
> ## Question 1: Which serena features map to Be's critical development patterns?
> 
> **Finding all dispatch sites: `find_referencing_symbols` is the direct analog, but the real story is more interesting.**
> 
> When you look at Haiku's `ServerWindow.cpp` (4,631 lines), the core dispatch pattern is a giant `switch` on protocol opcodes (`AS_SHOW_OR_HIDE_WINDOW`, `AS_MINIMIZE_WINDOW`, `AS_ACTIVATE_WINDOW`, etc.) split across four methods:
> 
> - `_DispatchMessage()` — window-level messages (show/hide, activate, move, resize)
> - `_DispatchViewMessage()` — view-level messages needing a valid `fCurrentView`
> - `_DispatchViewDrawingMessage()` — drawing primitives
> - `_DispatchPictureMessage()` — picture recording
> 
> At Be, when you added a new protocol message code, you had to update the switch in the right dispatch method, add the `AS_*` constant in the protocol header, add the client-side send call in the kit (BWindow, BView), and — this is the part people forgot — update `_MessageNeedsAllWindowsLocked()` if the new message touched cross-window state. That last function at line 4523 of `ServerWindow.cpp` is a manually maintained whitelist of opcodes that require the write lock instead of the read lock on the Desktop's window list. Miss it, and you get a race condition that might take weeks to reproduce.
> 
> `find_referencing_symbols` would have caught the obvious ones — "who calls this type/function?" But the *real* danger was the things that *should* reference a symbol but don't yet. The manually maintained opcode whitelist is a classic case: a new opcode is created, added to the dispatch switch, but nobody adds it to the lock-classification function. No tool that finds *existing* references helps you find *missing* references.
> 
> **For pane specifically**: You're in a much better position because Rust's `match` exhaustiveness catches the "missing arm" case. Your `handle_message()` in `server.rs` matches on `ClientToComp` — if you add a variant, the compiler forces you to handle it. This is something we would have killed for. But serena's `find_referencing_symbols` is still essential for the cases the compiler can't see: when you need to know every place that *constructs* a `ClientToComp` variant (the kit side), every place that dispatches a `CompToClient` variant, etc.
> 
> **Renaming**: At Be, renaming a public API type was essentially forbidden after a release shipped. Binary compatibility was sacrosanct — that's why you see those `_ReservedHandler2()`, `_ReservedHandler3()`, `_ReservedHandler4()` virtual method slots in every public class header (`Handler.h` has three of them, plus `uint32 _reserved[3]` for data padding). The cost of not being able to rename was paid in reserved vtable slots and data padding that had to be budgeted at design time. Haiku faithfully reproduces this pattern.
> 
> `rename_symbol` is a luxury we never had. For pane, you're pre-1.0, which means you should be renaming aggressively *now* — use serena's semantic rename to keep names honest. Once you commit to a stable protocol format, that window closes. The lesson from Be is: the cost of a bad name grows exponentially with time.
> 
> **Subclass updates for BHandler**: Every class that inherited from BHandler had to implement `MessageReceived()` and was supposed to call the base class for messages it didn't handle. The newsletter from Issue 4-35 (Owen Smith's article on single-threaded message handling) explicitly discusses how the per-looper threading model creates hazards when porting code. The chain-of-responsibility pattern meant that `find_referencing_symbols` on `MessageReceived` would give you every override, but it couldn't tell you whether each override remembered to call `inherited::MessageReceived()` in the default case. Again: the bug is the *absent* call, not the present one.
> 
> In pane, you've already solved this architecturally. Your `Handler` trait has default implementations that return `Ok(true)` (continue), and your `PaneEvent` enum is exhaustive. The dispatch in `looper.rs` handles the mapping. There's no "forgotten base class call" failure mode. That's good design learned from our mistakes.
> 
> ## Question 2: Which serena features would have prevented newsletter-documented bugs?
> 
> The newsletters documented several recurring categories of API evolution hazard:
> 
> **1. The protocol opcode coverage problem.** New `AS_*` codes added to the protocol had to be handled in multiple places: dispatch, lock classification, potentially the profiling infrastructure (`sMessageProfile[AS_LAST_CODE]` at line 129 of ServerWindow.cpp). `find_referencing_symbols` on each new opcode constant would have caught where it was referenced, but you still needed human discipline to ask "where *should* it also appear?" `search_for_pattern` with a regex like `AS_SET_FEEL|AS_SET_LOOK|AS_SET_FLAGS` to find "sibling" opcodes and verify the new one appears everywhere its siblings do — that's the actually useful workflow. Pattern search as consistency audit.
> 
> **2. Binary compatibility / vtable layout.** Pavel Cisler's article in Issue 4-48 describes the technique of runtime-loading symbols to use new APIs while staying compatible with older releases. The `_Reserved*` virtual methods and `_reserved` data fields in every public class were our answer to "how do you add functionality without breaking the ABI." `get_symbols_overview` on public headers would have been useful for auditing: does every public class have sufficient reserved slots? Are we running low anywhere?
> 
> For pane, you don't have this problem because your protocol is message-based (serde/bincode), not vtable-based. Adding a variant to `ClientToComp` is a protocol evolution question, not a binary layout question. Your strategy should be: use `search_for_pattern` to audit that every `ClientToComp` variant has a matching `CompToClient` response where appropriate, and that every `CompToClient` variant has a `PaneEvent` mapping in `event.rs`. Serena can verify structural completeness of your protocol coverage.
> 
> **3. The "forgotten dispatch" class.** In the app_server, there were effectively three layers of dispatch: `_DispatchMessage` -> `_DispatchViewMessage` -> `_DispatchViewDrawingMessage`. A message could fall through all three and be silently dropped. The Haiku code has DTRACE macros as a debugging crutch, but there's no compile-time guarantee. `get_symbols_overview` on ServerWindow.cpp would show you the structure of these dispatch methods; `find_referencing_symbols` would tell you which gets called from where. But the real fix, which you've already done, is Rust's exhaustive matching.
> 
> ## Question 3: Serena capabilities to lean on during compositor development
> 
> Your compositor right now is structurally at the stage Haiku's app_server was in DarkWyrm's early days — the plumbing works but the personality is thin. Based on what I see in `state.rs` and `server.rs`, here's what I'd lean on:
> 
> **`find_referencing_symbols` — religiously, on protocol types.** Every time you add a `ClientToComp` or `CompToClient` variant, use this to verify all the places that construct, match on, serialize, and deserialize it are updated. The Haiku app_server's biggest maintenance burden was exactly this: a new protocol message was "done" in the dispatch function but not in the lock classification, not in the profiling, not in the documentation. Let serena tell you who references `ClientToComp::SetVocabulary` and verify that chain is complete from kit through compositor.
> 
> **`get_symbols_overview` — for maintaining structural clarity as `CompState` grows.** Right now `CompState` is manageable: backend, output, size, atlas, renderer, server, handshakes. But the Haiku `Desktop` class — the equivalent — became a 4,000+ line monster because responsibilities accreted. Use `get_symbols_overview` on your key types regularly to notice when they're growing too many methods. ServerWindow's header alone shows the accretion: 30+ methods, multiple dispatch functions, multiple lock states. You want to notice that trajectory early and factor out.
> 
> **`search_for_pattern` — for lock protocol auditing.** The most important function in Haiku's ServerWindow is `_MessageNeedsAllWindowsLocked()` at line 4523 — a manually maintained whitelist that determines whether a message needs the read lock or write lock. In pane, your equivalent is the question "does this message need exclusive CompState access?" Right now you're single-threaded via calloop, so this isn't urgent, but when you add the per-pane threads (which you will — it's in the architecture spec), you'll need to audit which messages can be handled with shared access versus exclusive access. `search_for_pattern` for `&mut self` versus `&self` on your message handling methods will be your first-order check.
> 
> **`rename_symbol` — while the API is still fluid.** Your protocol types and handler methods are the API surface that third-party pane developers will code against. Rename now while the cost is zero. Once real apps exist, you're locked in. I'd specifically audit the `Handler` trait method names against the `PaneEvent` enum variants — right now `PaneEvent::Blur` maps to `Handler::blurred()`, `PaneEvent::Focus` maps to `Handler::focused()`. Make sure those naming conventions are ones you want to live with for a decade.
> 
> ## Question 4: What serena CAN'T help with
> 
> **Locking discipline and state access protocols.** The Haiku ServerWindow message loop (line ~4296) has a sophisticated dance: it checks `_MessageNeedsAllWindowsLocked()`, acquires the appropriate lock (read vs. write on the Desktop), processes up to 70 messages, and ensures it doesn't hold the lock for more than 10ms. This is a *policy* encoded in control flow, not in type signatures. No amount of reference-finding or symbol-searching catches the bug where you hold the wrong lock for too long, or process a message that should have had the write lock under the read lock. Serena can find the *references*, but the *correctness* of the locking protocol requires human reasoning about invariants.
> 
> In pane, this maps to the calloop ownership model. Right now `CompState` is passed as `&mut` to every callback, which is the Rust equivalent of holding the global lock. When you split to per-pane threads, the question of who can touch what state, through what channel, with what latency guarantee, is a design problem, not a search problem.
> 
> **Message coalescing policy.** Your `drain_and_coalesce()` in `looper.rs` already implements Be's pattern (keep last resize, keep last mouse move). But the *policy* of what to coalesce is a design judgment. The Haiku app_server had a similar batch-processing limit (70 messages, 10ms time budget). Getting these numbers right is empirical — you feel it in the UI responsiveness. Serena can tell you where coalescing happens; it can't tell you whether the policy is correct.
> 
> **Cross-component protocol consistency.** The deepest bugs at Be were the ones where the client-side kit (BWindow) and the server-side dispatcher (ServerWindow) had subtly different assumptions about a protocol message's semantics. For example: BWindow sends `AS_WINDOW_RESIZE` with a width/height, but ServerWindow interprets it as a delta in some paths and an absolute position in others. The *structure* was consistent (serena could verify both sides reference the same type), but the *semantic contract* was ambiguous. Your protocol is better here because serde gives you structural agreement for free — but you still need human judgment about what `SetContent { content: Vec<u8> }` actually *means* at each layer.
> 
> **Prioritization of protocol extension.** When you add a new message type, the question isn't "did I handle it everywhere?" (serena answers that) but "should this be a message at all, or should it be state that's queried?" At Be, we got this wrong sometimes — things that should have been queryable attributes were encoded as messages, and things that should have been messages were encoded as shared memory. The scripting protocol (`ResolveSpecifier` / `property_info`) was supposed to unify this, but it was added late and adoption was uneven. Your optics design for pane is trying to solve this properly. Serena can't tell you whether a given piece of state belongs in the protocol vocabulary or in the scripting/query layer — that's an architecture call.
> 
> **The emotional weight of the frame budget.** You have 16ms per frame in your timer callback, and right now you're doing `poll_handshakes()` + `process_client_messages()` + `render_frame()` in that window. The Haiku ServerWindow had a profiling mode (`PROFILE_MESSAGE_LOOP`) that tracked time per message type, with a hard 10ms budget for batch processing before releasing the lock. No tool tells you when your compositor is starting to miss frames because one client is chatty. You need telemetry — wall-clock timing on the message processing path — and you need to build it in now while the code is small. That's a human judgment call about what to measure.</result>

> 
> Here is the full research synthesis.
> 
> ## Project-by-Project Findings
> 
> ### 1. niri (niri-wm/niri)
> 
> **Workspace structure:** 3 workspace members — `niri-config`, `niri-ipc`, `niri-visual-tests` — plus the main `niri` binary. The separation is clean: config parsing, IPC types, and visual testing each get their own crate. Everything else lives in the main `niri` crate. No separate "server" or "protocol" crate.
> 
> **macOS support:** None. Linux-only across the board. No Darwin targets in CI or flake.
> 
> **Testing strategy (the most interesting part):**
> - **Unit tests:** `cargo test --all --exclude niri-visual-tests` — they explicitly exclude the visual test crate from automated runs.
> - **Property-based tests:** Layout code uses proptest with `RUN_SLOW_TESTS=1` gating. Full runs use `PROPTEST_CASES=200000`. This is very similar to what pane-proto already does.
> - **Visual tests:** `niri-visual-tests` is a standalone GTK4/Libadwaita application that renders test cases using mock windows with the real layout + rendering code. It is NOT an automated test — a developer runs it and visually inspects animations/layouts. Extremely useful for compositor dev, but manual.
> - **Development iteration:** Primary workflow is "run as nested window" via the winit backend, then test on a real TTY. The `Mod` key automatically remaps from Super to Alt when running nested to avoid conflicts with the host compositor.
> 
> **CI:** Ubuntu 24.04/26.04, Alpine, Fedora, FreeBSD (via VM action). No macOS. Tests skip EGL in sandbox. FreeBSD uses `vmactions/freebsd-vm@v1`. Visual tests compile but don't execute in CI.
> 
> **Nix flake:** Uses `rustPlatform.buildRustPackage` (NOT crane). Nightly Rust in devShell with `cargo-insta`. Custom RUSTFLAGS to force-link libEGL/libwayland for dlopen. Sets `XDG_RUNTIME_DIR` for tests. The community `niri-flake` (sodiboo) also uses `rustPlatform.buildRustPackage`, explicitly skips `--skip=::egl` tests.
> 
> ### 2. cosmic-comp (pop-os/cosmic-comp)
> 
> **Workspace structure:** Minimal — just `cosmic-comp-config` as a workspace member alongside the main crate. The COSMIC ecosystem spreads functionality across many separate repos (`cosmic-panel`, `cosmic-settings`, etc.) rather than a monorepo.
> 
> **macOS support:** None in cosmic-comp itself. However, `nixos-cosmic` declares outputs for `x86_64-darwin` and `aarch64-darwin` in its `forAllSystems`, though the compositor packages themselves are Linux-only — the Darwin outputs are likely for tooling/client packages.
> 
> **Testing:** `cargo test --all-features` in CI on Ubuntu. No headless or VM testing in the compositor's own CI. Very minimal test infrastructure.
> 
> **CI:** Single Ubuntu job. Format, clippy, feature matrix check (no-default, debug, tracy, tracy-gpu). Builds libseat from source. No sophisticated test patterns.
> 
> **Nix:** `nixos-cosmic` uses `rustPlatform.buildRustPackage` with `useFetchCargoVendor = true`. Builds via `make` (not cargo directly). A custom `libcosmicAppHook` handles COSMIC-specific wrapping. The `rustPlatformFor` function conditionally selects Rust toolchains based on version requirements.
> 
> **VM testing (this is the gold):** `nixos-cosmic` provides `legacyPackages.${system}.vm` — a full NixOS VM configuration with:
> - COSMIC desktop enabled
> - 4GB RAM, `virtio-gpu-gl-pci` for GPU acceleration
> - Host Nix store mounted for efficient iteration
> - SSH on port 2222
> - Both `vm` (unstable) and `vm-stable` (24.11) variants
> - `closure` attribute for CI checks
> 
> This is conceptually identical to pane's VM approach but more polished. The key difference: they mount the **Nix store** rather than the project source, so iteration happens through `nix build` rather than `cargo build` inside the VM.
> 
> ### 3. anvil (smithay's example compositor)
> 
> **Structure:** Part of the smithay workspace. Backends are feature-gated: `winit`, `x11`, `udev`. Default features: `egl, winit, x11, udev, xwayland`.
> 
> **Development workflow:** `cargo run -- --winit` for nested compositor testing. `--x11` for X11 backend. `--tty-udev` for real hardware. Environment variables control DRM device, 10-bit, scanout, etc.
> 
> **WLCS integration (crucial finding):** smithay has `wlcs_anvil` — a cdylib that integrates anvil with Canonical's Wayland Conformance Test Suite. The key architecture:
> - Uses `DummyRenderer::default()` — a zero-work renderer that implements all the Renderer traits but produces no pixels
> - Creates a `Display::new()` and `AnvilState::init()` with a minimal `TestState` backend
> - Sets up a virtual 800x600 output at 60Hz
> - Processes Wayland client connections through channels
> - Runs WLCS conformance tests categories: `SelfTest*`, `FrameSubmission*`, pointer input, buffer handling
> - **This is effectively a headless compositor** — full protocol compliance testing with no GPU
> 
> **CI:** Runs WLCS tests in GitHub Actions on Ubuntu. Coverage via grcov reported to codecov. Feature matrix with `cargo hack check --each-feature`.
> 
> ### 4. jay (mahkoh/jay)
> 
> **Structure:** 6 workspace members: `jay-config`, `toml-config`, `algorithms`, `toml-spec`, `wire-to-xml`, `xml-to-wire`. More crates than the others. The `it` feature enables integration testing.
> 
> **No nix flake** (404 on flake.nix). No macOS support. No sophisticated CI found.
> 
> **Testing:** Has a "small but growing integration test suite" but details are sparse. The `it` Cargo feature gates integration tests, which is a clean pattern.
> 
> ### 5. smithay itself
> 
> **Key testing infrastructure:**
> 
> - **`DummyRenderer`** (`renderer_test` feature): Zero-sized struct implementing `Renderer`, `ImportMem`, `ImportDma`, `ImportEgl`, etc. All operations are no-ops. This is the headless testing primitive.
> - **`renderer_pixman`**: Full software renderer using pixman. Renders real pixels without GPU. Suitable for CI environments without hardware acceleration.
> - **`test_all_features`**: Aggregates default features + `use_system_lib` + `renderer_glow` + `renderer_test` for comprehensive testing.
> - **Winit backend**: Run-as-window for nested development. Cross-platform (theoretically supports macOS via winit, but smithay's Wayland deps prevent this in practice).
> 
> **The cocoa-way project** demonstrates that smithay *can* build on macOS for a very specific use case (receiving Wayland protocol over waypipe, rendering via Metal/OpenGL), but it's a specialized fork approach, not general-purpose macOS support.
> 
> ## Synthesis: Patterns Across All Projects
> 
> ### Universal patterns
> 1. **Everyone uses `rustPlatform.buildRustPackage`** — not crane, not rust-flake. Niri, cosmic-comp, niri-flake all use the same basic Nix builder. Crane is not the industry choice for compositor projects.
> 2. **Winit backend for dev iteration** — every project that uses smithay runs nested via winit during development. This is THE dev loop.
> 3. **Linux-only testing** — no project does macOS CI for compositor code. Zero.
> 4. **Feature-gated platform separation** — backends behind cargo features, not separate crates.
> 5. **Tests skip GPU-dependent code** — EGL tests skipped in sandboxes, visual tests excluded from CI runs.
> 
> ### Patterns worth stealing
> 
> | Pattern | Source | Value for pane |
> |---------|--------|---------------|
> | `DummyRenderer` for headless protocol tests | smithay/WLCS | Test pane-server protocol handling without GPU |
> | `niri-visual-tests` visual inspection app | niri | Manual animation/layout validation tool |
> | `--exclude` visual tests from CI | niri | Keep CI fast, visual tests manual |
> | `RUN_SLOW_TESTS` gating | niri | Already doing this with proptest |
> | VM with `virtio-gpu-gl-pci` | nixos-cosmic | Already doing this |
> | Feature flag for integration tests (`it`) | jay | Gate expensive tests behind a feature |
> | `default-members` in Cargo.toml | (missing from all, but needed) | **Fix for pane's macOS build failure** |
> | WLCS conformance testing | smithay/anvil | Protocol correctness validation |
> 
> ## Concrete Recommendations for Pane
> 
> ### Immediate fix (quick win)
> 
> **Add `default-members` to `Cargo.toml`** to exclude `pane-comp` from the default workspace build. Right now `cargo check` on macOS fails because it tries to build pane-comp. Every other project solves this via feature flags on a single crate, but pane's multi-crate design calls for:
> 
> ```toml
> [workspace]
> resolver = "2"
> members = ["crates/pane-proto", "crates/pane-session", "crates/pane-notify", "crates/pane-app", "crates/pane-server", "crates/pane-comp", "crates/pane-hello"]
> default-members = ["crates/pane-proto", "crates/pane-session", "crates/pane-notify", "crates/pane-app", "crates/pane-server", "crates/pane-hello"]
> ```
> 
> Then `cargo check` / `cargo test` on macOS works for all platform-independent crates. `cargo build -p pane-comp` remains explicit.
> 
> ### Workflow alignment
> 
> **Your current approach is already close to best practice.** The VM workflow (`just vm-fresh`, `just vm-ssh`, `just vm-dev`) with 9p mount is essentially the nixos-cosmic pattern with one difference: you mount project source for `cargo build` inside the VM, while nixos-cosmic mounts the Nix store for `nix build` outputs. Your approach is actually better for fast iteration since you avoid Nix rebuild times.
> 
> **What you're doing that nobody else does (and it's fine):**
> - Separate `pane-server` crate with no rendering deps — this is architecturally cleaner than niri/cosmic-comp's monolithic approach and enables protocol-level testing on macOS
> - Using rust-flake/crane for the Nix build — works fine, but you may hit friction since the compositor ecosystem standardized on `rustPlatform.buildRustPackage`. If rust-flake causes issues, switching is low-cost.
> 
> ### Testing infrastructure to build
> 
> 1. **Headless protocol tests using `DummyRenderer`** — smithay's WLCS anvil proves this works. You can test the pane-server protocol handling, client connection lifecycle, message routing, etc. against a `DummyRenderer` backend with no GPU. This runs on macOS or CI without a display.
> 
> 2. **Add smithay features to pane-comp for test flexibility:**
>    ```toml
>    [features]
>    default = ["winit", "glow"]
>    winit = ["smithay/backend_winit"]
>    test-renderer = ["smithay/renderer_test"]
>    pixman = ["smithay/renderer_pixman"]
>    ```
>    The `test-renderer` feature gives you `DummyRenderer` for headless integration tests. The `pixman` feature gives you real software rendering in CI without GPU.
> 
> 3. **A `pane-test-clients` crate** (inspired by smithay's `test_clients` workspace member) for integration tests that connect to a headless pane-comp and verify protocol behavior.
> 
> ### CI recommendations
> 
> Based on what niri and smithay do:
> - **GitHub Actions on Ubuntu** for the Linux-only crates (pane-comp, integration tests)
> - **macOS job for platform-independent crates** (pane-proto, pane-session, pane-notify, pane-app, pane-server) — you'd be the ONLY compositor project doing this, but your architecture enables it because you extracted pane-server
> - **Skip EGL/GPU tests in CI** with `--skip` patterns or feature flags
> - **`cargo hack check --each-feature`** (from smithay's CI) to verify feature combinations compile
> 
> ### What NOT to do
> 
> - Don't switch to `rustPlatform.buildRustPackage` just because everyone else uses it. rust-flake/crane gives you incremental caching which is valuable for the dev loop. The switch only makes sense if you hit real friction.
> - Don't try to make smithay build on macOS. Nobody does it. The `pane-server` extraction was the right architectural move — it gets you macOS development for everything except rendering.
> - Don't build an automated visual test suite yet. Niri's approach (manual GTK app) is mature and they still don't automate it. Screenshot comparison testing is fragile and not worth the investment at this stage.
> 
> ### Key files from this research
> 
> - smithay's `wlcs_anvil/src/main_loop.rs` — canonical example of headless compositor setup with `DummyRenderer`
> - smithay's `wlcs_anvil/src/lib.rs` — WLCS integration harness architecture
> - niri's `niri-visual-tests/` — visual test app pattern
> - nixos-cosmic's `flake.nix` VM infrastructure — `legacyPackages.vm` pattern with `virtio-gpu-gl-pci`
> - niri-flake's test skipping — `--skip=::egl` pattern for CI</result>

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a388b30e0cf8d0258</task-id>

> <result>I now have a comprehensive picture. Here's the full report.
> 
> ---
> 
> # virtiofs on macOS/QEMU: Research Report
> 
> ## TL;DR
> 
> **virtiofs does not work with QEMU on macOS.** The daemon (`virtiofsd`) is Linux-only, and QEMU's vhost-user transport for virtiofs depends on Linux kernel infrastructure. Your current 9p setup with `msize=104857600` is already close to the best you can do with QEMU on macOS. The only path to virtiofs on macOS is abandoning QEMU in favor of Apple's Virtualization Framework (via `vfkit`), which is a significant architectural change.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Question 1: Does QEMU on macOS support virtiofs?
> 
> **No.**
> 
> virtiofs requires two components that are Linux-only:
> 
> 1. **virtiofsd** (the host-side daemon) -- the canonical Rust implementation at [gitlab.com/virtio-fs/virtiofsd](https://gitlab.com/virtio-fs/virtiofsd) uses Linux-specific syscalls: `openat2()`, `O_PATH` file descriptors, mount namespaces, and `seccomp`. It does not compile on macOS. There is an [open issue #169](https://gitlab.com/virtio-fs/virtiofsd/-/issues/169) requesting macOS support with no implementation and no maintainer response indicating feasibility.
> 
> 2. **vhost-user transport** -- QEMU's `vhost-user-fs-pci` device communicates with virtiofsd via vhost-user, which uses Linux eventfd and shared memory mechanisms.
> 
> virtiofsd is not packaged for macOS in Homebrew, nixpkgs (darwin), or MacPorts.
> 
> ## Question 2: Does HVF support the shared memory regions virtiofs needs (DAX)?
> 
> **Almost certainly not, and it's moot anyway.**
> 
> virtiofs DAX requires a shared memory region (`memory-backend-file` backed by `/dev/shm` or `memfd`) that maps host page cache into guest address space. macOS has neither `/dev/shm` nor `memfd_create()`. HVF exposes memory mapping through `hv_vm_map()`, but the vhost-user protocol that virtiofs requires for QEMU is the actual blocker -- DAX is a secondary concern.
> 
> Even if someone ported virtiofsd to macOS, the DAX window (which provides the big performance wins -- 6-7x over non-DAX virtiofs in benchmarks) would likely be impossible without Linux-specific memory management.
> 
> ## Question 3: Has anyone used virtiofs with QEMU on macOS?
> 
> **No evidence of anyone succeeding with QEMU specifically.** However:
> 
> - [**phaer/nixos-vm-on-macos**](https://github.com/phaer/nixos-vm-on-macos) achieves virtiofs on macOS by using **vfkit** instead of QEMU. vfkit wraps Apple's Virtualization.framework, which has its own virtiofs implementation via [VZVirtioFileSystemDeviceConfiguration](https://developer.apple.com/documentation/virtualization/vzvirtiofilesystemdeviceconfiguration) (macOS 12.0+). This project mounts the host's nix store with a writable overlay via virtiofs.
> 
> - [**microvm.nix**](https://github.com/microvm-nix/microvm.nix) also uses vfkit on macOS for virtiofs, including nix store sharing with writable overlays.
> 
> - **Docker Desktop for Mac** uses virtiofs through Apple's Virtualization.framework, not through QEMU's virtiofs device.
> 
> - **UTM** supports virtiofs only when using the Apple Virtualization backend (not the QEMU backend). The QEMU backend in UTM uses VirtFS/9p for directory sharing.
> 
> The pattern is clear: virtiofs on macOS goes through Apple's framework, not QEMU.
> 
> ## Question 4: Is your current 9p msize optimal?
> 
> **Your `msize=104857600` (100MB) is at the high end and should be fine.** Here's what the research shows:
> 
> - The QEMU wiki warns if msize <= 8192, calling it "degraded performance."
> - A kernel developer (Christian Schoenebeck) confirmed successfully using `msize=104857600` on kernel 4.9.
> - Some kernels cap msize at the transport module's maxsize. On one report, a 16MB msize was capped to 512000 by the kernel. This depends on kernel version and virtio-9p transport config.
> - The 9p Darwin patches were **merged into QEMU mainline** (the Will Cohen / Keno Fischer patch series, reviewed by Paolo Bonzini). QEMU 7.1+ has native Darwin 9p support with `security_model=none` or `security_model=mapped-xattr`.
> 
> **Your current QEMU command already uses the right flags:**
> ```
> -virtfs local,path=/nix/store,security_model=none,mount_tag=nix-store,readonly=on
> ```
> And in the guest mount:
> ```
> mount -t 9p -o trans=virtio,version=9p2000.L,msize=104857600,access=any project /home/pane/pane
> ```
> 
> One thing to check: whether the kernel in your NixOS VM actually honors the 100MB msize or silently caps it. You can verify by checking `/proc/mounts` after mounting -- it will show the effective msize. If it's being capped, you'd see a smaller number.
> 
> **Performance context:** Benchmarks show that without DAX, virtiofs and 9p perform nearly identically (both ~95-100 MB/s sequential). The big wins from virtiofs come from DAX mode (660-2280 MB/s), which is Linux-host-only. So even if you could use virtiofs without DAX, you wouldn't gain much over 9p.
> 
> ## Question 5: Does UTM support virtiofs?
> 
> **Only with the Apple Virtualization backend, not with the QEMU backend.**
> 
> From the [UTM sharing docs](https://docs.getutm.app/settings-qemu/sharing/): the QEMU backend uses VirtFS (9p) for directory sharing. The Apple Virtualization backend uses virtiofs. These are distinct code paths. If you switched your VM to UTM's Apple Virtualization backend, you'd get virtiofs but lose QEMU-specific features (and would need to restructure your VM launch entirely).
> 
> ## Question 6: NFS or SSHFS alternatives?
> 
> ### NFS (viable but tricky)
> 
> macOS has a built-in NFS server (`nfsd`). You could:
> 
> 1. Add to `/etc/exports`: `/nix/store -ro -mapall=nobody -network 10.0.2.0 -mask 255.255.255.0`
> 2. Start nfsd: `sudo nfsd enable`
> 3. In the NixOS VM guest, mount via NFS
> 
> **Gotchas:**
> - macOS Catalina+ may need `/System/Volumes/Data/nix/store` as the export path
> - nfsd needs "Full Disk Access" in System Preferences if it can't read the export
> - You need the VM to have network access to the host (your current `user` networking with `-nic user` makes the host visible at `10.0.2.2` by default)
> - NFS locking with nix store: mount with `local_lock=flock` or `local_lock=all`
> - NFS adds network protocol overhead but avoids the 9p protocol limitations
> 
> NFS would be a reasonable alternative if 9p proves problematic, but for a read-only nix store mount, 9p with your current config should be comparable or better (no TCP/IP stack overhead).
> 
> ### SSHFS (not recommended)
> 
> SSHFS adds FUSE + SSH encryption overhead. For a nix store with thousands of small files, the per-operation SSH round-trip latency would be painful. Not worth it when you already have 9p working.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Recommendations for your setup
> 
> 1. **Stick with 9p.** Your current configuration is already near-optimal for QEMU on macOS. virtiofs is simply not available through QEMU on this platform.
> 
> 2. **Verify effective msize.** SSH into the VM and check `cat /proc/mounts | grep 9p` to confirm the kernel isn't silently capping your 100MB msize.
> 
> 3. **If you need virtiofs badly enough**, the path is switching from QEMU to **vfkit** + Apple Virtualization.framework, following the pattern of [nixos-vm-on-macos](https://github.com/phaer/nixos-vm-on-macos). This is a significant rewrite of your VM launch infrastructure. The win would be Apple's native virtiofs for store sharing with writable overlay. The cost is losing your current QEMU invocation, `-display cocoa`, and potentially some device compatibility.
> 
> 4. **Consider the disk image approach** for the nix store specifically. Instead of sharing `/nix/store` live, you could bake the required store paths into the disk image at build time (your `make-disk-image.nix` already exists). This eliminates the filesystem sharing bottleneck entirely for the store, at the cost of rebuilding the image when store contents change. Your project directory mount via 9p would remain for fast iteration.
> 
> Sources:
> - [virtiofsd macOS support request (GitLab #169)](https://gitlab.com/virtio-fs/virtiofsd/-/issues/169)
> - [nixos-vm-on-macos (vfkit + virtiofs)](https://github.com/phaer/nixos-vm-on-macos)
> - [Apple VZVirtioFileSystemDeviceConfiguration docs](https://developer.apple.com/documentation/virtualization/vzvirtiofilesystemdeviceconfiguration)
> - [NixOS VM on macOS issue (nixpkgs #108984)](https://github.com/NixOS/nixpkgs/issues/108984)
> - [QEMU 9p Darwin patches (v8)](https://patchew.org/QEMU/20220220165056.72289-1-wwcohen@gmail.com/20220220165056.72289-12-wwcohen@gmail.com/)
> - [virtiofs vs 9p performance numbers](https://www.mail-archive.com/virtio-fs@redhat.com/msg02371.html)
> - [UTM sharing documentation](https://docs.getutm.app/settings-qemu/sharing/)
> - [microvm.nix](https://github.com/microvm-nix/microvm.nix)
> - [Linux 6.1 9p optimization (Phoronix)](https://www.phoronix.com/news/Linux-6.1-Faster-9P)
> - [Mounting nix-store via NFS](https://xyno.space/posts/nix-store-nfs/)
> - [9p msize discussion (qemu-devel)](https://www.mail-archive.com/virtio-fs@redhat.com/msg02743.html)</result>
