### `2026-03-29T10:46:44.188Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a47dc11117d0d7870</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_017UUhEvJefHK8W1dSMHSEWi</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/1cdb03a1-c64b-4ec9-b3bd-e512fbbdf449/tasks/a47dc11117d0d7870.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Audit pane API names vs BeAPI" completed</summary>
> <result>OK, `PaneGeometry` is width/height/cols/rows — no position. It *is* just size. So `resized` is actually more accurate than `frame_resized` for what's being communicated. **Keep `resized`.** The Be method was `FrameResized` because `BRect Frame()` included position. Your geometry doesn't include position, so `resized` is more honest.
> 
> ### `focused(proxy)` / `blurred(proxy)` (Be: `BWindow::WindowActivated(bool)`)
> 
> **Consider renaming to `activated(proxy)` / `deactivated(proxy)`.** Be used a single method with a boolean: `WindowActivated(bool focus)`. Your split into two methods is better API design (no boolean parameter that requires reading docs to understand polarity). The question is the *word*.
> 
> "Focus" and "blur" are web terminology (DOM focus/blur events). "Activated" and "deactivated" are Be/native desktop terminology. In a Wayland context, `wl_keyboard::enter`/`leave` and `wl_surface::enter`/`leave` are the underlying primitives — neither "focus" nor "activation" exactly.
> 
> For a project trying to carry Be's spirit: `activated` / `deactivated` would be more natural. But `focused` / `blurred` is universally understood by anyone who's done UI programming in the last 20 years. This is taste. **Consider** — I'd lean toward `activated`/`deactivated` because this project is deliberately not a web project, and the Be terminology was more precise (a window can be activated without having keyboard focus in some window managers).
> 
> ### `key(proxy, event)` / `mouse(proxy, event)` (Be: `KeyDown`/`KeyUp`/`MouseDown`/`MouseUp`/`MouseMoved`)
> 
> **Keep.** Be split these into 5+ methods because C++ didn't have tagged unions. You have `KeyEvent` and `MouseEvent` which carry the state (pressed/released/moved). The collapsed form is better. The names are short and unambiguous.
> 
> ### `close_requested(proxy)` (Be: `BWindow::QuitRequested()`)
> 
> **Rename to `quit_requested`.** This is a case where the Be name is *clearly* better and the rename costs nothing. `QuitRequested` is the exact semantics: "the system is asking if it's OK to quit." `close_requested` implies the visual act of clicking a close button, which is one of several ways you might arrive at `QuitRequested`. In Be, `QuitRequested` was also called when the app was shutting down, not just when the user clicked close. Your handler should use the broader term because the same method will be called in both scenarios.
> 
> Every Be developer will look for `quit_requested`. Every non-Be developer will understand it perfectly. There's no cost to using the Be name.
> 
> ### `command_activated(proxy)` / `command_dismissed(proxy)` / `command_executed(proxy, cmd, args)` (no Be equivalent)
> 
> **Keep.** These are new to pane's command surface concept. No Be analogue.
> 
> ### `completion_request(proxy, token, input)` (no Be equivalent)
> 
> **Keep.** New concept.
> 
> ### `disconnected(proxy)` (no Be equivalent)
> 
> **Keep.** New concept — Be apps didn't get disconnected from app_server (and when they did, the whole system was going down anyway).
> 
> ### `unhandled(proxy)` (Be: `BHandler::MessageReceived(msg)` default case)
> 
> **Rename to `message_received`.** Here's why. `MessageReceived` was the *primary* dispatch method in Be — the one you put your application logic in. Your `unhandled` is the catch-all for events that don't match any specific handler method. But the *name* "unhandled" implies these are events you *failed* to handle, when in reality it's a perfectly valid dispatch target. `MessageReceived` said "a message arrived, here it is" — neutral about whether you expected it.
> 
> However, your architecture is different. In Be, `MessageReceived` was the *only* dispatch method and you `switch`-ed on `message->what`. Your `Handler` has specific methods for each event type, and `unhandled` is truly the "nothing else matched" case. In that context, `unhandled` is actually more accurate to the *role* it plays.
> 
> **Consider.** If you plan to add custom message types that go through `unhandled`, rename to `message_received`. If `unhandled` is always genuinely the fallback for events the developer didn't override, **Keep**.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Methods on App
> 
> ### `connect(sig)` (Be: `BApplication(sig)` constructor)
> 
> **Keep.** Constructors in Rust are named functions, not `new()` when they do I/O. `connect` tells you this talks to the compositor. Better than `new` for something that can fail.
> 
> ### `create_pane(tag)` (Be: `new BWindow(...)`)
> 
> **Keep.** In Be, windows were created with `new` and registered themselves. In pane, the compositor is the authority. `create_pane` correctly communicates that this is a request to the compositor, not a local allocation.
> 
> ### `wait()` (Be: `BApplication::Run()`)
> 
> **Rename to `run`.** `BApplication::Run()` blocked the main thread until the app was done. Your `wait()` does the same. The problem with `wait` is that it sounds passive — "wait for something to happen." `Run` says "this is the main loop, enter it." Every GUI framework uses `run` for this: `gtk::main()`, `QApplication::exec()`, `NSApp.run()`, `BApplication::Run()`. Developers will expect `app.run()`.
> 
> The distinction matters: `wait` suggests you could be doing other things on the main thread. `run` says "this thread is now the app's main loop." Even if the implementation is just a condvar wait, the *semantics* are "run the application."
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Methods on Pane
> 
> ### `run(closure)` / `run_with(handler)` (Be: `BWindow::Show()` + message loop)
> 
> **Keep.** The Be lifecycle was very different (construct window, Show() makes it visible, Run() was inherited from BLooper). Your `run` consumes the Pane and enters the event loop. The name is correct for what it does.
> 
> ### `proxy()` (no Be equivalent)
> 
> **Consider renaming to `messenger()`.** If you rename `PaneHandle` to `Messenger`, this method should be `messenger()` to match. In Be you'd construct `BMessenger(myWindow)` explicitly. This convenience method creates one for you. If you keep `PaneHandle`, keep `proxy()`. If you rename to `Messenger`, rename to `messenger()`.
> 
> ### `add_filter(filter)` (Be: `BLooper::AddCommonFilter`)
> 
> **Keep.** The distinction between `AddFilter` (on BHandler, per-handler) and `AddCommonFilter` (on BLooper, applies to all handlers) existed because BLooper could have multiple BHandlers. Your Pane has one handler, so there's only one level. `add_filter` is correct and simpler.
> 
> ### `id()` (Be: internal `fServerToken`)
> 
> **Keep.** Exposing the ID is fine. Be kept it internal because they had different abstraction boundaries.
> 
> ### `geometry()` (Be: `BWindow::Frame()` / `BWindow::Bounds()`)
> 
> **Keep.** Your geometry struct is richer than BRect (it has cols/rows). The name reflects the different type.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Summary: Recommended Renames
> 
> | Current | Proposed | Rationale |
> |---------|----------|-----------|
> | `PaneHandle` | `Messenger` | Matches Be name, encodes role not relationship, universally understood |
> | `Pane::proxy()` | `Pane::messenger()` | Follows from above |
> | `App::wait()` | `App::run()` | Every GUI framework convention, matches `BApplication::Run()` |
> | `Handler::close_requested()` | `Handler::quit_requested()` | Exact Be semantics, broader than "close", zero cost |
> 
> ## Recommended Considerations (argue either way)
> 
> | Current | Alternative | Notes |
> |---------|------------|-------|
> | `Handler::focused()`/`blurred()` | `activated()`/`deactivated()` | Be terminology vs web terminology; I lean Be |
> | `PaneHandle::send_message()` | `post_message()` | Depends on whether cross-pane messaging is planned |
> | `Handler::unhandled()` | `message_received()` | Depends on whether custom messages will route here |
> | `FilterAction` | `FilterVerdict` | Minor polish; avoids "action" implying side-effect |
> 
> ## Everything Else: Keep
> 
> `App`, `Pane`, `PaneMessage`, `Handler`, `Filter`, `FilterChain`, `TimerToken`, `ExitReason`, `Tag`, `CommandBuilder`, `cmd()`, error types, `connect()`, `create_pane()`, `run()`/`run_with()`, `add_filter()`, `set_title()`, `set_vocabulary()`, `set_content()`, `send_delayed()`, `send_periodic()`, `id()`, `geometry()`, `ready()`, `resized()`, `key()`, `mouse()`, `command_*()`, `disconnected()`, all routing/scripting types.</result>

> ---
> 
> # BeOS Scripting → Pane Filesystem: Architectural Validation
> 
> ## Source Material
> 
> All BeOS scripting behavior verified from Haiku source:
> - `/Users/lane/src/haiku/src/bin/hey.cpp` — the hey tool
> - `/Users/lane/src/haiku/src/kits/app/Application.cpp` — BApplication::ResolveSpecifier, ScriptReceived
> - `/Users/lane/src/haiku/src/kits/interface/Window.cpp` — BWindow property table, ResolveSpecifier, MessageReceived
> - `/Users/lane/src/haiku/src/kits/interface/View.cpp` — BView property table, ResolveSpecifier, MessageReceived
> - `/Users/lane/src/haiku/src/kits/interface/Menu.cpp` — BMenu property table, _ScriptReceived, _ItemScriptReceived
> - `/Users/lane/src/haiku/src/kits/tracker/TrackerScripting.cpp` — Tracker scripting (create/delete/execute Folder, delete Trash)
> 
> Pane filesystem model from: `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/openspec/specs/pane-fs/spec.md`
> 
> ---
> 
> ## The 10 Scenarios
> 
> ### A. Get a Window's Title
> 
> **BeOS:**
> ```
> hey AppName get Title of Window 0
> ```
> 
> Mechanics: hey builds `BMessage(B_GET_PROPERTY)`, adds specifier chain `[{property:"Title", specifier:DIRECT}, {property:"Window", specifier:INDEX, index:0}]`. Sends to BApplication. BApplication::ResolveSpecifier sees "Window" + INDEX, looks up `WindowAt(0)`, pops specifier, forwards the remaining message to that BWindow via `BMessenger(window).SendMessage(message)`. BWindow::ResolveSpecifier sees "Title" + DIRECT, returns `this`. BWindow::MessageReceived case 6 handles B_GET_PROPERTY for Title: replies with `replyMsg.AddString("result", Title())`.
> 
> BMessage fields used: `what=B_GET_PROPERTY`, specifier stack with property names and indices, reply has `"result"` (B_STRING_TYPE).
> 
> **Pane equivalent:**
> ```sh
> cat /pane/1/tag
> ```
> Or for the title attribute specifically:
> ```sh
> cat /pane/1/attrs/title
> ```
> 
> **Assessment: Natural.** This is the filesystem model's strongest case. One syscall (open+read+close via FUSE), no protocol to understand. The 15-30us FUSE overhead is invisible for scripting. The pane-fs spec defines `tag` as a direct read of the tag line content.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### B. Set a Window's Title
> 
> **BeOS:**
> ```
> hey AppName set Title of Window 0 to "New Title"
> ```
> 
> Same specifier chain as above. hey builds `BMessage(B_SET_PROPERTY)`, adds the specifier chain, adds `"data"` field with `"New Title"` (B_STRING_TYPE). BWindow::MessageReceived case 6 handles B_SET_PROPERTY: calls `SetTitle(newTitle)`.
> 
> **Pane equivalent:**
> ```sh
> echo "New Title" > /pane/1/attrs/title
> ```
> Or equivalently for the tag line:
> ```sh
> echo "New Title" > /pane/1/tag
> ```
> 
> **Assessment: Natural.** FUSE write translates to a pane protocol message. The pane-fs spec explicitly defines attribute writes: "WHEN `echo 'new title' > /pane/1/attrs/title` is executed, THEN the pane's title attribute SHALL be updated."
> 
> One subtlety: BMessage SET was atomic in the sense that the message was processed in the target looper's thread (single-threaded dispatch within the window). A FUSE write is similarly serialized through the FUSE daemon, which dispatches to the compositor. Atomicity is preserved at the pane level because the compositor is the single authority for pane state.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### C. Count Windows
> 
> **BeOS:**
> ```
> hey AppName count Window
> ```
> 
> BMessage(B_COUNT_PROPERTIES) with specifier `{property:"Window", specifier:DIRECT}`. BApplication::ScriptReceived handles this case directly — it's a property of the Application itself, not forwarded. Returns `CountWindows()` as an int32 in `"result"`.
> 
> **Pane equivalent:**
> ```sh
> ls /pane/ | wc -l
> ```
> Or from the index:
> ```sh
> cat /pane/index | wc -l
> ```
> Or, if you want composited pane IDs for a specific application (by signature):
> ```sh
> ls /pane/ | while read id; do
>   [ "$(cat /pane/$id/attrs/signature)" = "com.example.app" ] && echo "$id"
> done | wc -l
> ```
> 
> **Assessment: Natural with a caveat.** The simple "count all panes" case maps trivially to `ls /pane/`. But B_COUNT_PROPERTIES was per-application — "how many windows does *this* application own?" The filesystem model exposes the global namespace. Filtering by application signature requires a loop.
> 
> This is not a regression in capability — it's actually more powerful. You can count panes by any predicate (signature, type, workspace, etc.) using standard unix tools. The BeOS model only supported counting the object types that each handler hardcoded in its property table. But the simple case of "count my own windows" goes from one message to a pipeline.
> 
> Possible refinement: a per-app symlink directory at `/pane/by-sig/com.example.app/` would recover the one-step case. The query system (pane-store) could handle this: `pane-query 'signature == "com.example.app"'` would be the direct analogue.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### D. Get a View's Frame
> 
> **BeOS:**
> ```
> hey AppName get Frame of View "statusbar" of Window 0
> ```
> 
> Three-level specifier chain: `[{Frame, DIRECT}, {View, NAME:"statusbar"}, {Window, INDEX:0}]`. BApplication resolves Window → forwards to BWindow. BWindow::ResolveSpecifier sees "View" → returns `fTopView` (does NOT pop the specifier — the View property is a traversal into the view hierarchy). BView::ResolveSpecifier sees "View" + NAME → calls `FindView("statusbar")` → pops specifier → returns the child view. BView::MessageReceived sees "Frame" + B_GET_PROPERTY → replies with `Frame()` as B_RECT_TYPE.
> 
> The reply contains a BRect (left, top, right, bottom floats).
> 
> **Pane equivalent:**
> 
> This is where the model diverges meaningfully. Pane doesn't have a view hierarchy in the BeOS sense — it has panes, which are compositor-level objects. The "statusbar" in BeOS was a BView inside a BWindow. In pane, a status bar would either be:
> 
> 1. A separate child pane (a component pane composed within a parent), exposed at `/pane/<child_id>/attrs/frame`
> 2. Part of the pane's own content, not individually addressable from outside
> 
> If it's a separate pane:
> ```sh
> cat /pane/42/attrs/frame    # returns geometry JSON or rect string
> ```
> 
> If it's internal to the pane's content, there's no filesystem path for it. The pane's `body` might expose it, but the internal structure of a pane's rendering is opaque to the compositor and to pane-fs.
> 
> **Assessment: Depends on composition model.** For pane-level objects (which are the unit of composition), this works naturally. For internal UI widgets within a pane, it doesn't work at all — and this is by design.
> 
> BeOS's view scripting was powerful but created a deep coupling: every application's internal widget tree was exposed as a scriptable object hierarchy. This meant scripts could break when an application rearranged its UI. Pane deliberately stops at the pane boundary. If a pane wants to expose internal structure, it does so through its own properties/attributes — the developer explicitly decides what's scriptable.
> 
> This is a conscious loss of granularity in exchange for a more stable scripting contract. I think it's the right call. The deep view traversal was BeOS's most fragile scripting feature.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### E. Enumerate Menu Items
> 
> **BeOS:**
> ```
> hey AppName get Label of MenuItem 0 of Menu "File" of Window 0
> ```
> 
> Four-level specifier chain. BApplication → BWindow → BWindow::ResolveSpecifier returns `fKeyMenuBar` for "MenuBar" (or the Menu resolves "Menu" by name). BMenu::_ScriptReceived resolves "MenuItem" by index, then _ItemScriptReceived handles "Label" GET → returns item's label string.
> 
> **Pane equivalent:**
> 
> Pane doesn't have menus. It has the command surface (tag line). Commands are exposed through `CommandVocabulary`:
> 
> ```sh
> # List available commands for a pane
> cat /pane/1/attrs/vocabulary    # returns JSON of command groups
> ```
> 
> Or if commands are exposed as individual files:
> ```sh
> ls /pane/1/commands/
> cat /pane/1/commands/save
> ```
> 
> To enumerate all available commands (the closest analogue to enumerating menu items):
> ```sh
> cat /pane/1/attrs/vocabulary | jq '.groups[].commands[].name'
> ```
> 
> **Assessment: Conceptual remapping, not direct mapping.** The menu hierarchy (Menu "File" → MenuItem "Save") maps to the command vocabulary (CommandGroup "file" → Command "save"). The filesystem representation is flatter (one vocabulary document vs. a tree of menus/items), but the information is the same and actually easier to process — it's structured data (JSON or similar) rather than requiring individual round-trips per menu item.
> 
> This is one place pane actually improves on the BeOS model. Getting all menu items in BeOS required N+1 messages (one to count, N to get each label). Reading the vocabulary is one read.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### F. Execute a Menu Item
> 
> **BeOS:**
> ```
> hey AppName do MenuItem "Save" of Menu "File" of Window 0
> ```
> 
> The specifier chain resolves to a specific BMenuItem. BMenu::_ScriptReceived case 12 (B_EXECUTE_PROPERTY) calls `item->Invoke()`, which triggers the menu item's action message. The key insight: `Invoke()` synthesizes and sends the BMessage that the menu item would normally send when clicked by the user. It's indistinguishable from a user click.
> 
> **Pane equivalent:**
> ```sh
> echo "save" > /pane/1/ctl
> ```
> 
> Or through the command surface protocol:
> ```sh
> # The ctl file accepts line commands
> echo "file:save" > /pane/1/ctl
> ```
> 
> **Assessment: Natural.** The `ctl` file in the pane-fs spec is the command interface: "Line commands for control files (ctl)." Writing a command to `ctl` is the pane equivalent of B_EXECUTE_PROPERTY. The compositor delivers it as a `CommandExecuted` message to the pane.
> 
> There's a meaningful design question here: does `ctl` need structured command syntax (namespacing, argument passing), or is freeform text sufficient? BeOS's menu invocation was fully structured (resolved to a specific BMenuItem by the specifier chain). A `ctl` interface needs at minimum a command name and optional arguments. The spec doesn't define the syntax yet.
> 
> Suggestion: `ctl` accepts the same command strings the command surface accepts. If a pane registers a vocabulary with `save` as a command, `echo "save" > /pane/1/ctl` invokes it. Arguments follow the command: `echo "save /tmp/out.txt" > /pane/1/ctl`. This maps 1:1 to `CommandExecuted { command: "save", args: "/tmp/out.txt" }`.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### G. Get/Set IsMinimized
> 
> **BeOS:**
> ```
> hey AppName get Minimize of Window 0
> hey AppName set Minimize of Window 0 to bool(true)
> ```
> 
> BWindow property table entry: `"Minimize", { B_GET_PROPERTY, B_SET_PROPERTY }, { B_DIRECT_SPECIFIER }, B_BOOL_TYPE`. The MessageReceived handler gets/sets the minimize state.
> 
> **Pane equivalent:**
> ```sh
> cat /pane/1/attrs/minimized        # returns "true" or "false"
> echo "true" > /pane/1/attrs/minimized
> ```
> 
> **Assessment: Natural.** Boolean state attributes are the simplest case. One file per attribute, text representation. The `attrs/` directory is designed exactly for this.
> 
> Type safety note: BMessage carried `B_BOOL_TYPE` — the recipient knew it was getting a bool. The filesystem representation is text. Writing "true"/"false" is unambiguous for booleans, but for numeric types, the consumer loses the type tag. The pane-fs implementation needs to know the type of each attribute (from the pane's declared properties) to validate writes. This is analogous to how BPropertyInfo declared the expected type for each property — the validation happens at the receiving end.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### H. Create a New Object
> 
> **BeOS:**
> ```
> hey Tracker create Folder to '/boot/home/Desktop/hello'
> hey AppName create MenuItem 0 of Menu "File" of Window 0 with data="New Item" and what=12345
> ```
> 
> Two real examples from the source:
> 
> 1. **Tracker Folder creation** (TrackerScripting.cpp): `B_CREATE_PROPERTY` with property "Folder", value is a path. TTracker::CreateProperty creates the directory.
> 
> 2. **Menu item creation** (Menu.cpp case 10): `B_CREATE_PROPERTY` with property "MenuItem", specifier gives the insertion position. Extra fields: `data` (label, B_STRING_TYPE), `what` (command int32), `be:invoke_message` (optional BMessage), `be:target` (optional BMessenger). The handler creates a new BMenuItem with the specified label and command, and inserts it at the specified position.
> 
> **Pane equivalent:**
> 
> For Tracker folder creation:
> ```sh
> mkdir /path/to/new/folder
> ```
> This is not a pane operation at all — it's a regular filesystem operation. The Tracker was unique in that it exposed filesystem operations through its scripting protocol. Pane doesn't need to — the real filesystem is always available.
> 
> For command/menu creation (adding a command to a pane's vocabulary):
> ```sh
> # Structured write to add a command
> echo '{"name":"new-item","action":"message","what":12345}' > /pane/1/ctl
> ```
> Or by updating the vocabulary:
> ```sh
> # Read current vocabulary, modify it, write it back
> cat /pane/1/attrs/vocabulary > /tmp/vocab.json
> # ... modify with jq ...
> jq '.groups[0].commands += [{"name":"new-item"}]' /tmp/vocab.json > /pane/1/attrs/vocabulary
> ```
> 
> **Assessment: Awkward for structured creation.** B_CREATE_PROPERTY allowed creating complex objects with multiple named fields in a single message (data, what, be:invoke_message, be:target). The filesystem model requires either:
> 
> 1. A structured format in the write payload (JSON), which means the `ctl` or attribute file needs to parse structured input.
> 2. Multiple writes (set each field separately), which loses atomicity.
> 
> This is the first real pressure point. The `with name=value and name=value` syntax in hey mapped naturally to BMessage's dynamic key-value fields. The filesystem doesn't have a natural equivalent for "create an object with these five properties, atomically."
> 
> The `ctl` file is the closest match — it can accept structured commands. But the command format needs to support structured payloads.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### I. Delete an Object
> 
> **BeOS:**
> ```
> hey Tracker delete Trash
> hey AppName delete MenuItem "Save" of Menu "File" of Window 0
> ```
> 
> 1. **Tracker delete Trash** (TrackerScripting.cpp): `B_DELETE_PROPERTY` with property "Trash". Empties the trash.
> 
> 2. **Menu item deletion** (Menu.cpp case 11): `B_DELETE_PROPERTY` with property "MenuItem", resolved by name or index. Calls `RemoveItem()`.
> 
> **Pane equivalent:**
> 
> For Tracker Trash:
> ```sh
> rm -rf /trash/*    # or whatever pane's trash abstraction is
> ```
> 
> For removing a command from a vocabulary:
> ```sh
> echo "remove-command save" > /pane/1/ctl
> ```
> 
> Or for removing a child pane:
> ```sh
> echo "close" > /pane/42/ctl    # close a pane
> ```
> 
> **Assessment: Natural for pane-level operations.** Closing a pane or removing a command is a clean mapping to `ctl` writes. The awkwardness from scenario H (structured creation) doesn't apply to deletion — you just need to identify what to delete.
> 
> The filesystem model also offers something BeOS didn't: you could potentially use `rm` to remove a pane:
> ```sh
> rm -r /pane/42/    # could be equivalent to close
> ```
> Though this is a design choice — should FUSE `unlink` on a pane directory close it? I'd say no. The `ctl` interface is the right command channel. FUSE `unlink` semantics don't map cleanly to "close a pane" (which involves teardown, save prompts, etc.).
> 
> ---
> 
> ### J. Complex Traversal: 3+ Level Deep Specifier Chain
> 
> **BeOS:**
> ```
> hey AppName get Label of MenuItem 0 of Menu "Edit" of MenuBar of Window "Document"
> ```
> 
> Five-level chain: Label → MenuItem[0] → Menu["Edit"] → MenuBar → Window["Document"]. Each level resolves in a different handler:
> 
> 1. BApplication resolves Window["Document"] → BWindow by name
> 2. BWindow resolves MenuBar → fKeyMenuBar (BMenuBar)
> 3. BMenuBar (inherits BMenu) resolves Menu["Edit"] → finds submenu by name
> 4. The "Edit" submenu resolves MenuItem[0] → finds item by index
> 5. The item handler returns the Label property
> 
> Each resolution pops one specifier and either returns `this` (I'll handle the final property) or returns a child handler (forward the message deeper).
> 
> **Pane equivalent:**
> 
> If panes are composed:
> ```sh
> # Nested composition: split contains editor, which has a child pane
> cat /pane/1/2/attrs/label    # where 1 is the parent, 2 is the child
> ```
> 
> More realistically, since pane IDs are flat:
> ```sh
> # Find the pane you want by querying attributes
> id=$(ls /pane/ | while read p; do
>   sig=$(cat /pane/$p/attrs/signature 2>/dev/null)
>   title=$(cat /pane/$p/attrs/title 2>/dev/null)
>   [ "$sig" = "com.example.editor" ] && [ "$title" = "Document" ] && echo $p
> done)
> # Then read its property
> cat /pane/$id/attrs/some-property
> ```
> 
> Or with pane-store queries:
> ```sh
> pane-query 'signature == "com.example.editor" && title == "Document"'
> ```
> 
> **Assessment: Fundamentally different model — not worse, different.** BeOS's deep traversal was a tree walk through the object hierarchy. Pane's model is a flat namespace with query-based lookup. You don't navigate App → Window → MenuBar → Menu → MenuItem. You query for the pane you want and then read its properties.
> 
> The deep traversal model has a fixed cost proportional to tree depth (one ResolveSpecifier per level). The query model has a cost proportional to the total number of panes (or O(1) if indexed). For small applications, BeOS wins on simplicity. For the system as a whole, the query model scales better.
> 
> But here's the critical difference: **BeOS's deep traversal worked across all applications without cooperation.** Any application's internal structure was scriptable by default because BWindow, BView, and BMenu all implemented ResolveSpecifier. Pane's model requires the pane to explicitly expose properties as attributes. If a pane doesn't expose a "label" attribute, there's nothing to read.
> 
> This is both the cost and the benefit of the filesystem model. Cost: implicit scriptability is gone. Benefit: the scripting contract is explicit and stable.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Step 4: Pressure Point Analysis
> 
> ### Round-trips
> 
> | Scenario | BeOS messages | Pane FUSE ops | Assessment |
> |----------|--------------|---------------|------------|
> | A. Get title | 1 | 1 | Equal |
> | B. Set title | 1 | 1 | Equal |
> | C. Count windows | 1 | 1 (readdir) | Equal |
> | C'. Count app windows | 1 | N+1 (readdir + N reads) | Pane loses |
> | D. Get view frame | 1 | 1 (if separate pane) | Equal |
> | E. Enumerate menu items | N+1 | 1 (read vocabulary) | **Pane wins** |
> | F. Execute menu item | 1 | 1 (write to ctl) | Equal |
> | G. Get/set bool | 1 | 1 | Equal |
> | H. Create structured object | 1 | 1 (if ctl accepts JSON) | Equal (with format) |
> | I. Delete object | 1 | 1 | Equal |
> | J. Deep traversal | 1 (resolved in-process) | 2+ (query + read) | Pane loses |
> 
> For batching: BeOS could batch multiple operations by sending multiple BMessages. The filesystem model can batch with shell pipelines, but each operation is a separate FUSE round-trip. A script that reads 10 attributes of one pane makes 10 FUSE calls vs. potentially 1 batched BMessage.
> 
> **Verdict on round-trips:** For individual operations, parity. For bulk queries, the filesystem model is chatty. If pane needs a "read all attributes" bulk operation, it should be the `attrs/` directory itself — `cat /pane/1/attrs/*` in a single read, or a special file like `/pane/1/props` that returns all properties as JSON.
> 
> ### Atomicity
> 
> BMessage operations were atomic within the target looper's lock. The looper processed one message at a time. Between receiving the message and sending the reply, no other message could modify the same handler's state.
> 
> The filesystem model has the same atomicity property for individual operations: a FUSE read or write to a single attribute is atomic at the pane level (the compositor serializes updates). But multi-attribute operations are not atomic — reading `title` then `frame` could see inconsistent state if another writer modifies between reads.
> 
> BeOS had the same limitation for multi-message sequences, but B_SET_PROPERTY with `with` could set multiple fields in one message. The `ctl` file could serve this role if it accepts structured payloads.
> 
> **Verdict on atomicity:** No worse than BeOS for single operations. For multi-property atomic updates, pane needs either (a) a structured `ctl` command that sets multiple attrs, or (b) a transaction model (which is overkill).
> 
> ### Type Safety
> 
> BMessage had typed fields: `B_STRING_TYPE`, `B_INT32_TYPE`, `B_RECT_TYPE`, `B_BOOL_TYPE`. The property_info table declared expected types. Type mismatches were caught at the receiving end.
> 
> The filesystem is text. `cat /pane/1/attrs/frame` returns a string representation. `echo "not a rect" > /pane/1/attrs/frame` would be caught by the pane-fs daemon (which knows the attribute's type from the pane's property declarations), but the error path is a write error rather than a typed protocol error.
> 
> **Verdict on type safety:** The filesystem model is weakly typed at the interface. This is fine for scripting (scripts work with text anyway). For programmatic access from native code, the kit API provides type safety. The three-tier model (in-process → protocol → filesystem) means type safety is available when you want it and text convenience when you want that instead.
> 
> ### Gains
> 
> The filesystem model gains several things BMessage scripting never had:
> 
> 1. **Tool composability.** `grep`, `jq`, `awk`, `watch`, `diff`, `tail -f` all work out of the box. `watch -n1 cat /pane/1/attrs/title` monitors title changes. BeOS had no equivalent — you'd need to write a BMessageRunner and a custom handler.
> 
> 2. **No custom protocol.** Any language, any tool. No `hey` command needed. No BMessenger required. `curl` to a pane's attributes over the network if you expose the FUSE mount.
> 
> 3. **Event streaming.** `tail -f /pane/1/event` gives you a live event stream as JSONL. BeOS required writing a BLooper to receive notifications. This is a massive win for debugging and monitoring.
> 
> 4. **Discoverability.** `ls /pane/1/attrs/` lists all attributes. `ls /pane/` lists all panes. BeOS had `GetSupportedSuites` but it required sending a message and parsing the reply — you couldn't just `ls` an application's scriptable properties.
> 
> 5. **Persistence and logging.** You can snapshot pane state with `cp -r /pane/1/ /tmp/snapshot/`. You can log all events with `tail -f /pane/1/event >> /var/log/pane-events.log`. No custom infrastructure needed.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Step 5: Verdict
> 
> ### Scoreboard
> 
> | Scenario | Filesystem fit |
> |----------|---------------|
> | A. Get title | Clean |
> | B. Set title | Clean |
> | C. Count windows | Clean (global), Awkward (per-app) |
> | D. Get view frame | Clean (pane-level), N/A (widget-level) |
> | E. Enumerate commands | Clean (better than BeOS) |
> | F. Execute command | Clean |
> | G. Get/set bool attr | Clean |
> | H. Create structured object | Workable (needs ctl JSON format) |
> | I. Delete object | Clean |
> | J. Deep traversal | Different model (query vs. tree walk) |
> 
> **7 of 10 work cleanly.** 1 works better than BeOS (E). 2 require design attention (C' per-app count, H structured creation). 1 is a fundamentally different model (J deep traversal).
> 
> ### Friction Patterns
> 
> The friction comes from exactly two patterns:
> 
> 1. **Multi-property atomic operations.** Creating or updating an object with multiple fields simultaneously. The `ctl` file handles this if it accepts structured payloads (JSON lines). This is the right solution — don't try to make multi-file writes atomic, make the ctl interface structured.
> 
> 2. **Implicit hierarchical traversal.** BeOS scripts could traverse into any application's internal object hierarchy without the application explicitly exposing that hierarchy. Pane stops at the pane boundary. If a pane wants internal structure to be scriptable, it exposes named attributes. This is a deliberate choice, not a limitation.
> 
> ### Does Pane Need `Message::Custom(BTreeMap<String, AttrValue>)`?
> 
> **No.** The filesystem model is sufficient. Here's why:
> 
> The temptation to add a custom dynamic message type comes from the BeOS pattern of BMessage as a universal dynamic container. But BMessage served two roles that pane separates:
> 
> 1. **Protocol messages** (CreatePane, SetTitle, Focus, etc.) — these are the session-typed Message enum. They stay closed and typed. Adding `Custom(BTreeMap)` would undermine the type safety that session types provide.
> 
> 2. **Dynamic state access** (get Title, set Frame, count Windows) — this is the scripting/automation surface. In pane, this lives in the filesystem.
> 
> The `ctl` file is the right escape hatch for structured commands. If a script needs to create a complex object with multiple fields, it writes a structured command to `ctl`. The pane's handler parses it. No dynamic message fields pollute the protocol.
> 
> The one thing I'd add to the spec: **the `attrs/` directory should support a bulk-read shorthand.** Either a special file like `/pane/1/attrs.json` that returns all attributes as a JSON object, or simply guaranteeing that `readdir` + `read` of the entire `attrs/` directory is efficient enough that it doesn't matter. This addresses the multi-round-trip concern for bulk reads.
> 
> ### Three Specific Recommendations
> 
> 1. **Define ctl syntax now.** The `ctl` file needs a documented command format that supports arguments and structured payloads. Suggestion: `COMMAND [ARGS...]` for simple commands, `COMMAND <<JSON>>` for structured payloads. This is the `hey AppName do ...` equivalent.
> 
> 2. **Add per-signature index.** `/pane/by-sig/com.example.app/` as symlinks to `/pane/<id>/`. This recovers the "count my application's windows" use case without requiring a full pane-store query. Alternatively, make pane-store's query interface available as a filesystem path: `/pane/query/signature=com.example.app`.
> 
> 3. **Document the pane boundary as a design principle.** The fact that pane doesn't expose internal widget hierarchies is a feature, not a bug. But it should be an explicit design principle so that nobody tries to add deep view traversal later. The scripting contract is: a pane exposes the attributes it chooses to expose. That's the level of abstraction that works for stable automation.
> 
> ### Bottom Line
> 
> The filesystem model is not just adequate — it's better than BMessage scripting for the things that matter in a unix environment. The BeOS scripting protocol was elegant but bespoke: you needed `hey` or a BMessenger to use it. The pane filesystem works with every tool unix has accumulated over fifty years. That composability advantage is worth the minor friction in the two patterns identified above.
> 
> The bet holds.</result>
